// 5. Cover the query — project only fields in the index
db.orders.createIndex({ status: 1, placed_at: -1, total_cents: 1, user_id: 1 });
db.orders.find(
  { status: "paid", placed_at: { $gte: ISODate("2026-04-01") } },
  { _id: 0, placed_at: 1, total_cents: 1, user_id: 1 }
).sort({ placed_at: -1 }).limit(50);
//    explain() shows totalDocsExamined: 0 — index alone answers the query

// 6. Find unused indexes — they slow writes for nothing
db.orders.aggregate([{ $indexStats: {} }])
  .forEach(s => print(s.name, s.accesses.ops));
db.orders.dropIndex("legacy_idx_unused");

// 7. Aggregation-specific tuning
db.orders.explain("executionStats").aggregate([
  { $match: { status: "paid" } },     // FIRST — uses index
  { $project: { user_id: 1, total_cents: 1 } },
  { $group: { _id: "$user_id", spend: { $sum: "$total_cents" } } }
]);

// 8. Pagination — replace skip with cursor for deep pages
db.orders.find({ status: "paid", _id: { $gt: lastSeen } })
         .sort({ _id: 1 })
         .limit(50);
// vs. .skip(50000).limit(50)  — quadratic cost

Trade-offs / common bottlenecks:

SymptomLikely causeFix
COLLSCAN in explainNo suitable indexBuild a compound index per ESR
SORT stage above IXSCANIndex doesn’t match sort directionAdd or reorder fields in the index
docsExamined >> nReturnedFilter checks fields not in indexCover the query, or extend the index
Slow at depth (skip/limit)Skip is O(N)Cursor pagination on indexed key
Aggregation memory error$group/$sort exceeds 100MBAdd { allowDiskUse: true }; pre-filter with $match
Slow under load onlyWorking set > RAMAdd RAM, prune indexes, archive cold data

Production polish: Atlas Performance Advisor recommends indexes from real query traffic and shows index-usage stats — first stop on Atlas; Atlas Query Profiler visualizes slow ops over time; mongotop, mongostat, and db.serverStatus() reveal cluster-level pressure (cache evictions, lock contention, network); APM tools like Datadog, New Relic, Honeycomb, or Sentry Performance show the per-request flame graph; Percona PMM is the open-source equivalent for self-hosted; maxTimeMS on every query is a safety net — runaway queries kill servers.

Question 21

Describe how you would implement a notification system that stores and retrieves notifications for users in MongoDB.

Situation: users receive notifications — new follower, comment on a post, mention, payment received. Need to store, retrieve unread, mark-as-read, paginate, and avoid notification fatigue (dedupe similar events). Volume is high during viral moments.

Approach: a notifications collection keyed by recipient with denormalized actor/object snapshots so reads don’t join. Aggregate similar events (“Alice and 4 others liked your post”) via grouping logic on write. Push through BullMQ/Inngest queues to handle bursts.

db.notifications.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  recipient_id: ObjectId("user_alice"),
  type:         "comment",                         // follow | like | comment | mention | system
  actor: {                                         // who did it — snapshot
    _id: ObjectId(),
    username: "bob",
    display_name: "Bob",
    avatar_url: "..."
  },
  object: {                                        // what they did it on — snapshot
    type: "post",
    _id: ObjectId(),
    preview: "Designing schemas..."
  },
  read:        false,
  read_at:     null,
  created_at:  new Date()
});

db.notifications.createIndex({ recipient_id: 1, created_at: -1 });
db.notifications.createIndex({ recipient_id: 1, read: 1, created_at: -1 });
db.notifications.createIndex({ created_at: 1 }, { expireAfterSeconds: 90*24*3600 });

// Recent notifications (most common read)
db.notifications.find({ recipient_id: userId })
                .sort({ created_at: -1 })
                .limit(20);

// Unread count for badge
db.notifications.countDocuments({ recipient_id: userId, read: false });

// Mark all as read
db.notifications.updateMany(
  { recipient_id: userId, read: false },
  { $set: { read: true, read_at: new Date() } }
);

// Aggregation: “Alice and 4 others liked your post”
//   On every new like, look for an existing “like on this post” notification < 24h old;
//   if found, append the actor; otherwise create a new one
async function notifyLike(postOwnerId, postId, actor) {
  const since = new Date(Date.now() - 24*3600*1000);
  const existing = await db.collection("notifications").findOneAndUpdate(
    {
      recipient_id: postOwnerId,
      type: "like_aggregate",
      "object._id": postId,
      created_at: { $gte: since }
    },
    {
      $addToSet: { "actors": actor },
      $set:      { last_actor: actor, updated_at: new Date(), read: false, read_at: null }
    },
    { returnDocument: "after" }
  );
  if (!existing) {
    await db.collection("notifications").insertOne({
      recipient_id: postOwnerId,
      type:        "like_aggregate",
      object:      { type: "post", _id: postId },
      actors:      [actor],
      last_actor:  actor,
      read:        false,
      created_at:  new Date(),
      updated_at:  new Date()
    });
  }
}

// Async fan-out via queue
import { Queue } from "bullmq";
const notifQueue = new Queue("notifications");
await notifQueue.add("like", { postOwnerId, postId, actor });

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
One doc per notificationSimple; per-notification read-stateEmbedded array on user — doc grows, can’t paginate efficiently
Denormalized actor/objectList render in 1 read; survives original deletionLive $lookup — slower; broken when source disappears
Aggregate similar eventsOne notification for 50 likes, not 50One per event — spam; overwhelming UX
TTL after 90 daysSelf-managing; old data is rarely viewedManual purge cron — another moving part
Async via queueBursty viral moments don’t back up the APISync writes — tail latency spikes

Production polish: push notifications need a delivery channel — Firebase Cloud Messaging, OneSignal, Pusher Beams, APNs (iOS), or Web Push via service workers; for transactional email, Postmark, Resend, Loops, or SendGrid; SMS via Twilio; respect user preferences — per-channel, per-type opt-outs; Knock, Courier, and Novu are turnkey notification platforms that handle templating, fan-out, batching, digesting, and DND windows out of the box; rate-limit per recipient + type to defeat notification storms; track open/click via campaign IDs and feed into product analytics for tuning frequency.

Question 22

You need to design a database for an online education platform with courses, students, and instructors. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: an online education platform with courses, lessons, quizzes, students, instructors, enrollments, and progress tracking. Need to render course pages quickly, track per-student progress, and aggregate analytics for instructors.

Approach: separate courses, users, enrollments, and progress collections. Embed lesson outline inside the course doc (bounded). Track progress per (student, lesson) row. Snapshot pricing and instructor metadata on enrollment so historical records survive course updates.

db.users.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  email: "alice@example.com",
  display_name: "Alice",
  roles: ["student"],          // student | instructor | admin
  created_at: new Date()
});

db.courses.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  slug: "intro-to-mongodb",
  title: "Intro to MongoDB",
  description: "...",
  instructor: {                 // snapshot
    _id: ObjectId("instructor_bob"),
    display_name: "Bob",
    avatar_url: "..."
  },
  price_cents: 4900,
  duration_minutes: 480,
  modules: [                    // embedded outline; bounded
    {
      _id: ObjectId(),
      title: "Getting Started",
      lessons: [
        {
          _id: ObjectId(),
          title: "What is MongoDB?",
          type: "video",        // video | reading | quiz
          duration_minutes: 12,
          video_url: "..."
        },
        { _id: ObjectId(), title: "Quiz 1", type: "quiz", quiz_id: ObjectId("...") }
      ]
    }
  ],
  status: "published",
  rating: { avg: 4.6, count: 230 },
  enrollment_count: 0,
  created_at: new Date()
});
db.courses.createIndex({ slug: 1 }, { unique: true });
db.courses.createIndex({ status: 1, created_at: -1 });
db.courses.createIndex({ "instructor._id": 1 });

db.enrollments.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  student_id: ObjectId("alice"),
  course_id:  ObjectId("..."),
  course_snapshot: {           // historical pricing/title
    title: "Intro to MongoDB",
    price_paid_cents: 4900
  },
  enrolled_at:    new Date(),
  status:         "active",     // active | completed | refunded
  completed_at:   null,
  certificate_id: null
});
db.enrollments.createIndex({ student_id: 1, course_id: 1 }, { unique: true });
db.enrollments.createIndex({ student_id: 1, enrolled_at: -1 });
db.enrollments.createIndex({ course_id: 1 });

db.progress.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  enrollment_id: ObjectId("..."),
  student_id:    ObjectId("alice"),
  course_id:     ObjectId("..."),
  lesson_id:     ObjectId("..."),
  status:        "completed",   // not_started | in_progress | completed
  watched_seconds: 720,
  completed_at:    new Date(),
  quiz_score:      null
});
db.progress.createIndex({ enrollment_id: 1, lesson_id: 1 }, { unique: true });
db.progress.createIndex({ student_id: 1, completed_at: -1 });

// “My courses” with progress percentages
db.enrollments.aggregate([
  { $match: { student_id: ObjectId("alice"), status: "active" } },
  { $lookup: {
      from: "progress",
      let: { eid: "$_id" },
      pipeline: [
        { $match: { $expr: { $eq: ["$enrollment_id", "$$eid"] } } },
        { $group: { _id: null, completed: { $sum: { $cond: [{ $eq: ["$status", "completed"] }, 1, 0] } }, total: { $sum: 1 } } }
      ],
      as: "stats"
  }},
  { $unwind: { path: "$stats", preserveNullAndEmptyArrays: true } }
]);

// Mark a lesson complete
db.progress.updateOne(
  { enrollment_id: eid, lesson_id: lid },
  { $set: { status: "completed", completed_at: new Date(), watched_seconds: 720 } },
  { upsert: true }
);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Embed module/lesson outlineCourse page renders in 1 read; bounded sizeSeparate lessons collection — needed if 1000+ lessons per course
Per-(student, lesson) progress rowGranular tracking; partial completion is normalEmbed progress on enrollment — doc grows with lesson count
Snapshot price & instructorPrice changes don’t rewrite history; refund math intactLive join — refund disputes when prices change
Compound unique on (student, lesson)Idempotent “mark complete” via upsertApp-level dedupe — race condition risk
Denormalized rating + enrollment countsCourse list renders without aggregationsLive countDocuments — slow at scale

Production polish: for video delivery, never serve from MongoDB — use Mux, Cloudflare Stream, Bunny Stream, or AWS MediaConvert + S3 + CloudFront; for assignments and quizzes at scale, integrate LearnDash, TalentLMS, or build on top of Open edX; certificates: generate PDFs with Puppeteer/WeasyPrint, store in S3, link from enrollment doc; payments via Stripe Checkout or Lemon Squeezy (handles tax for digital goods globally); progress events feed instructor analytics dashboards via change streams → BigQuery or PostHog; for live cohort-based courses, layer in Discord/Circle/Slack for community.

Question 23

How would you handle full-text search in MongoDB for a collection of articles?

Situation: a content site has 100K+ articles. Users search by free text — titles, body, tags — with typo tolerance, autocomplete, faceted filters (category, year, author), and relevance ranking. Built-in $text covers basic cases; serious search needs more.

Approach: for prototypes, use the built-in text index with $text. For production-grade search (typo tolerance, autocomplete, synonyms, multi-language), use MongoDB Atlas Search (Lucene-powered) or mirror to a dedicated engine like Elasticsearch, Meilisearch, or Typesense.

// Option 1: Built-in text index (fastest to set up; limited features)
db.articles.createIndex(
  { title: "text", body: "text", tags: "text" },
  { weights: { title: 10, tags: 5, body: 1 }, name: "articles_text" }
);

db.articles.find(
  { $text: { $search: "mongodb schema design" } },
  { score: { $meta: "textScore" } }
).sort({ score: { $meta: "textScore" } });

// Phrases, exclusions, multi-term
db.articles.find({ $text: { $search: "\"schema design\" -relational" } });

// Limitations of $text:
//   - one text index per collection
//   - no fuzzy matching (typo tolerance)
//   - no autocomplete
//   - one language per index (or per-doc via `language` field)

// Option 2: Atlas Search (recommended for production on Atlas)
//   Define an Atlas Search index in the Atlas UI or via API:
{
  "mappings": {
    "dynamic": false,
    "fields": {
      "title":     { "type": "string", "analyzer": "lucene.english" },
      "body":      { "type": "string", "analyzer": "lucene.english" },
      "tags":      { "type": "string" },
      "category":  { "type": "stringFacet" },
      "published_year": { "type": "numberFacet" },
      "author.name": { "type": "string" }
    }
  }
}

// Query via $search aggregation
db.articles.aggregate([
  { $search: {
      index: "articles",
      compound: {
        must: [
          { text: { query: "mongodb schema",
                    path: ["title", "body"],
                    fuzzy: { maxEdits: 1, prefixLength: 2 } } }
        ],
        filter: [
          { equals: { path: "category", value: "tutorial" } }
        ]
      },
      highlight: { path: ["title", "body"] }
  }},
  { $facet: {
      results: [
        { $project: { title: 1, score: { $meta: "searchScore" }, highlights: { $meta: "searchHighlights" } } },
        { $limit: 20 }
      ],
      facets: [
        { $searchMeta: {
            index: "articles",
            facet: {
              operator: { compound: { must: [...] } },
              facets: {
                category_facet:  { type: "string", path: "category" },
                year_facet:      { type: "number", path: "published_year", boundaries: [2020,2021,2022,2023,2024,2025,2026] }
              }
        }}}
      ]
  }}
]);

// Autocomplete via Atlas Search edgeGram analyzer
db.articles.aggregate([
  { $search: {
      index: "articles_autocomplete",
      autocomplete: { query: userInput, path: "title", tokenOrder: "sequential" }
  }},
  { $limit: 10 }
]);

// Option 3: Mirror to Elasticsearch / Meilisearch via change streams
const stream = db.collection("articles").watch(
  [{ $match: { operationType: { $in: ["insert", "update", "delete"] } } }],
  { fullDocument: "updateLookup" }
);
stream.on("change", change => {
  if (change.operationType === "delete") {
    elastic.delete({ index: "articles", id: change.documentKey._id });
  } else {
    elastic.index({ index: "articles", id: change.fullDocument._id, body: change.fullDocument });
  }
});

Trade-offs:

EngineProsCons
$text + text indexNo extra infra; in the same clusterNo fuzzy/autocomplete; one per collection; weak ranking
Atlas Search (Lucene)In Atlas; fuzzy/autocomplete/synonyms/highlights/facets; BM25 scoringAtlas-only; per-search-index pricing
Elasticsearch / OpenSearchMost powerful; aggregations + ML; on-premOperational complexity; sync lag; resource-heavy
Meilisearch / TypesenseEasy to run; fast; great for catalogsLess feature-rich than ES; weaker analytics
AlgoliaHosted, blazing fast, polished UX widgetsCost scales with traffic

Production polish: mirror via change streams to keep the search index near-real-time consistent (typically <1s lag); for typo tolerance + autocomplete, Atlas Search’s fuzzy + autocomplete operators are the easiest path; for vector search (semantic, “articles like this one”), use Atlas Vector Search with embeddings from OpenAI, Cohere, or Voyage; combine with text relevance for hybrid search; cache hot query+facet combinations in Redis; track no-result queries to identify content gaps and synonym tuning opportunities.

Question 24

Explain how you would implement a data archiving strategy for a MongoDB database.

Situation: a database with millions of historical records grows daily. Most queries hit recent data; old records are kept for compliance and rare lookups. Storage and backup costs balloon. Need an archival strategy without breaking reads on archived data.

Approach: tier the data — hot in MongoDB, warm in Atlas Online Archive (queryable, cheaper), cold in S3/Glacier (compliance-only). Use TTL indexes for ephemeral data, scheduled $out/$merge for warm migration, and Federated Database queries to span tiers transparently.

// Tier 1: hot — recent 90 days, fully indexed in main cluster
//   no special config, just normal collection

// Tier 2: warm — Atlas Online Archive (Atlas-only feature)
//   Configure in Atlas UI: archive documents older than 90 days
//   Documents transparently move to cheaper object storage
//   Queries via Federated Database can span hot + warm
db.orders.aggregate([
  { $match: { placed_at: { $gte: ISODate("2024-01-01") } } }   // spans hot + archive
]);

// Tier 3: cold — S3/Glacier
//   Older than 7 years, only restored on legal request

// Self-hosted equivalent: scheduled archival to a cold collection or cluster
// 1. Stream eligible documents to an archive cluster
db.orders.aggregate([
  { $match: { placed_at: { $lt: ninetyDaysAgo } } },
  { $merge: { into: { db: "archive", coll: "orders" } } }
]);

// 2. Then delete from hot cluster
db.orders.deleteMany({ placed_at: { $lt: ninetyDaysAgo } });

// TTL for ephemeral data (sessions, OTPs, audit logs after retention)
db.sessions.createIndex({ expires_at: 1 }, { expireAfterSeconds: 0 });
db.audit_log.createIndex({ created_at: 1 }, { expireAfterSeconds: 7*365*24*3600 });

// Time-series collections accept expireAfterSeconds at creation
db.createCollection("metrics", {
  timeseries: { timeField: "ts", metaField: "device" },
  expireAfterSeconds: 90 * 24 * 3600
});

// Selective archival via background job (BullMQ)
import { Queue, Worker } from "bullmq";
const archiveQueue = new Queue("archive");
new Worker("archive", async () => {
  const cutoff = new Date(Date.now() - 90*24*3600*1000);
  // Stream to S3 as compressed BSON
  const cursor = db.collection("orders").find({ placed_at: { $lt: cutoff } }).batchSize(1000);
  const stream = bsonEncoderStream();
  cursor.stream().pipe(zstdCompress()).pipe(stream).pipe(s3Upload("archive/orders.bson.zst"));
  await stream.finished;
  // Then delete from hot
  await db.collection("orders").deleteMany({ placed_at: { $lt: cutoff } });
});

// Querying cold data: rehydrate on demand
async function fetchCold(id) {
  const fileKey = "archive/orders/2023.bson.zst";
  // Stream from S3, decompress, find by id
}

// Atlas Federated Database: query S3 + MongoDB in one statement
//   set up a federated database in Atlas; configure S3 as a virtual collection
db.federated.orders.find({ user_id: uid });   // spans live + S3 transparently

Trade-offs:

TierCostLatencyUse
Hot (live MongoDB)$$$ (RAM-bound)msLast 90 days; daily reads
Atlas Online Archive$ (S3-backed, queryable)seconds1-7 years; rare reads
S3 Standard$ per GB-monthsecondsBackups, raw events for warehouse
S3 Glacier / Glacier Deep Archive$$ per restore, ¢ per GBhoursCompliance retention 7+ years
TTL indexesFreen/a (auto-delete)Sessions, ephemeral, anything purely transient

Production polish: Atlas Online Archive is the simplest path on Atlas — tag the field and rule, MongoDB does the rest; for self-hosted, write the archival job once, schedule via BullMQ/Inngest/Temporal, monitor via Datadog; verify integrity periodically (random-sample row hashes); use S3 Object Lock in compliance mode for tamper-proof archives (ransomware defense + regulatory); for warehouse-style historical analytics, ship raw events to BigQuery, Snowflake, or Databricks via Airbyte/Fivetran/Estuary; document the retention matrix per data type with legal — GDPR and HIPAA dictate floors and ceilings.

Question 25

You need to design a schema for a reservation system with hotels, rooms, and bookings. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a hotel reservation system with hotels, rooms, and bookings. Users search availability by date range and city, view hotel details with photos and reviews, and book rooms. Concurrency matters — two users mustn’t book the same room for overlapping dates.

Approach: separate hotels, rooms, and bookings. Embed hotel-level data (photos, amenities) for read efficiency; rooms reference hotel; bookings hold the date range. Enforce no-overlap via a transactional check on insert, since MongoDB has no native range-overlap exclusion constraint.

db.hotels.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  slug: "grand-hotel-paris",
  name: "Grand Hotel Paris",
  address: { line1: "1 Rue de...", city: "Paris", country: "FR", postal: "75001" },
  location: { type: "Point", coordinates: [2.3522, 48.8566] },     // GeoJSON
  amenities: ["wifi", "pool", "parking", "breakfast"],
  photos: ["https://cdn.../1.jpg", "https://cdn.../2.jpg"],
  rating: { avg: 4.5, count: 1230 },
  star_rating: 4,
  active: true
});
db.hotels.createIndex({ location: "2dsphere" });
db.hotels.createIndex({ "address.city": 1, active: 1 });
db.hotels.createIndex({ slug: 1 }, { unique: true });

db.rooms.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  hotel_id: ObjectId("..."),
  room_number: "401",
  type: "deluxe_double",      // standard | deluxe_double | suite
  capacity: 2,
  price_per_night_cents: 19900,
  active: true
});
db.rooms.createIndex({ hotel_id: 1, active: 1 });

db.bookings.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id:  ObjectId("..."),
  hotel_id: ObjectId("..."),
  room_id:  ObjectId("..."),
  check_in:  ISODate("2026-06-01"),
  check_out: ISODate("2026-06-05"),
  guests:    2,
  status:    "confirmed",      // pending | confirmed | cancelled | refunded
  total_cents: 79600,
  hotel_snapshot: { name: "Grand Hotel Paris", city: "Paris" },
  room_snapshot:  { type: "deluxe_double", price_per_night_cents: 19900 },
  created_at: new Date()
});
db.bookings.createIndex({ room_id: 1, check_in: 1 });
db.bookings.createIndex({ user_id: 1, check_in: -1 });
db.bookings.createIndex({ hotel_id: 1, check_in: 1 });

// Search: hotels in city with rooms available on dates
const requestedRange = { check_in: ISODate("2026-06-01"), check_out: ISODate("2026-06-05") };

// 1. Find rooms NOT booked in the requested range
db.bookings.aggregate([
  { $match: {
      status: { $in: ["pending", "confirmed"] },
      // overlap: existing.check_in < requested.check_out AND existing.check_out > requested.check_in
      check_in:  { $lt: requestedRange.check_out },
      check_out: { $gt: requestedRange.check_in }
  }},
  { $group: { _id: "$room_id" } }   // booked-room ids
]);

// 2. Available rooms = (rooms in hotels in city) MINUS (booked rooms)
//    Achievable via $lookup or two-step query

// Booking creation — transactional double-check to prevent races
async function bookRoom(userId, roomId, checkIn, checkOut) {
  const session = client.startSession();
  let booking;
  await session.withTransaction(async () => {
    // Re-check no overlap inside the transaction
    const conflict = await db.collection("bookings").findOne({
      room_id: roomId,
      status:  { $in: ["pending", "confirmed"] },
      check_in:  { $lt: checkOut },
      check_out: { $gt: checkIn }
    }, { session });
    if (conflict) throw new Error("Room no longer available");

    booking = {
      _id: new ObjectId(), user_id: userId, room_id: roomId,
      check_in: checkIn, check_out: checkOut, status: "confirmed",
      created_at: new Date()
    };
    await db.collection("bookings").insertOne(booking, { session });
  });
  return booking;
}

// Geo search: hotels within 5km of a point
db.hotels.find({
  location: {
    $nearSphere: {
      $geometry: { type: "Point", coordinates: [2.3522, 48.8566] },
      $maxDistance: 5000
    }
  },
  active: true
});

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Range-overlap check in transactionOnly safe way without exclusion constraintsApp-only check — race condition between read and insert
Snapshot hotel/room on bookingPricing/name changes don’t corrupt historyLive join — broken when room is renamed/deleted
2dsphere index on locationNative “within X km” queriesApp-side haversine — reinventing the wheel
Compound index (room_id, check_in)Overlap query uses index for narrow scansScan-then-filter — slow with many bookings
Status fieldCancellation invalidates booking without deleteDelete — loses audit trail; refund races

Production polish: for serious hotel inventory, integrate a global distribution system (Sabre, Amadeus, Travelport) or channel manager (SiteMinder, Cloudbeds) — never source pricing/availability from your own DB alone; payments via Stripe with manual capture (authorize at booking, capture at check-in); fraud detection via Stripe Radar or Sift; geo search beyond simple radius needs PostGIS or Tile38; reviews moderated via Perspective API; transactional emails (confirmations, reminders) via Postmark/Resend; analytics on conversion funnels via Amplitude; consider idempotency keys on booking POST to defeat double-clicks and retries.

Question 26

How would you handle data replication and failover in a MongoDB replica set?

Situation: a production replica set must survive node failure with zero data loss for committed writes and minimal application downtime. Reads should ideally continue during a primary failover.

Approach: deploy three data-bearing nodes across availability zones — never two-node + arbiter, which is fragile. Configure w: "majority" for writes and a sensible read preference per workload. The driver handles automatic retry on transient errors.

// Replica set initiation
rs.initiate({
  _id: "rs0",
  members: [
    { _id: 0, host: "mongo-a.prod:27017", priority: 2 },   // preferred primary
    { _id: 1, host: "mongo-b.prod:27017", priority: 1 },
    { _id: 2, host: "mongo-c.prod:27017", priority: 1 }
  ]
});

// Inspect status
rs.status();           // members[].stateStr: PRIMARY | SECONDARY | RECOVERING
rs.printSecondaryReplicationInfo();

// Driver connection string — list ALL members; driver discovers topology
const uri = "mongodb://mongo-a.prod,mongo-b.prod,mongo-c.prod/app" +
            "?replicaSet=rs0&retryWrites=true&w=majority&readConcernLevel=majority";

// Per-operation read preference for stale-tolerant reads
db.collection("products").find({ active: true })
  .readPref("secondaryPreferred", [{ region: "us-east" }]);

// Force a manual stepdown for maintenance (60s freeze)
rs.stepDown(60);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
w: "majority"Write isn’t lost on primary failurew: 1 — faster but loses committed writes on rollback
Three data nodes (no arbiter)Arbiters can’t serve reads; can’t hold majority alone — {w: "majority", j: true} may stallP-S-A: cheaper but risky; only for prototypes
retryWrites=trueTransient blips during failover are auto-retriedApp retries: more code; easy to miss edge cases
Cross-AZ deploymentOne AZ outage doesn’t take down quorumSingle AZ — cheap but a single AZ failure = full outage
Priority on preferred nodePredictable post-failover topologyEqual priority — election picks arbitrarily

Production polish: use MongoDB Atlas — replica sets are managed, AZ placement is automatic, point-in-time restore is built in. Monitor replication lag via Atlas alerts or Datadog / New Relic; lag >10s typically signals primary write saturation. Use the MongoDB Kubernetes Operator for self-hosted; Percona PMM for observability outside Atlas. Test failover regularly with chaos drills (Gremlin, AWS FIS) — an untested failover plan is a hope. Application-level circuit breakers (Resilience4j, opossum) shed load gracefully when MongoDB is unreachable. For multi-region writes, evaluate Atlas Global Clusters with zoned sharding rather than cross-region replica sets, which suffer from latency-bound write throughput.

Question 27

Describe how you would implement geospatial queries in MongoDB for a location-based service.

Situation: a location-based service (food delivery, ride-sharing, “nearby” search) needs to find points of interest within a radius, sort them by distance, and stay sub-100ms even at millions of POIs.

Approach: store coordinates as GeoJSON Point in a dedicated field, build a 2dsphere index, and use $geoNear (in aggregation, returns distance) or $nearSphere (in find()). 2dsphere uses spherical Earth math — meters as the natural unit.

// Document shape — coordinates are [longitude, latitude] (NOT lat, lng)
db.restaurants.insertOne({
  _id:    ObjectId(),
  name:   "Pizza Place",
  cuisine:"italian",
  rating: 4.5,
  location: { type: "Point", coordinates: [-73.9857, 40.7484] },  // Times Square
  active: true,
  open_until_minutes: 1380   // 23:00 in minutes from midnight
});

// Index: 2dsphere + filterable secondary fields
db.restaurants.createIndex({ location: "2dsphere", cuisine: 1, active: 1 });

// Find restaurants within 1km, sorted by distance, returning the distance
db.restaurants.aggregate([
  { $geoNear: {
      near:          { type: "Point", coordinates: [-73.9857, 40.7484] },
      distanceField: "distance_meters",
      maxDistance:   1000,                              // meters
      spherical:     true,
      query:         { active: true, cuisine: "italian" }
  }},
  { $limit: 20 }
]);

// "Within polygon" query — e.g., delivery zone
db.restaurants.find({
  location: { $geoWithin: {
    $geometry: {
      type: "Polygon",
      coordinates: [[[-74.0, 40.7], [-73.9, 40.7], [-73.9, 40.8], [-74.0, 40.8], [-74.0, 40.7]]]
    }
  }}
});

// Drivers tracking moving objects: store + update every 10-30s
db.drivers.updateOne(
  { _id: driverId },
  { $set: { location: { type: "Point", coordinates: [lng, lat] }, updated_at: new Date() } }
);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
2dsphere over 2dSpherical Earth math; meters as unit; works at poles/dateline2d — flat-plane only, deprecated for new apps
$geoNear (pipeline)Returns distance, supports complex chained stages$nearSphere (find) — no distance, fewer post-stages
Filter inside query2dsphere index narrows by both location AND filterFilter after — index only narrows by location
GeoJSON point formatStandard; works with all modern toolsLegacy [lng, lat] field — works but less interop
Coordinates [lng, lat]GeoJSON spec mandates this order — the universal source of bugsDon’t reorder — you’ll always regret it

Production polish: for high-update moving fleets (every-second driver location), MongoDB struggles — use Tile38 or Redis with GEO commands for the hot path and persist snapshots back to MongoDB. For complex polygon ops (intersections, buffers, ST_Union), PostGIS on PostgreSQL is far more capable. H3 (Uber’s hexagonal grid) is the modern way to bucket geo data into cells for analytics. Mapping/visualization: Mapbox, MapTiler, Google Maps Platform. Atlas Search supports geo too — combine text + geo queries cleanly. Always set a maxDistance — without it, the query scans the entire dataset sorted by distance. For ETA calculations, never haversine in JS — call Mapbox Directions, Google Routes, HERE, or Valhalla.

Question 28

You need to migrate a large MongoDB collection to a new schema. How would you plan and execute the migration?

Situation: a 200M-document users collection needs a schema change — renaming a field, splitting an embedded doc out, or normalizing a denormalized array. Direct updateMany would lock the collection for hours and bloat the oplog.

Approach: apply backwards-compatible changes in stages: dual-write to both shapes, lazy-migrate on read, batch-migrate the rest in chunks during off-peak, then remove the old shape. Never a big-bang.

// Stage 0: deploy app code that READS both shapes
function readUser(doc) {
  // New shape preferred; fall back to old shape
  return {
    name: doc.name ?? `${doc.first_name} ${doc.last_name}`,
    email: doc.email
  };
}

// Stage 1: deploy app code that WRITES both shapes
db.users.updateOne(
  { _id: userId },
  {
    $set: {
      name:       newName,
      first_name: firstFromName(newName),    // backfill old shape
      last_name:  lastFromName(newName)
    }
  }
);

// Stage 2: backfill in chunks (run in maintenance window)
const cursor = db.users.find({ name: { $exists: false } }).batchSize(500);
let count = 0;
for await (const doc of cursor) {
  await db.users.updateOne(
    { _id: doc._id },
    [
      { $set: {
          name: { $concat: ["$first_name", " ", "$last_name"] },
          schema_version: 2
      }}
    ]
  );
  if (++count % 500 === 0) await sleep(50);   // pace replication
}

// Stage 3: deploy app code that READS new shape only

// Stage 4: deploy app code that WRITES new shape only

// Stage 5: cleanup — remove old fields
db.users.updateMany(
  { schema_version: 2 },
  { $unset: { first_name: "", last_name: "" } }
);
db.users.dropIndex("first_name_1");
db.users.dropIndex("last_name_1");

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Six-stage rolloutEach step is reversible; no “all or nothing” riskBig-bang — if it fails halfway you’re stuck
Schema version fieldIdentifies migrated docs; idempotent re-runsNo version — can’t resume after a crash
Pipeline-form updateCompute new value from existing fields server-sideRead-then-write — race condition between read and update
Batched chunksAvoids lock contention; bounds replication lagSingle updateMany({}) — oplog floods, secondaries lag minutes
batchSize(500) + sleepCaps pressure on the primaryUnbounded scan — can starve user traffic

Production polish: use Atlas Migration Service for managed cluster moves; for self-hosted, mongosync handles online cluster-to-cluster replication. Track migration progress with a stats collection ({ phase, total, migrated, errors_count }) updated every batch. Run on a secondary with readPreference: "secondaryPreferred" if read traffic suffers. Add a kill switch (a config doc the migration script polls) so on-call can stop it instantly. JSON Schema validation at the collection level enforces the new shape after stage 5. For trickier transformations, evaluate Debezium + Kafka to stream changes to a parallel collection while you cut over. Always test the full migration on a restored production snapshot first — staging data never has the same edge cases as prod.

Question 29

Explain how you would design a schema for a real-time analytics dashboard using MongoDB.

Situation: a SaaS dashboard needs to show real-time metrics — signups, revenue, active users, error rates — with sub-second response times even as raw event volume hits millions per day.

Approach: separate raw events from pre-aggregated rollups. Events flow into a time-series collection. A scheduled $merge aggregation rolls up to per-minute, per-hour, per-day buckets. The dashboard queries the rollup collection — never the raw stream.

// Raw events — time-series collection (5.0+) auto-buckets internally
db.createCollection("events", {
  timeseries: { timeField: "ts", metaField: "meta", granularity: "seconds" }
});
db.events.insertOne({
  ts: new Date(),
  meta: { type: "signup", tenant_id: "acme", region: "us-east" },
  user_id: ObjectId(),
  metadata: { plan: "pro" }
});

// Per-minute rollup (run every minute via cron / Atlas Trigger)
db.events.aggregate([
  { $match: { ts: { $gte: lastMinuteStart, $lt: thisMinuteStart } } },
  { $group: {
      _id: {
        minute:    { $dateTrunc: { date: "$ts", unit: "minute" } },
        type:      "$meta.type",
        tenant_id: "$meta.tenant_id"
      },
      count:    { $sum: 1 },
      revenue:  { $sum: { $ifNull: ["$metadata.amount_cents", 0] } }
  }},
  { $merge: {
      into: "metrics_minute",
      on:   ["_id"],
      whenMatched:    "replace",
      whenNotMatched: "insert"
  }}
]);

// Per-hour rollup — from per-minute (cheap)
db.metrics_minute.aggregate([
  { $match: { "_id.minute": { $gte: lastHourStart, $lt: thisHourStart } } },
  { $group: {
      _id: {
        hour:      { $dateTrunc: { date: "$_id.minute", unit: "hour" } },
        type:      "$_id.type",
        tenant_id: "$_id.tenant_id"
      },
      count:   { $sum: "$count" },
      revenue: { $sum: "$revenue" }
  }},
  { $merge: { into: "metrics_hour", on: ["_id"], whenMatched: "replace", whenNotMatched: "insert" } }
]);

// Indexes for fast dashboard queries
db.metrics_minute.createIndex({ "_id.tenant_id": 1, "_id.minute": -1 });
db.metrics_hour.createIndex({ "_id.tenant_id": 1, "_id.hour": -1 });

// Dashboard query: last 24h hourly revenue
db.metrics_hour.find({
  "_id.tenant_id": "acme",
  "_id.type":      "purchase",
  "_id.hour":      { $gte: ISODate(new Date() - 24*3600*1000) }
}).sort({ "_id.hour": 1 });

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Time-series collectionAuto-buckets, ~5x compression, built-in TTLRegular collection — bigger index, slower scans
Pre-aggregated tiersDashboard queries hit small collection; sub-100msLive aggregation — seconds-to-minutes per query
Rollup-from-rollupHour from minute is cheap; day from hour is cheapEach tier from raw — recomputes everything
$dateTrunc grouping5.0+ feature; handles timezone, DST correctly$year/$month/$day chains — verbose, error-prone
$merge replaceRe-runnable; recovers from missed runs cleanly$out — replaces whole collection, loses other tenants’ data

Production polish: for sub-second, true-real-time (single-event latency), pair MongoDB with Redis counters or ClickHouse for OLAP queries that span billions of rows. MongoDB Atlas Charts renders the dashboard directly from the rollup collection — no app server needed. Atlas Triggers schedule the rollups; alternatives are Inngest, Trigger.dev, Temporal, or a Kubernetes CronJob. Stream raw events through Apache Kafka or AWS Kinesis for buffering at scale — MongoDB then becomes the materialized sink. For complex BI dashboards (cohorts, funnels, retention), copy events to a warehouse: BigQuery, Snowflake, Databricks, or Tinybird (purpose-built for fast analytics over event streams).

Question 30

How would you handle pagination and sorting for a collection with a large number of documents?

Situation: a feed-style or table-style UI lists posts/orders/events from a collection of millions, sorted by recency, with infinite scroll. Naive skip(N).limit(P) degrades linearly — page 1000 is unusable.

Approach: use cursor-based pagination. The client passes back the last item’s sort key; the next query is { sort_key: { $lt: last_value } }. The query stays O(P) regardless of page depth.

// Schema: posts sorted by created_at desc
db.posts.createIndex({ created_at: -1, _id: -1 });   // tie-breaker for ties

// Page 1 — no cursor
const page1 = await db.posts.find({})
  .sort({ created_at: -1, _id: -1 })
  .limit(20)
  .toArray();

// Send to client + last item’s cursor
const lastItem = page1[page1.length - 1];
const next_cursor = {
  created_at: lastItem.created_at,
  _id:        lastItem._id
};

// Page 2 — continue strictly past the last item
const page2 = await db.posts.find({
  $or: [
    { created_at: { $lt: next_cursor.created_at } },
    { created_at: next_cursor.created_at, _id: { $lt: next_cursor._id } }
  ]
})
.sort({ created_at: -1, _id: -1 })
.limit(20)
.toArray();

// Encode cursor for safe URL transport (base64 of JSON)
const encoded = Buffer.from(JSON.stringify(next_cursor)).toString("base64url");
// /api/posts?cursor=eyJjcmVhdGVkX2F0IjoiMjAyNi0wNC0yNyJ9

// Filtered + paginated — index covers everything
db.posts.createIndex({ author_id: 1, created_at: -1, _id: -1 });
db.posts.find({
  author_id: authorId,
  $or: [
    { created_at: { $lt: cursor.created_at } },
    { created_at: cursor.created_at, _id: { $lt: cursor._id } }
  ]
}).sort({ created_at: -1, _id: -1 }).limit(20);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Cursor over skip/limitO(page_size); page 1000 is as fast as page 1skip(N) — scans N docs every page; degrades linearly
Compound (sort_key, _id)Resolves ties when multiple docs share the same timestampsort_key alone — same-timestamp items can be skipped or duplicated
Index direction matches sortNo SORT stage; index streams in orderMismatch — in-memory sort, fails over 100MB
Filter inside index prefixESR: equality first, then sortFilter after sort — can’t use index efficiently
Encode cursor opaquelyHides DB shape; lets you change schema laterPlain JSON in URL — coupling clients to internals

Production polish: cursor pagination doesn’t support “jump to page 47” — that’s a UX choice, not a tech limitation. For numbered pagination at moderate depth, hybrid approach: deep pages funnel to search/filter UI. For total-count display, keep an estimated count via estimatedDocumentCount() or a separate counter document — never countDocuments({}) on every page. Backend: implement consistent cursor encoding via Relay’s spec (forward+backward cursors, hasNextPage, hasPreviousPage) for clean GraphQL APIs. For full-text search results pagination, use Atlas Search’s searchAfter token — it understands relevance scoring. HTTP caching with Cache-Control: private for first pages of stable feeds. Bots and SEO need rel="next"/rel="prev" link headers; or render a sitemap. Watch for “phantom items” — new posts inserted between page loads can cause skips; mitigate with snapshot timestamps in the cursor.

Question 31

Describe how you would implement a tagging system where multiple tags can be associated with multiple documents.

Situation: an app needs to attach many tags to many documents (articles, photos, products) — a classic many-to-many. Filter by tag, find related docs, count usage per tag, suggest popular tags.

Approach: embed tags as a string array on the document (denormalized) for fast read+filter; maintain a separate tags collection for canonical metadata, usage counts, and autocomplete; multikey index on the array for index-backed lookups.

// Documents embed tag slugs (lowercase, normalized)
db.articles.insertOne({
  _id:        ObjectId(),
  title:      "Why MongoDB is great",
  body:       "...",
  author_id:  ObjectId(),
  tags:       ["mongodb", "databases", "nosql", "tutorial"],
  created_at: new Date()
});

// Multikey index over tags — one entry per tag value per doc
db.articles.createIndex({ tags: 1, created_at: -1 });

// Canonical tags collection — metadata, counts, slugs
db.tags.insertOne({
  _id:           "mongodb",                    // lowercase slug as _id
  display_name:  "MongoDB",
  description:   "NoSQL document database",
  usage_count:   1247,                         // denormalized for ranking
  related_tags:  ["nosql", "databases"],
  created_at:    new Date()
});
db.tags.createIndex({ usage_count: -1 });
db.tags.createIndex({ display_name: "text" });   // for autocomplete

// Find articles with ANY of these tags
db.articles.find({ tags: { $in: ["mongodb", "redis"] } }).sort({ created_at: -1 });

// Find articles tagged with ALL of these tags
db.articles.find({ tags: { $all: ["mongodb", "tutorial"] } });

// Atomic add tag (idempotent — no duplicates)
async function addTag(articleId, tag) {
  const session = client.startSession();
  await session.withTransaction(async () => {
    const result = await db.collection("articles").updateOne(
      { _id: articleId, tags: { $ne: tag } },
      { $addToSet: { tags: tag } },
      { session }
    );
    if (result.modifiedCount === 1) {
      await db.collection("tags").updateOne(
        { _id: tag },
        {
          $inc: { usage_count: 1 },
          $setOnInsert: { display_name: tag, created_at: new Date() }
        },
        { upsert: true, session }
      );
    }
  });
}

// Top 10 tags
db.tags.find().sort({ usage_count: -1 }).limit(10);

// Tag autocomplete (Atlas Search recommended; here’s the basic version)
db.tags.find({ display_name: /^mong/i }).sort({ usage_count: -1 }).limit(10);

// Drift correction: rebuild usage counts (nightly)
db.articles.aggregate([
  { $unwind: "$tags" },
  { $group:  { _id: "$tags", count: { $sum: 1 } } },
  { $merge:  { into: "tags", on: "_id", whenMatched: [{ $set: { usage_count: "$$new.count" } }] } }
]);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Embed tag slugs in docOne read returns doc + tags; index-backed filteringJunction collection — needs $lookup for every read
Separate tags collectionOwns metadata, count, related-tag linksCompute on-the-fly — expensive for autocomplete
Slug as _idLookups by slug = primary-key lookupObjectId + slug field — extra index, more memory
Transactional count updateDoc tags and tag count stay consistentAsync — counts drift; need periodic reconciliation
$addToSet over $pushIdempotent; same tag never duplicates$push — duplicates appear after retry

Production polish: for autocomplete with typo tolerance and ranking, MongoDB Atlas Search with autocomplete operator and edge-n-gram analyzers far outperforms regex. Alternatives: Algolia, Meilisearch, Typesense, Elasticsearch. For tag suggestions (“people who tagged X also tagged Y”), compute a co-occurrence matrix nightly via aggregation and store top related tags on each tag doc. Slug normalization should happen on input: lowercase, strip whitespace, replace spaces with -, remove non-ASCII or slugify them. Banned/reserved tags (profanity, spam) live in a banned_tags collection checked on creation. For tag clouds and faceted UI, expose a /api/tags?prefix=mong&limit=10 endpoint backed by Atlas Search. Soft-delete tags (don’t hard-delete) so historical articles still render their tag context.

Question 32

You need to design a schema for a healthcare application with patients, doctors, and appointments. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a healthcare app stores patients, doctors, and appointments. PHI must be encrypted; appointments need conflict-free booking; doctors view their schedule; patients view their visit history; HIPAA dictates audit trails on every access.

Approach: three primary collections with carefully chosen denormalization. Encrypt PHI fields with Queryable Encryption. Booking goes through a transaction to prevent double-bookings. Every read/write is logged to an immutable audit collection.

// patients — PHI encrypted client-side via Queryable Encryption (7.0+)
db.patients.insertOne({
  _id:           ObjectId(),
  external_id:   "PAT-00012",                       // safe to query unencrypted
  first_name:    encryptedClient.encrypt("Alice"),  // encrypted
  last_name:     encryptedClient.encrypt("Smith"),  // encrypted
  dob:           encryptedClient.encrypt(ISODate("1990-05-12")),  // encrypted, range queryable
  ssn_last4:     encryptedClient.encrypt("1234"),   // encrypted, equality queryable
  insurance: { provider: "BCBS", member_id: "M123456" },
  primary_doctor_id: ObjectId(),
  created_at:    new Date()
});
db.patients.createIndex({ external_id: 1 }, { unique: true });
db.patients.createIndex({ primary_doctor_id: 1 });

// doctors — npis, specialty, availability
db.doctors.insertOne({
  _id:        ObjectId(),
  npi:        "1234567890",                       // unique national provider id
  name:       "Dr. Jane Doe",
  specialty:  "cardiology",
  active:     true,
  schedule_template: {                            // weekly recurring availability
    monday:    [{ start: "09:00", end: "12:00" }, { start: "14:00", end: "17:00" }],
    tuesday:   [{ start: "09:00", end: "17:00" }]
  }
});
db.doctors.createIndex({ npi: 1 }, { unique: true });
db.doctors.createIndex({ specialty: 1, active: 1 });

// appointments — reference both, snapshot key fields
db.appointments.insertOne({
  _id:         ObjectId(),
  patient_id:  ObjectId(),
  doctor_id:   ObjectId(),
  start:       ISODate("2026-05-15T14:00:00Z"),
  end:         ISODate("2026-05-15T14:30:00Z"),
  status:      "scheduled",  // scheduled | completed | cancelled | no_show
  reason:      encryptedClient.encrypt("annual physical"),
  patient_snapshot: { name: "Alice S.", external_id: "PAT-00012" },
  created_at:  new Date()
});
db.appointments.createIndex({ doctor_id: 1, start: 1 }, { unique: true });   // no double-book
db.appointments.createIndex({ patient_id: 1, start: -1 });

// Booking with transaction — atomic conflict check
async function book(patientId, doctorId, start, end) {
  const session = client.startSession();
  await session.withTransaction(async () => {
    const overlap = await db.collection("appointments").findOne({
      doctor_id: doctorId,
      status:    { $ne: "cancelled" },
      start:     { $lt: end },
      end:       { $gt: start }
    }, { session });
    if (overlap) throw new Error("Doctor unavailable at requested time");

    await db.collection("appointments").insertOne({
      _id: new ObjectId(),
      patient_id: patientId, doctor_id: doctorId,
      start, end, status: "scheduled",
      created_at: new Date()
    }, { session });

    await db.collection("audit_log").insertOne({
      action:     "appointment.create",
      actor_id:   currentUser._id,
      patient_id: patientId,
      doctor_id:  doctorId,
      ts:         new Date()
    }, { session });
  });
}

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Queryable EncryptionHIPAA-grade encryption; equality + range still workPlaintext — non-compliant; client-decrypt-and-filter — slow
Unique (doctor_id, start)DB-enforced no-double-book at the index levelApp-only check — race condition unless transaction
Snapshot patient name on apptList view doesn’t need a $lookup; survives renamesLive $lookup — slower, fragile if patient deleted
Three collectionsEach grows independently; clean access patternsEmbed appointments inside patients/doctors — bounded array problem
Audit log in transactionEvery PHI access logged atomicallyAsync logging — missed audits on app crash

Production polish: healthcare requires HIPAA compliance: BAAs with MongoDB Atlas, AWS/GCP/Azure, end-to-end encryption (CSFLE / Queryable Encryption), audit trails on every PHI access. Authentication via Auth0, Okta, or WorkOS with MFA mandatory. Drata/Vanta/Secureframe automate compliance evidence collection. For real clinical scheduling, integrate with EHRs via FHIR APIs (Redox, 1up Health); reminder/notification workflows via Twilio SMS, SendGrid/Postmark email; telehealth via Twilio Video, Daily.co, or Zoom Healthcare. Calendar sync needs Google Calendar API / Microsoft Graph; consent management via OneTrust. Backups: point-in-time restore via Atlas Backup with retention matching HIPAA’s 6-year minimum.

Question 33

How would you handle sharding in MongoDB to distribute data across multiple servers?

Situation: a single-replica-set deployment hits the wall — working set exceeds RAM, write throughput saturates the primary, or storage hits cluster limits. The collection needs to be split across multiple shards.

Approach: pick a shard key that distributes writes evenly AND matches the dominant read patterns. Pre-split chunks before bulk loads. Plan ahead: a bad shard key is hard to fix and may need reshardCollection.

// Enable sharding for the database
sh.enableSharding("ecommerce");

// BAD shard keys
//  - { _id: 1 }              all-new writes hit the highest chunk — hot shard
//  - { created_at: 1 }       same; monotonically increasing = single hot shard
//  - { country: 1 }          low cardinality — few chunks, can’t balance

// GOOD shard keys (5.0+ supports compound hashed)
sh.shardCollection("ecommerce.orders", { user_id: "hashed" });          // even by user
sh.shardCollection("ecommerce.events", { tenant_id: 1, ts: 1 });        // co-locate per tenant
sh.shardCollection("ecommerce.posts",  { author_id: "hashed", _id: 1 });// hashed prefix for distribution

// Pre-split for bulk loads
sh.splitAt("ecommerce.orders", { user_id: NumberLong("1000000000") });
sh.splitAt("ecommerce.orders", { user_id: NumberLong("2000000000") });

// Query patterns — targeted vs scatter-gather
//   Targeted (uses shard key)
db.orders.find({ user_id: ObjectId("...") });   // hits 1 shard

//   Scatter-gather (no shard key in filter)
db.orders.find({ status: "paid" });              // hits ALL shards — expensive

// Reshard if you picked badly (5.0+) — runs online
db.adminCommand({
  reshardCollection: "ecommerce.orders",
  key: { user_id: "hashed" }
});
// Monitor:
db.adminCommand({ currentOp: 1, "command.reshardCollection": { $exists: true } });

// Zone sharding (5.0+) — pin data to regions for compliance
sh.addShardTag("shard-eu", "EU");
sh.addShardTag("shard-us", "US");
sh.updateZoneKeyRange("ecommerce.users",
  { region: "EU", _id: MinKey }, { region: "EU", _id: MaxKey }, "EU");
sh.shardCollection("ecommerce.users", { region: 1, _id: 1 });

// Inspect distribution
db.orders.getShardDistribution();
sh.status();

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Hashed shard keyEven distribution even on monotonic IDsRange — intuitive but hot-shards on time-ordered inserts
Compound (tenant_id, ts)Per-tenant queries are targeted to one shardSingle-field key — cross-tenant queries scatter-gather
Pre-split before bulk loadAvoids initial-load hot-shard while balancer catches upAuto-split — first load floods one shard
Filter on shard key in queriesTargeted queries hit one shard; sub-100msNo shard key — scatter-gather; latency multiplied
Zone sharding for complianceGDPR data stays in EU shards physicallyApp-level partition — risk of misrouted writes

Production polish: sharding is a one-way decision — un-sharding is hard. Always benchmark first: profile your current bottleneck (storage, RAM, write throughput, network) and confirm sharding solves it. Often the answer is vertical scaling, better indexes, or archiving cold data via Atlas Online Archive. Use MongoDB Atlas for managed sharded clusters — balancer tuning, chunk migration, and config-server quorum are all hands-off. Self-hosted: MongoDB Kubernetes Operator handles config servers and mongos routing. Watch for jumbo chunks (chunks too big to migrate) — they pin a shard at high storage and require manual splits or shard-key fixes. Cluster-to-Cluster Sync migrates data between clusters with shard key changes. Atlas Performance Advisor recommends compound shard keys based on observed query patterns.

Question 34

Explain how you would use MongoDB change streams to track changes in a collection.

Situation: the app needs to react to changes — refresh a cache, trigger a workflow, fan out to webhooks, push to a search index, or sync to a warehouse. Polling is wasteful; tailing the oplog directly is fragile.

Approach: use change streams — the official, replica-set-aware tail of the oplog with resume tokens, schema-friendly events, and pipeline filtering. Persist the resume token after each batch so a restart picks up where it left off.

// Watch a single collection (resumable)
const stream = db.collection("orders").watch([
  { $match: { operationType: { $in: ["insert", "update"] } } },
  { $match: { "fullDocument.status": "paid" } }   // pre/post-image filter
], {
  fullDocument:               "updateLookup",        // include current doc
  fullDocumentBeforeChange:   "whenAvailable",       // include pre-image (6.0+)
  resumeAfter:                lastSavedToken         // restart from here
});

for await (const event of stream) {
  await handleOrderPaid(event.fullDocument);
  await saveResumeToken(event._id);                   // persist after success
}

// Configure pre/post-images on the collection (6.0+)
db.runCommand({
  collMod: "orders",
  changeStreamPreAndPostImages: { enabled: true }
});

// Watch the entire database
const dbStream = db.watch();

// Watch the entire deployment
const allStream = client.watch();

// Aggregation in the pipeline — reshape events
const stream2 = db.collection("orders").watch([
  { $match: { operationType: "update" } },
  { $project: {
      orderId:  "$documentKey._id",
      ts:       "$clusterTime",
      changed:  "$updateDescription.updatedFields"
  }}
]);

// Resume token storage pattern
async function saveResumeToken(token) {
  await db.collection("change_stream_state").updateOne(
    { _id: "orders_processor" },
    { $set: { resume_token: token, updated_at: new Date() } },
    { upsert: true }
  );
}
async function loadResumeToken() {
  const state = await db.collection("change_stream_state").findOne({ _id: "orders_processor" });
  return state?.resume_token;
}

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Change stream over pollSub-second latency; no extra DB loadPolling — misses fast updates; constant index scans
Persist resume tokenResume after consumer restart without missing eventsRe-tail from now — loses events during downtime
fullDocument: "updateLookup"Get the current doc, not just the diffDiff only — consumer must $lookup itself
Pre-image with whenAvailableCompute deltas (e.g., status changed from X to Y)No pre-image — can’t detect “changed FROM” conditions
Pipeline filter on streamServer-side filter cuts wire trafficClient-side filter — consumer wastes CPU on noise

Production polish: change streams require a replica set — standalone deployments can’t emit them. The oplog window must exceed your worst-case consumer downtime — size oplogSize for a 24-48h buffer at peak write rates. For high-throughput downstream pipelines, use the Debezium MongoDB connector to stream into Kafka, then fan out to consumers; or Atlas Triggers (managed Lambda-style functions). Kafka topics buffer minutes-to-days of events with strong delivery guarantees, eliminating the “consumer slow, oplog rolled over” risk. For cache invalidation, drive Redis/Dragonfly/KeyDB from the stream. For search index sync, drive Elasticsearch/Atlas Search/Meilisearch. For warehouse sync, route to BigQuery/Snowflake/Databricks via Fivetran, Airbyte, or Estuary. Monitor consumer lag — if events arrive faster than processing, queue depth grows unbounded; alert on lag >30s.

Question 35

You are asked to implement a feature that allows users to upload and retrieve files in MongoDB. How would you design this?

Situation: users upload files (avatars, attachments, exports) and retrieve them later. Files range from 5KB icons to 200MB documents. The system must scale to terabytes, serve uploads/downloads efficiently, and survive node loss.

Approach: for files <1MB, store in a files collection as BinData (or base64). For files >1MB, use object storage (S3/GCS/R2) with metadata in MongoDB. GridFS is the MongoDB-native middle ground but rarely the right answer in 2026 — object storage wins on cost and throughput.

// Pattern A — small files inline (under ~1MB)
db.files.insertOne({
  _id:        ObjectId(),
  user_id:    ObjectId(),
  name:       "avatar.png",
  mime:       "image/png",
  size_bytes: 12453,
  data:       BinData(0, base64Bytes),       // BSON BinData
  uploaded_at: new Date()
});

// Pattern B (recommended) — metadata in Mongo, blob in object storage
db.files.insertOne({
  _id:         ObjectId(),
  user_id:     ObjectId(),
  name:        "report-2026-q1.pdf",
  mime:        "application/pdf",
  size_bytes:  5_242_880,
  storage:     { provider: "s3", bucket: "app-uploads", key: "reports/abc123.pdf" },
  checksum:    "sha256:abc...",
  visibility:  "private",
  uploaded_at: new Date()
});
db.files.createIndex({ user_id: 1, uploaded_at: -1 });

// Direct upload via presigned URL — client-to-S3, no app server bottleneck
async function getUploadUrl(userId, filename, mime) {
  const key = `users/${userId}/${crypto.randomUUID()}-${filename}`;
  const url = await s3.getSignedUrl("putObject", {
    Bucket: "app-uploads",
    Key: key,
    ContentType: mime,
    Expires: 300                           // 5-minute URL
  });
  return { upload_url: url, key };
}

// After upload, client confirms; app records metadata
await db.collection("files").insertOne({
  user_id, name: filename, mime,
  storage: { provider: "s3", bucket: "app-uploads", key },
  uploaded_at: new Date()
});

// Download via presigned URL too — same approach in reverse
async function getDownloadUrl(fileId, requestor) {
  const f = await db.collection("files").findOne({ _id: fileId });
  if (!canRead(f, requestor)) throw new Error("Forbidden");
  return s3.getSignedUrl("getObject", {
    Bucket: f.storage.bucket,
    Key:    f.storage.key,
    Expires: 60
  });
}

// Pattern C — GridFS (only when you need DB-only deployment)
const bucket = new GridFSBucket(db, { bucketName: "files" });
fs.createReadStream("./report.pdf").pipe(
  bucket.openUploadStream("report.pdf", { metadata: { user_id } })
);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Object storage + Mongo metadataCheap, scales to PB, CDN-friendlyBinData/GridFS — bloats DB; slow restores
Presigned URLsApp server isn’t in the upload/download pathStream through app — bandwidth cap; slower
Checksum on metadataDetects corruption; idempotent re-uploadTrust storage — bit rot is rare but real
Short-lived presigned URLsLimits exposure if URL leaksLong-lived — harder to revoke
GridFS only when single-systemAvoids extra deps for tiny deploymentsAlways GridFS — more expensive than S3 above ~10GB

Production polish: use Cloudflare R2 (no egress fees), AWS S3, GCP Cloud Storage, or Backblaze B2 for object storage. Front with CloudFront, Cloudflare CDN, or Bunny.net for global download speed. Image processing on the fly via Cloudinary, imgix, or Cloudflare Images; videos via Mux, Cloudflare Stream, Bunny Stream. Virus scanning before allowing access: ClamAV, VirusTotal, or AWS GuardDuty Malware Protection. EXIF scrubbing for user uploads to protect privacy. Encryption: enable SSE-S3 (default), or SSE-KMS for per-tenant keys. Lifecycle rules archive cold files to Glacier/Coldline. For HIPAA/PCI workloads, S3 Object Lock (WORM) provides immutable audit-grade storage. Direct upload SDKs: Uppy, FilePond, tus for resumable uploads of large files.

Question 36

Describe how you would design a schema for a music streaming service with users, playlists, and songs.

Situation: a music streaming app needs users, playlists, songs (with artists/albums), and play history. Heavy reads on browse, profile, and playlist pages; writes concentrate on play events and playlist edits.

Approach: four primary collections with strategic denormalization. Songs reference artists/albums by ID but cache common display fields. Playlists embed song refs (capped at thousands) with snapshot metadata. Play events go to a separate time-series collection.

// Songs — canonical track metadata
db.songs.insertOne({
  _id:           ObjectId(),
  title:         "Never Gonna Give You Up",
  artist_id:     ObjectId(),
  artist_name:   "Rick Astley",                 // denormalized for display
  album_id:      ObjectId(),
  album_name:    "Whenever You Need Somebody",  // denormalized
  duration_sec:  213,
  isrc:          "GBARL8703001",                // industry standard
  storage:       { provider: "s3", key: "tracks/abc.flac" },
  preview_url:   "https://cdn.app/preview/abc.mp3",
  release_date:  ISODate("1987-07-27"),
  genres:        ["pop", "synth-pop"],
  popularity:    87
});
db.songs.createIndex({ artist_id: 1, release_date: -1 });
db.songs.createIndex({ title: "text", artist_name: "text" });
db.songs.createIndex({ popularity: -1 });

// Artists
db.artists.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  name: "Rick Astley",
  bio: "British singer...",
  monthly_listeners: 12_000_000,
  image_url: "https://cdn.app/artists/abc.jpg"
});

// Playlists — embed song refs with snapshot
db.playlists.insertOne({
  _id:    ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId(),
  name:   "Workout Mix",
  description: "...",
  visibility: "public",                // public | private | unlisted
  cover_url:  "https://cdn.app/playlists/abc.jpg",
  song_count: 42,                      // denormalized count
  duration_sec: 9_840,                 // denormalized total
  songs: [
    {
      song_id:     ObjectId(),
      title:       "Never Gonna Give You Up",  // snapshot
      artist_name: "Rick Astley",              // snapshot
      duration_sec: 213,
      added_at:    new Date()
    }
  ],
  follower_count: 0,
  created_at: new Date(),
  updated_at: new Date()
});
db.playlists.createIndex({ user_id: 1, updated_at: -1 });
db.playlists.createIndex({ visibility: 1, follower_count: -1 });

// Add song to playlist (atomic)
db.playlists.updateOne(
  { _id: playlistId, user_id: userId },
  {
    $push: { songs: songSnapshot },
    $inc:  { song_count: 1, duration_sec: songSnapshot.duration_sec },
    $set:  { updated_at: new Date() }
  }
);

// Play events — time-series
db.createCollection("plays", {
  timeseries: { timeField: "ts", metaField: "user_id", granularity: "minutes" },
  expireAfterSeconds: 90 * 24 * 3600         // keep 90 days for analytics
});
db.plays.insertOne({
  ts:        new Date(),
  user_id:   ObjectId(),
  song_id:   ObjectId(),
  artist_id: ObjectId(),
  context:   { type: "playlist", id: ObjectId() },
  duration_listened_sec: 187,
  completed: false
});

// Recently played (use most recent N)
db.plays.find({ user_id: userId }).sort({ ts: -1 }).limit(50);

// Top songs this week
db.plays.aggregate([
  { $match: { ts: { $gte: thisWeekStart } } },
  { $group: { _id: "$song_id", plays: { $sum: 1 } } },
  { $sort:  { plays: -1 } },
  { $limit: 100 }
]);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Embed song snapshots in playlistRender playlist in one read; survives song deletion$lookup on every render — slow at scale
Denormalize artist_name on songSearch/list views skip the artist joinAlways join — expensive on large feeds
Time-series for plays5x compression; fast time-bucketed analyticsRegular collection — bloats, slow scans
Cap playlist size (e.g., 10k songs)Embedded array stays manageableUnbounded — doc-size limit eventually hit
Drift via periodic resyncSnapshots stay reasonably fresh on artist renameReal-time sync — expensive, often unnecessary

Production polish: audio storage in Cloudflare R2/S3; transcoding via AWS MediaConvert, Mux, or Cloudflare Stream. Adaptive bitrate streaming via HLS or DASH; DRM via Widevine/FairPlay/PlayReady. CDN essential — Cloudflare, Fastly, BunnyCDN. Search beyond text: MongoDB Atlas Search with synonyms (Beyoncé ↔ Béyoncé), or Algolia/Elasticsearch. Recommendations: collaborative filtering via Apache Spark/Snowflake ML; vector embeddings of audio in Atlas Vector Search, Pinecone, or Qdrant. Music identification: Shazam API, ACRCloud. Royalty tracking: every play event must persist for licensing audits — archive to S3 + Athena or BigQuery. Real-time “listening together” / live-sync features via Pusher, Ably, or Liveblocks. Industry-standard metadata sources: MusicBrainz, Spotify API, Apple Music API.

Question 37

How would you handle real-time collaboration features, such as shared document editing, in MongoDB?

Situation: users edit a shared document simultaneously — Google-Docs-style. Edits must merge without conflicts, sub-100ms latency, with cursor presence and history.

Approach: MongoDB stores the document state and operation history; the live merge happens in a CRDT/OT layer like Yjs or Automerge. WebSockets handle the realtime channel. MongoDB persists periodic snapshots and the operation log for replay.

// Documents — one per editable artifact
db.docs.insertOne({
  _id:        ObjectId(),
  title:      "Q4 Plan",
  owner_id:   ObjectId(),
  collaborators: [
    { user_id: ObjectId(), role: "editor" },
    { user_id: ObjectId(), role: "viewer" }
  ],
  state_blob: BinData(0, yjsStateAsBytes),    // CRDT state snapshot
  state_vector: BinData(0, yjsVectorAsBytes), // for diff sync
  version:    142,                             // monotonic
  updated_at: new Date()
});
db.docs.createIndex({ owner_id: 1, updated_at: -1 });

// Operations log — capped collection or time-series
db.createCollection("doc_ops", {
  timeseries: { timeField: "ts", metaField: "doc_id", granularity: "seconds" },
  expireAfterSeconds: 30 * 24 * 3600
});
db.doc_ops.insertOne({
  ts:      new Date(),
  doc_id:  ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId(),
  op:      BinData(0, yjsUpdateBytes),    // single CRDT update
  version_after: 143
});

// Snapshot the CRDT state periodically (every 100 ops or 1 minute)
async function snapshot(docId) {
  const ydoc = await loadFromOps(docId);
  const state = Y.encodeStateAsUpdate(ydoc);
  await db.collection("docs").updateOne(
    { _id: docId },
    { $set: { state_blob: state, version: ydoc.version, updated_at: new Date() } }
  );
}

// Presence — ephemeral; never persist
// Use Pusher / Ably / Liveblocks / your own WebSocket gateway

// Permission check before applying any op (server-side)
async function applyOp(docId, userId, opBytes) {
  const doc = await db.collection("docs").findOne({
    _id: docId,
    "collaborators.user_id": userId,
    "collaborators.role":    { $in: ["editor", "owner"] }
  });
  if (!doc) throw new Error("Forbidden");

  await db.collection("doc_ops").insertOne({
    ts: new Date(), doc_id: docId, user_id: userId,
    op: opBytes, version_after: doc.version + 1
  });

  await db.collection("docs").updateOne(
    { _id: docId, version: doc.version },
    { $inc: { version: 1 }, $set: { updated_at: new Date() } }
  );
  // Broadcast to other connected clients via WebSocket
}

// Recover doc state on load: snapshot + replay ops since version
async function loadDoc(docId) {
  const doc = await db.collection("docs").findOne({ _id: docId });
  const ydoc = new Y.Doc();
  Y.applyUpdate(ydoc, doc.state_blob);
  const opsSince = await db.collection("doc_ops").find({
    doc_id: docId, version_after: { $gt: doc.version }
  }).sort({ ts: 1 }).toArray();
  for (const o of opsSince) Y.applyUpdate(ydoc, o.op);
  return ydoc;
}

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
CRDT (Yjs/Automerge) for mergeMathematically conflict-free; offline-friendlyOT — complex server logic; Server-side merge — not offline-tolerant
Mongo for state + opsDurable storage; query historyPure in-memory — lost on crash; no audit
Snapshots + op logFast load; full history if neededReplay all ops — slow for long-lived docs
Time-series doc_opsCompressed, auto-bucketed, TTL purgeRegular collection — bigger, no built-in retention
Permission check on every opServer enforces access; client can’t bypassClient-side only — trivially exploitable

Production polish: use Liveblocks, Yjs + y-websocket, PartyKit, or Convex as the realtime + CRDT layer; MongoDB stores the durable record. Hocuspocus is a server framework specifically for Yjs persistence. WebSocket transport via Pusher, Ably, Cloudflare Durable Objects, or Soketi. Presence cursors / typing indicators are ephemeral — don’t persist them; use Redis or a memory-only channel. Conflict-free editing implies idempotent application of ops; ensure your op format includes a unique ID for dedup. Version control / branch + merge: Automerge shines; Yjs is excellent for plain-text and structured docs. Rich-text editors: TipTap, Lexical, BlockNote, ProseMirror all integrate with Yjs. AI assistance (suggest edits): stream completions from Anthropic Claude, OpenAI, or Mistral against the current doc state — never apply directly, always confirm. For 50+ concurrent editors per doc, scale WebSocket gateways horizontally with Redis Pub/Sub or NATS as the inter-server channel.

Question 38

Explain how you would implement role-based access control in a MongoDB application.

Situation: a multi-tenant SaaS app needs RBAC — users belong to organizations with roles (admin, editor, viewer), permissions cascade from role to action, and resources have owners. The check must run on every request in <5ms.

Approach: store roles as named documents with explicit permission lists. User membership is denormalized onto the user record for fast lookup. Resource ownership is checked separately. The permission check is a single doc lookup — no joins on the hot path.

// roles — small, mostly static
db.roles.insertOne({
  _id:         "admin",
  description: "Full org control",
  permissions: [
    "user.invite", "user.remove", "user.view",
    "billing.view", "billing.update",
    "doc.create", "doc.update", "doc.delete", "doc.view",
    "settings.update"
  ]
});
db.roles.insertOne({
  _id: "editor",
  permissions: ["doc.create", "doc.update", "doc.view", "user.view"]
});
db.roles.insertOne({
  _id: "viewer",
  permissions: ["doc.view", "user.view"]
});

// users — embed memberships for fast permission checks
db.users.insertOne({
  _id:         ObjectId(),
  email:       "alice@example.com",
  email_verified: true,
  memberships: [
    { org_id: ObjectId("o1"), role: "admin",  joined_at: new Date() },
    { org_id: ObjectId("o2"), role: "editor", joined_at: new Date() }
  ],
  created_at:  new Date()
});
db.users.createIndex({ email: 1 }, { unique: true });
db.users.createIndex({ "memberships.org_id": 1 });

// Permission check — single read of the user’s memberships, then role lookup
async function can(userId, orgId, permission) {
  const user = await db.collection("users").findOne(
    { _id: userId, "memberships.org_id": orgId },
    { projection: { "memberships.$": 1 } }
  );
  if (!user || !user.memberships?.[0]) return false;
  const role = await db.collection("roles").findOne({ _id: user.memberships[0].role });
  return role?.permissions.includes(permission) ?? false;
}

// Cache the role permissions in app memory (roles rarely change)
const ROLE_CACHE = new Map();   // role_id -> Set(permissions)
async function loadRoles() {
  const roles = await db.collection("roles").find().toArray();
  for (const r of roles) ROLE_CACHE.set(r._id, new Set(r.permissions));
}

// Now the permission check is one DB read + one Set lookup — sub-1ms
async function canFast(userId, orgId, permission) {
  const user = await db.collection("users").findOne(
    { _id: userId },
    { projection: { memberships: 1 } }
  );
  const m = user?.memberships?.find(x => x.org_id.equals(orgId));
  if (!m) return false;
  return ROLE_CACHE.get(m.role)?.has(permission) ?? false;
}

// Resource-scoped permission — resource has explicit ACL
db.docs.findOne({
  _id: docId,
  $or: [
    { owner_id: userId },
    { "shared_with.user_id": userId, "shared_with.role": { $in: ["editor", "viewer"] } }
  ]
});

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Roles as named docsOne source of truth; easy to add permissionsHardcoded in app — deployment to change permissions
Memberships embedded on userSingle read for permission checkSeparate memberships collection — extra lookup
App-level role cacheSub-millisecond checks; roles rarely changeDB read every check — latency adds up at scale
Resource ACL alongside RBACOwners and explicit shares without role bloatRoles per resource — explosion of role docs
Separate from authIdentity (who) decoupled from authorization (what)Bundled — harder to swap identity providers

Production polish: for fine-grained authorization (per-document, per-field, conditional rules), evaluate SpiceDB (Google Zanzibar-style), OpenFGA, Cerbos, or Permify — purpose-built authorization services with sub-millisecond checks and policy languages. They make MongoDB the data store and authorization the separate concern. For identity, use Auth0, Okta, WorkOS, Clerk, Stack Auth, or Supabase Auth; integrate via OIDC/SAML for enterprise SSO. Audit every permission decision: who-checked-what-for-which-resource at-what-time, into a separate immutable collection or stream to Datadog/Splunk. For B2B SaaS, expose a SCIM endpoint for enterprise customer’s IdP to provision/deprovision users automatically. Multi-tenancy isolation: ensure every query filters by org_id — consider middleware that enforces this at the data-access layer (Mongoose hooks, Prisma extensions). Test the permission matrix with property-based tests: for every (role, resource, action) combo, assert allow/deny matches the spec.

Question 39

You need to design a schema for a content management system with articles, authors, and categories. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a CMS for a publication needs articles, authors, and categories. Editors draft, schedule, publish. Public site reads must be sub-100ms even at thousands of articles per author. Categories nest 3-4 levels deep.

Approach: three collections. Articles embed author and category snapshots for read-fast rendering. Categories use the array-of-ancestors pattern for cheap subtree queries. Drafts and published versions live in the same collection with a status field.

// Authors
db.authors.insertOne({
  _id:        ObjectId(),
  slug:       "jane-doe",
  name:       "Jane Doe",
  bio:        "Senior writer covering...",
  avatar_url: "https://cdn.app/authors/jane.jpg",
  social:     { twitter: "@janedoe", linkedin: "..." },
  created_at: new Date()
});
db.authors.createIndex({ slug: 1 }, { unique: true });

// Categories — tree via ancestors array
db.categories.insertOne({
  _id:       "tech",
  name:      "Tech",
  slug:      "tech",
  ancestors: [],
  parent:    null
});
db.categories.insertOne({
  _id:       "tech-databases",
  name:      "Databases",
  slug:      "tech/databases",
  ancestors: ["tech"],
  parent:    "tech"
});

// Articles — snapshot author & category for fast read
db.articles.insertOne({
  _id:    ObjectId(),
  slug:   "why-mongodb-rocks",
  title:  "Why MongoDB Rocks in 2026",
  excerpt: "A practical look at modern MongoDB...",
  body:   "...full markdown or HTML body...",
  status: "published",                      // draft | scheduled | published | archived
  author: { _id: ObjectId(), slug: "jane-doe", name: "Jane Doe" },     // snapshot
  categories: [
    { _id: "tech",          slug: "tech",          name: "Tech" },
    { _id: "tech-databases", slug: "tech/databases", name: "Databases" }
  ],
  tags:        ["mongodb", "nosql", "tutorial"],
  cover_image: { url: "https://cdn.app/covers/x.jpg", alt: "..." },
  published_at: new Date(),
  scheduled_at: null,
  word_count:  1850,
  reading_time_min: 7,
  view_count:  0,
  created_at:  new Date(),
  updated_at:  new Date()
});

// Critical indexes for the public site
db.articles.createIndex({ status: 1, published_at: -1 });
db.articles.createIndex({ slug: 1 }, { unique: true });
db.articles.createIndex({ "author._id": 1, published_at: -1 });
db.articles.createIndex({ "categories._id": 1, published_at: -1 });
db.articles.createIndex({ tags: 1, published_at: -1 });
db.articles.createIndex({ title: "text", body: "text", excerpt: "text" });

// Public homepage feed — latest published articles
db.articles.find({ status: "published", published_at: { $lte: new Date() } })
  .sort({ published_at: -1 })
  .limit(20)
  .project({ slug:1, title:1, excerpt:1, "author.name":1, "author.slug":1, cover_image:1, published_at:1 });

// Articles in a category subtree (any descendant)
db.articles.find({
  status: "published",
  "categories._id": { $in: ["tech", "tech-databases", "tech-cloud"] }
}).sort({ published_at: -1 });

// Schedule a publication
db.articles.updateOne(
  { _id: articleId, status: "draft" },
  { $set: { status: "scheduled", scheduled_at: ISODate("2026-05-01T08:00:00Z") } }
);

// Publish-on-time job (every minute)
db.articles.updateMany(
  { status: "scheduled", scheduled_at: { $lte: new Date() } },
  [ { $set: { status: "published", published_at: "$scheduled_at" } } ]
);

// View count — throttle async
db.articles.updateOne(
  { _id: articleId },
  { $inc: { view_count: 1 } },
  { writeConcern: { w: 0 } }                     // fire-and-forget
);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Snapshot author/category in articleList/feed renders without joins$lookup — slower; fragile if author renamed
Same collection for drafts/publishedSingle source; revision is a status flipSeparate drafts collection — double schema, sync bugs
Ancestors array on categoriesOne-shot subtree query$graphLookup — works but slower per request
Slug as queryable + uniquePretty URLs; SEO-friendlyUse _id in URL — ugly, opaque
Periodic snapshot drift correctionAuthor name change refreshed via batch jobSync on every edit — high write amplification
