Production polish: for editorial workflows, use a headless CMS shell — Strapi, Payload CMS, Sanity, Contentful, or Builder.io — many can use MongoDB as their backing store. Render publicly via Next.js with ISR / SSG and revalidate on publish; or Astro for content-heavy sites; deploy to Vercel/Netlify/Cloudflare Pages. Image pipeline: Cloudinary/imgix/Cloudflare Images for responsive variants. Search beyond $text: Atlas Search (BM25, fuzzy, autocomplete), Algolia for instant search UX. Editor: TipTap, Lexical, BlockNote, Notion-like blocks. Comments: Disqus, Hyvor Talk, Giscus. Newsletter: Buttondown, ConvertKit, Mailchimp. Analytics: Plausible, Umami, Fathom; PostHog for product analytics. SEO: structured data (JSON-LD Article schema), sitemap, RSS, OpenGraph; Ahrefs/SEMrush for keyword research.

Question 40

How would you handle large file storage and retrieval in MongoDB?

Situation: the app must store and retrieve files from kilobytes (avatars) up to gigabytes (videos, datasets). Throughput, cost, and operational simplicity all matter.

Approach: the right pattern is rarely “all in MongoDB”. Tier by file size: tiny inline; small-to-medium in object storage with metadata in MongoDB; only use GridFS when the deployment must avoid an external storage dependency.

// Tier 1: tiny files inline (under ~256KB — e.g., avatars, icons)
db.assets.insertOne({
  _id:   ObjectId(),
  name:  "avatar.png",
  mime:  "image/png",
  size:  12453,
  data:  BinData(0, base64Data)
});

// Tier 2 (recommended): metadata in Mongo + blob in object storage
db.files.insertOne({
  _id:        ObjectId(),
  user_id:    ObjectId(),
  name:       "training-dataset.csv",
  mime:       "text/csv",
  size_bytes: 3_500_000_000,                     // 3.5 GB
  storage:    { provider: "s3", bucket: "app-data", key: "datasets/x/train.csv" },
  checksum:   "sha256:...",
  uploaded_at: new Date()
});

// Tier 3: GridFS — ONLY when external storage isn’t available
const bucket = new GridFSBucket(db, { bucketName: "files", chunkSizeBytes: 1024*1024 });

// Upload via streaming
fs.createReadStream("./video.mp4").pipe(
  bucket.openUploadStream("video.mp4", {
    metadata: { user_id: userId, mime: "video/mp4" }
  })
).on("finish", () => console.log("done"));

// Stream download — never load whole file into memory
res.setHeader("Content-Type", "video/mp4");
bucket.openDownloadStreamByName("video.mp4").pipe(res);

// GridFS uses two collections: files.chunks (the binary) and files.files (metadata)
db["files.files"].find({ "metadata.user_id": userId });
db["files.chunks"].count({ files_id: gridFsId });   // chunks per file

// Direct upload pattern with object storage (preferred)
async function getUploadUrl(userId, name, mime) {
  const key = `users/${userId}/${randomUUID()}-${name}`;
  const url = await s3.getSignedUrl("putObject", {
    Bucket: "app-data", Key: key, ContentType: mime, Expires: 600
  });
  return { upload_url: url, key };
}

// Resumable / chunked uploads via tus protocol (large files)
// Server: tusd or tus-node-server; client: Uppy
// On completion, app server records metadata in db.files.

// Multipart upload to S3 directly (built-in, parallel, resumable)
const multipart = await s3.createMultipartUpload({ Bucket, Key }).promise();
// upload parts in parallel; complete with their ETags

// CDN URL for serving
function downloadUrl(file) {
  if (file.storage.provider === "s3") {
    return `https://cdn.app.com/${file.storage.key}`;   // CloudFront-fronted
  }
}

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Object storage over GridFSCheaper per GB; CDN-friendly; faster restoresGridFS — bloats DB; slow backup/restore
Streaming reads/writesBounded memory; works on huge filesRead whole file — OOM on multi-GB
Direct upload to S3App server isn’t in the bandwidth pathStream through app — bottleneck; egress cost
Checksum on metadataDetect corruption; idempotent re-uploadTrust storage — bit rot real but rare
Resumable for large files3GB upload doesn’t restart on flaky networkSingle PUT — user retries from byte zero

Production polish: use Cloudflare R2 (zero egress fees), AWS S3, GCP Cloud Storage, Backblaze B2, or MinIO (self-hosted S3-compatible) for blob storage. Front with CloudFront, Cloudflare CDN, Fastly, or Bunny.net for global delivery. Image pipelines: Cloudinary, imgix, Cloudflare Images for resize/format/quality on demand. Video: Mux, Cloudflare Stream, Bunny Stream, Bitmovin for transcoding + adaptive streaming + DRM. Resumable uploads: Uppy + tus; or use S3 multipart upload directly. Lifecycle: archive cold files to S3 Glacier/GCP Coldline after 90 days; S3 Object Lock for WORM/compliance retention. Virus scanning: ClamAV, VirusTotal, AWS GuardDuty Malware Protection. EXIF/metadata scrubbing: do it on upload to prevent privacy leaks. Encryption: SSE-S3 by default; SSE-KMS for per-tenant keys; client-side encryption for zero-trust.

Question 41

Describe how you would implement a schema for a customer support system with tickets, agents, and responses.

Situation: a customer support system has tickets, agents, and conversations. SLAs by priority, ownership transfers, customer + agent visibility, full conversation history, attachments. Bursty load when an outage hits.

Approach: tickets are the parent doc with embedded message thread (capped) plus a separate messages collection for unbounded history. Agents have status (online/offline/busy). Routing assigns tickets via priority + workload. SLA timers are computed from event timestamps.

// Tickets
db.tickets.insertOne({
  _id:           ObjectId(),
  number:        "T-10042",                    // human-readable
  customer_id:   ObjectId(),
  customer_snapshot: { email: "alice@example.com", name: "Alice" },
  subject:       "Cannot log in",
  description:   "Receives 'invalid credentials' but password is correct...",
  priority:      "high",                       // low | normal | high | urgent
  status:        "open",                       // open | pending | resolved | closed
  category:      "auth",
  assigned_to:   ObjectId(),                   // agent id, null if unassigned
  team:          "support-tier-1",
  tags:          ["login", "saml"],
  recent_messages: [                           // last ~10 for quick render
    {
      _id:       ObjectId(),
      author:    { type: "customer", id: ObjectId(), name: "Alice" },
      body:      "Cannot log in...",
      attachments: [],
      ts:        new Date()
    }
  ],
  message_count: 1,
  first_response_at: null,                     // for SLA: time to first response
  resolved_at:       null,
  sla:               { first_response_min: 60, resolution_hr: 24 },
  created_at:        new Date(),
  updated_at:        new Date()
});

// Indexes
db.tickets.createIndex({ assigned_to: 1, status: 1, priority: -1, created_at: 1 });
db.tickets.createIndex({ customer_id: 1, created_at: -1 });
db.tickets.createIndex({ status: 1, sla_breach_at: 1 });   // for SLA dashboard
db.tickets.createIndex({ number: 1 }, { unique: true });
db.tickets.createIndex({ subject: "text", description: "text" });

// Messages — full thread, unbounded
db.messages.insertOne({
  _id:        ObjectId(),
  ticket_id:  ObjectId(),
  author:     { type: "agent", id: ObjectId(), name: "Bob" },
  body:       "Have you tried resetting your password?",
  attachments: [{ name: "screenshot.png", url: "..." }],
  internal:   false,                           // true = agent-only note
  ts:         new Date()
});
db.messages.createIndex({ ticket_id: 1, ts: 1 });

// Agents
db.agents.insertOne({
  _id:        ObjectId(),
  user_id:    ObjectId(),
  name:       "Bob",
  email:      "bob@company.com",
  team:       "support-tier-1",
  status:     "online",                        // online | busy | away | offline
  capacity:   10,                              // max active tickets
  active_count: 4,                             // denormalized
  skills:     ["saml", "billing", "general"],
  shift:      "americas",
  available:  true
});
db.agents.createIndex({ team: 1, status: 1, available: 1 });

// Send a customer message: insert + update + maybe SLA timer
async function customerReply(ticketId, body) {
  const session = client.startSession();
  await session.withTransaction(async () => {
    const msg = {
      _id: new ObjectId(), ticket_id: ticketId,
      author: { type: "customer", id: customerId, name: customerName },
      body, attachments: [], ts: new Date()
    };
    await db.collection("messages").insertOne(msg, { session });
    await db.collection("tickets").updateOne(
      { _id: ticketId },
      {
        $push: { recent_messages: { $each: [msg], $slice: -10 } },
        $inc:  { message_count: 1 },
        $set:  { status: "open", updated_at: new Date() }   // re-open if pending
      },
      { session }
    );
  });
}

// Auto-assign on ticket creation (priority + skills + workload)
async function autoAssign(ticket) {
  const candidate = await db.collection("agents").findOneAndUpdate(
    {
      team:      ticket.team,
      status:    "online",
      available: true,
      $expr:     { $lt: ["$active_count", "$capacity"] },
      skills:    { $in: [ticket.category] }
    },
    { $inc: { active_count: 1 } },
    { sort: { active_count: 1 }, returnDocument: "after" }
  );
  if (candidate) {
    await db.collection("tickets").updateOne(
      { _id: ticket._id },
      { $set: { assigned_to: candidate._id, updated_at: new Date() } }
    );
  }
}

// SLA breach detection (every minute)
db.tickets.find({
  status: { $in: ["open", "pending"] },
  first_response_at: null,
  created_at: { $lte: new Date(Date.now() - 60*60*1000) }   // older than SLA
});

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Recent_messages capped at 10Detail page renders in one queryAlways join messages — extra query for every render
Separate messages collectionUnbounded thread doesn’t bloat ticket docEmbed all — doc-size hit; rewrite cost
Snapshot customer name on ticketList view doesn’t need a $lookup$lookup — slow at thousands of tickets per page
active_count on agent (atomic)Routing decision uses indexed counterCount tickets per agent on-the-fly — expensive
SLA dashboard via indexSub-second “tickets at risk” queryScan everything — minutes-long dashboards

Production polish: for enterprise support, integrate with platforms: Zendesk, Intercom, Freshdesk, HubSpot Service Hub, Help Scout, or Plain. AI-assisted triage: classify priority and category via Anthropic Claude, OpenAI, or Cohere; suggest replies based on knowledge base. Live chat: Pusher/Ably for the realtime channel; Crisp/tawk.to as full-stack alternatives. Knowledge base / docs: Notion, GitBook, HelpKit, Helpjuice. Email integration via Postmark, SendGrid, or Resend; parse incoming via Mailgun/Postmark Inbound. CSAT surveys via Typeform, Delighted. Phone/voice: Twilio Flex, Aircall. Status pages: StatusPage.io, BetterStack. Observability of agent productivity: Looker/Metabase/Mode. SLAs need clearly-defined business hours, holidays, and escalation policies — encode as separate sla_policies documents.

Question 42

You are asked to design a database for a financial application with transactions, accounts, and users. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a financial app stores transactions, accounts, and users. Money-in/money-out must be exactly accurate; double-spend must be impossible; full audit trail required; balances visible in real time.

Approach: use the double-entry bookkeeping pattern — every money movement is two entries (debit + credit) summing to zero. Store amounts as integer cents (or Decimal128). All multi-account writes go through a transaction. Balances are derived by aggregating entries — cached but reconciled.

// Users
db.users.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  email: "alice@example.com",
  email_verified: true,
  kyc_status: "verified",
  created_at: new Date()
});

// Accounts — one user can own many (checking, savings, business...)
db.accounts.insertOne({
  _id:          ObjectId(),
  user_id:      ObjectId(),
  type:         "checking",                      // checking | savings | merchant
  currency:     "USD",
  balance_cents: 250_000,                        // CACHED — reconciled nightly
  pending_cents: 0,                              // authorized but not settled
  status:       "active",                        // active | frozen | closed
  account_number_last4: "1234",
  created_at:   new Date()
});
db.accounts.createIndex({ user_id: 1 });
db.accounts.createIndex({ status: 1, type: 1 });

// Entries (immutable, append-only)
db.entries.insertOne({
  _id:           ObjectId(),
  transaction_id: ObjectId(),                    // groups debit + credit
  account_id:    ObjectId(),
  direction:     "debit",                        // debit | credit
  amount_cents:  10_000,
  currency:      "USD",
  description:   "Transfer to Bob",
  ts:            new Date()
});
db.entries.createIndex({ account_id: 1, ts: -1 });
db.entries.createIndex({ transaction_id: 1 });

// Transactions — metadata about the money movement
db.transactions.insertOne({
  _id:        ObjectId(),
  type:       "transfer",                        // transfer | deposit | withdrawal | payment
  status:     "completed",                       // pending | completed | reversed
  from_account_id: ObjectId(),
  to_account_id:   ObjectId(),
  amount_cents:    10_000,
  currency:        "USD",
  description:     "Transfer to Bob",
  external_ref:    "stripe_pi_123abc",           // payment processor reference
  created_at:      new Date()
});

// Atomic transfer with double-entry — uses a transaction
async function transfer(fromId, toId, amountCents, description) {
  const session = client.startSession();
  let txnId;
  await session.withTransaction(async () => {
    txnId = new ObjectId();

    // Re-read source balance under transaction snapshot
    const from = await db.collection("accounts").findOne({ _id: fromId }, { session });
    if (from.balance_cents - from.pending_cents < amountCents) {
      throw new Error("Insufficient funds");
    }
    if (from.status !== "active") throw new Error("Account not active");

    // Two entries (debit source, credit destination)
    await db.collection("entries").insertMany([
      { transaction_id: txnId, account_id: fromId, direction: "debit",
        amount_cents: amountCents, currency: "USD", description, ts: new Date() },
      { transaction_id: txnId, account_id: toId,   direction: "credit",
        amount_cents: amountCents, currency: "USD", description, ts: new Date() }
    ], { session });

    // Update cached balances atomically
    await db.collection("accounts").updateOne(
      { _id: fromId }, { $inc: { balance_cents: -amountCents } }, { session }
    );
    await db.collection("accounts").updateOne(
      { _id: toId },   { $inc: { balance_cents:  amountCents } }, { session }
    );

    await db.collection("transactions").insertOne({
      _id: txnId, type: "transfer", status: "completed",
      from_account_id: fromId, to_account_id: toId,
      amount_cents: amountCents, currency: "USD",
      description, created_at: new Date()
    }, { session });
  }, { readConcern: { level: "snapshot" }, writeConcern: { w: "majority" } });
  return txnId;
}

// Reconcile balance from entries (nightly drift correction)
db.entries.aggregate([
  { $match: { account_id: accountId } },
  { $group: {
      _id: null,
      total_cents: {
        $sum: { $cond: [{ $eq: ["$direction", "credit"] }, "$amount_cents", { $multiply: ["$amount_cents", -1] }] }
      }
  }}
]);

// Statement (transaction history) for an account
db.entries.find({ account_id: accountId })
  .sort({ ts: -1 })
  .limit(50);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Double-entry ledgerInviolable accounting equation; auditableSingle-entry — no integrity check; corruption silent
Integer cents (or Decimal128)No floating-point drift; 0.1 + 0.2 bugs banishedFloat — precision loss accumulates over time
Cached balance + ledger truthFast read; entries are source of truthCompute from entries on every read — slow
Multi-doc transactionAtomic balance update + entries + transactionBest-effort writes — partial state on crash
Append-only entriesMutations are new entries (reversals)Update entries — loses audit trail

Production polish: dedicated ledger systems for serious money: Tigerbeetle (purpose-built for high-throughput financial txns), Modern Treasury, Increase, Unit; payment processors via Stripe, Adyen, Plaid (banking + ACH), Wise (FX). Compliance: KYC/AML via Persona, Onfido, Sumsub, Alloy; sanctions screening via ComplyAdvantage; transaction monitoring via Hummingbird, Sardine. Fraud detection: Stripe Radar, Sift, Forter. SOC 2 + PCI-DSS required; Drata/Vanta/Secureframe for compliance automation. Queryable Encryption 7.0+ for sensitive PII (SSN, account numbers). Atlas Backup with point-in-time restore for 7+ year regulatory retention. Reporting: Looker/Metabase/Cube over the warehouse copy; daily reconciliation against bank/processor ledgers; alerts on cents-off mismatches via Datadog. Idempotency keys on every transfer endpoint to defeat double-clicks and retries.

Question 43

How would you implement a feature that tracks changes to documents and maintains a history of revisions in MongoDB?

Situation: documents (contracts, articles, configs) need full revision history — who changed what when, undo to any prior version, audit trail. Edits are frequent; storage cost matters.

Approach: the document holds the current state; a separate revisions collection holds the history. Each revision stores either a full snapshot (cheap, fast) or a compact diff (cheap storage, slower restore). Hybrid pattern: snapshot every N edits, diffs in between.

// Current state
db.documents.insertOne({
  _id:           ObjectId(),
  title:         "Contract draft",
  body:          "Lorem ipsum...",
  fields:        { client: "Acme", value: 50000 },
  current_version: 7,                            // latest revision number
  updated_by:    ObjectId(),
  updated_at:    new Date(),
  created_at:    new Date()
});

// Revisions — one entry per edit
db.revisions.insertOne({
  _id:         ObjectId(),
  document_id: ObjectId(),
  version:     7,                                // monotonic per doc
  type:        "snapshot",                       // snapshot | diff
  // For snapshot:
  state: {
    title: "Contract draft",
    body:  "Lorem ipsum...",
    fields: { client: "Acme", value: 50000 }
  },
  // For diff (alternative form): use JSON Patch or similar
  // patches: [
  //   { op: "replace", path: "/fields/value", value: 60000, prev: 50000 },
  //   { op: "add",     path: "/fields/notes", value: "Updated by client" }
  // ],
  changed_by:  ObjectId(),
  changed_at:  new Date(),
  reason:      "Client requested price increase"
});
db.revisions.createIndex({ document_id: 1, version: -1 });

// Edit + revision in one transaction
async function editDoc(docId, changes, userId, reason) {
  const session = client.startSession();
  await session.withTransaction(async () => {
    const doc = await db.collection("documents").findOne({ _id: docId }, { session });
    const newVersion = doc.current_version + 1;

    // Compute patches (e.g., via fast-json-patch)
    const patches = jsonPatch.compare(doc, { ...doc, ...changes });

    // Snapshot every 10 versions; diff otherwise
    const isSnapshot = newVersion % 10 === 0;
    await db.collection("revisions").insertOne({
      _id: new ObjectId(),
      document_id: docId,
      version:     newVersion,
      type:        isSnapshot ? "snapshot" : "diff",
      ...(isSnapshot ? { state: { ...doc, ...changes } } : { patches }),
      changed_by:  userId,
      changed_at:  new Date(),
      reason
    }, { session });

    await db.collection("documents").updateOne(
      { _id: docId, current_version: doc.current_version },   // optimistic lock
      {
        $set: { ...changes, current_version: newVersion, updated_by: userId, updated_at: new Date() }
      },
      { session }
    );
  });
}

// Restore a specific version
async function getVersion(docId, version) {
  // Find nearest snapshot at or before requested version
  const snap = await db.collection("revisions").findOne(
    { document_id: docId, type: "snapshot", version: { $lte: version } },
    { sort: { version: -1 } }
  );
  let state = snap.state;

  // Apply diffs from snapshot+1 up to version
  const diffs = await db.collection("revisions").find({
    document_id: docId, type: "diff",
    version: { $gt: snap.version, $lte: version }
  }).sort({ version: 1 }).toArray();

  for (const d of diffs) state = jsonPatch.applyPatch(state, d.patches).newDocument;
  return state;
}

// Audit query — who edited what, when
db.revisions.find({ document_id: docId })
  .sort({ version: -1 })
  .limit(50)
  .project({ version: 1, changed_by: 1, changed_at: 1, reason: 1, type: 1 });

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Hybrid snapshot+diffBounded restore time + bounded storageAll snapshots — storage explosion; All diffs — slow restore
Separate revisions collectionDocument doesn’t grow unboundedEmbedded array — doc-size limit, slow updates
Optimistic version lockConcurrent edits detected (no lost updates)No lock — later edit silently overwrites earlier
JSON Patch diffsStandard format; portable; reversibleCustom diff — reinventing the wheel
Transaction for doc + revisionEither both saved or neitherSequential writes — partial state on crash

Production polish: for collaborative real-time editing with history, use Yjs or Automerge CRDTs — they preserve operation history natively; persist their state to MongoDB. Liveblocks, Hocuspocus, PartyKit, Convex as managed real-time + history providers. Diff visualization: render side-by-side with diff2html, react-diff-viewer, or Monaco editor’s diff view. For document-style apps, TipTap Pro, Lexical, BlockNote have history built in. Compliance use cases (legal contracts, SOX): immutable storage via S3 Object Lock WORM; signing via DocuSign, Adobe Acrobat Sign, Dropbox Sign. Periodic compaction: every quarter, fold old snapshot+diff sequences into denser snapshots; archive ancient revisions to cold storage. Approval workflows on edits: status transitions (draft → review → approved) tracked via state machine library like XState.

Question 44

Explain how you would use the aggregation framework to generate complex reports in MongoDB.

Situation: reporting needs — revenue by region by product by month, top customers, cohort retention, funnel conversion. The naive approach (read all docs, compute in app) doesn’t scale past tens of thousands.

Approach: use the aggregation pipeline. Filter early ($match), reshape ($project/$addFields), group ($group), join ($lookup), bucket ($bucket), and either return the cursor or materialize via $merge for fast subsequent reads.

// Revenue by region, product, month — classic OLAP cube
db.orders.aggregate([
  // 1. Filter early — only completed orders in date window
  { $match: {
      status: "completed",
      placed_at: { $gte: ISODate("2026-01-01"), $lt: ISODate("2026-04-01") }
  }},

  // 2. Unwind line items — one row per item
  { $unwind: "$items" },

  // 3. Project clean shape
  { $project: {
      month:    { $dateTrunc: { date: "$placed_at", unit: "month" } },
      region:   "$shipping_address.region",
      product_id: "$items.product_id",
      revenue:  { $multiply: ["$items.price_cents", "$items.quantity"] }
  }},

  // 4. Group at the cube grain
  { $group: {
      _id: { month: "$month", region: "$region", product_id: "$product_id" },
      revenue_cents: { $sum: "$revenue" },
      order_count:   { $sum: 1 }
  }},

  // 5. Enrich with product details
  { $lookup: {
      from: "products",
      localField: "_id.product_id",
      foreignField: "_id",
      as: "product"
  }},
  { $unwind: "$product" },

  // 6. Final shape for the report
  { $project: {
      _id:           0,
      month:         "$_id.month",
      region:        "$_id.region",
      product:       "$product.name",
      category:      "$product.category",
      revenue_cents: 1,
      order_count:   1
  }},

  // 7. Sort for the dashboard
  { $sort: { month: 1, region: 1, revenue_cents: -1 } }
]);

// Materialize the report nightly for fast reads
db.orders.aggregate([
  { $match: { status: "completed", placed_at: { $gte: lastNightStart } } },
  { $unwind: "$items" },
  // ... same shape ...
  { $merge: {
      into: "monthly_revenue_cube",
      on:   ["_id"],
      whenMatched:    "replace",
      whenNotMatched: "insert"
  }}
]);
db.monthly_revenue_cube.createIndex({ "_id.month": -1, "_id.region": 1 });

// $facet — multiple summaries in one pass
db.orders.aggregate([
  { $match: { placed_at: { $gte: lastMonth } } },
  { $facet: {
      by_status:  [
        { $group: { _id: "$status", count: { $sum: 1 } } }
      ],
      by_region:  [
        { $group: { _id: "$shipping_address.region", revenue: { $sum: "$total_cents" } } }
      ],
      total: [
        { $group: { _id: null, count: { $sum: 1 }, revenue: { $sum: "$total_cents" } } }
      ]
  }}
]);

// Funnel: signup → first_order → repeat_order
db.events.aggregate([
  { $match: { ts: { $gte: cohortStart, $lt: cohortEnd } } },
  { $group: {
      _id: "$user_id",
      signed_up:    { $max: { $cond: [{ $eq: ["$type", "signup"] }, "$ts", null] } },
      first_order:  { $min: { $cond: [{ $eq: ["$type", "order"] },  "$ts", null] } },
      orders:       { $sum: { $cond: [{ $eq: ["$type", "order"] },  1, 0] } }
  }},
  { $project: {
      stage: {
        $cond: [
          { $gte: ["$orders", 2] }, "repeat",
          { $cond: [{ $ne: ["$first_order", null] }, "first_order", "signup_only"] }
        ]
      }
  }},
  { $group: { _id: "$stage", count: { $sum: 1 } } }
]);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
$match firstUses index; cuts pipeline volume earlyMatch late — processes all docs unnecessarily
$merge for materializationDashboard hits pre-computed; sub-secondLive aggregation — seconds to minutes per query
$facet for multi-summarySingle pass; shared $matchN separate pipelines — redundant data scans
$dateTrunc groupingTimezone+DST correct; clean group keys$year/$month chains — verbose, error-prone
Index on time fieldRange $match is targetedNo index — full collection scan

Production polish: for serious analytics over billions of rows, MongoDB’s aggregation can hit limits — copy data to a warehouse: BigQuery, Snowflake, Databricks, ClickHouse (open-source, blazingly fast for OLAP). ETL via Fivetran, Airbyte, Estuary, MongoDB Atlas Data Federation. Visualization: Atlas Charts (lives on top of MongoDB), Metabase, Superset, Tableau, Looker, Mode, Hex (notebook-driven). Semantic layer: Cube, dbt for shared business metrics. Real-time analytics needs: ClickHouse, Tinybird, Apache Pinot, Druid. Time-series specifically: InfluxDB, TimescaleDB. Always set { allowDiskUse: true } on heavy pipelines — bypasses the 100MB per-stage memory cap. Profile via explain("executionStats") for stages dominated by COLLSCAN. Monitor query duration with Datadog/New Relic; alert on aggregation queries >5s.

Question 45

You need to design a schema for an inventory management system with products, suppliers, and stock levels. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: an inventory app tracks products across multiple warehouses with reorder triggers, supplier relationships, and stock movements. Concurrent receipts and shipments must not overcount or undercount.

Approach: three primary collections. Products own metadata. Stock levels live in a separate collection keyed by (product_id, warehouse_id) for atomic per-warehouse updates. Movements are append-only events. Reorders trigger on threshold breach.

// Products
db.products.insertOne({
  _id:           ObjectId(),
  sku:           "WIDGET-001",
  name:          "Premium Widget",
  description:   "...",
  unit_cost_cents: 1500,
  unit_price_cents: 4900,
  reorder_point: 50,
  reorder_quantity: 200,
  primary_supplier_id: ObjectId(),
  active: true
});
db.products.createIndex({ sku: 1 }, { unique: true });
db.products.createIndex({ active: 1 });

// Suppliers
db.suppliers.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  name: "Acme Manufacturing",
  contact: { email: "orders@acme.com", phone: "+1..." },
  lead_time_days: 14,
  payment_terms: "net_30",
  status: "active"
});

// Stock levels — one doc per (product, warehouse)
db.stock.insertOne({
  _id:          { product_id: ObjectId(), warehouse_id: ObjectId() },
  on_hand:      245,                       // physically present
  reserved:     12,                         // committed to open orders
  on_order:     200,                        // expected from supplier
  available:    233,                        // on_hand - reserved
  reorder_at:   50,
  last_counted: new Date()
});
db.stock.createIndex({ "_id.warehouse_id": 1, available: 1 });
db.stock.createIndex({ "_id.product_id": 1 });
// Note: _id is naturally compound — no extra index needed for composite key lookups

// Stock movements — append only (audit trail)
db.movements.insertOne({
  _id:          ObjectId(),
  product_id:   ObjectId(),
  warehouse_id: ObjectId(),
  type:         "receive",          // receive | ship | adjust | transfer | count
  quantity:     200,                // positive for in, negative for out
  reason:       "PO-12345",
  reference:    { type: "purchase_order", id: ObjectId() },
  performed_by: ObjectId(),
  ts:           new Date()
});
db.movements.createIndex({ product_id: 1, warehouse_id: 1, ts: -1 });

// Atomic stock change — reserve for an order
async function reserveStock(productId, warehouseId, quantity, orderId) {
  const result = await db.collection("stock").updateOne(
    {
      "_id.product_id":    productId,
      "_id.warehouse_id":  warehouseId,
      $expr: { $gte: [{ $subtract: ["$on_hand", "$reserved"] }, quantity] }
    },
    [
      { $set: {
          reserved:  { $add: ["$reserved", quantity] },
          available: { $subtract: ["$on_hand", { $add: ["$reserved", quantity] }] }
      }}
    ]
  );
  if (result.matchedCount === 0) throw new Error("Insufficient stock");

  await db.collection("movements").insertOne({
    product_id: productId, warehouse_id: warehouseId,
    type: "reserve", quantity: -quantity,
    reference: { type: "order", id: orderId },
    ts: new Date()
  });
}

// Receive stock from supplier — transactional with PO update
async function receiveStock(poId, productId, warehouseId, quantity) {
  const session = client.startSession();
  await session.withTransaction(async () => {
    await db.collection("stock").updateOne(
      { "_id.product_id": productId, "_id.warehouse_id": warehouseId },
      [
        { $set: {
            on_hand:   { $add: ["$on_hand", quantity] },
            on_order:  { $max: [0, { $subtract: ["$on_order", quantity] }] },
            available: { $subtract: [{ $add: ["$on_hand", quantity] }, "$reserved"] }
        }}
      ],
      { upsert: true, session }
    );
    await db.collection("movements").insertOne({
      product_id: productId, warehouse_id: warehouseId,
      type: "receive", quantity,
      reference: { type: "purchase_order", id: poId }, ts: new Date()
    }, { session });
    await db.collection("purchase_orders").updateOne(
      { _id: poId },
      { $inc: { received_quantity: quantity }, $set: { last_received_at: new Date() } },
      { session }
    );
  });
}

// Reorder check — daily scan
db.stock.aggregate([
  { $match: { $expr: { $lte: ["$available", "$reorder_at"] } } },
  { $lookup: {
      from: "products",
      localField: "_id.product_id",
      foreignField: "_id",
      as: "product"
  }},
  { $unwind: "$product" },
  { $match: { "product.active": true } }
]);

// Inventory valuation by warehouse
db.stock.aggregate([
  { $lookup: { from: "products", localField: "_id.product_id", foreignField: "_id", as: "p" } },
  { $unwind: "$p" },
  { $group: {
      _id: "$_id.warehouse_id",
      total_units: { $sum: "$on_hand" },
      total_value_cents: { $sum: { $multiply: ["$on_hand", "$p.unit_cost_cents"] } }
  }}
]);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Per-(product, warehouse) docAtomic stock updates without contentionSingle product doc with array of stocks — whole-doc rewrite per change
Movements as append-onlyFull audit trail; reconcile from sourceUpdate stock only — no “why did it change?”
Cached available fieldReorder query indexed on itCompute on-the-fly — can’t use index for thresholds
Conditional update with $exprRace-free “reserve only if enough”Read-then-write — classic concurrency bug
Movement + stock in transactionMovement always reflects actual changeOut-of-band movement log — drift inevitable

Production polish: for retail/B2B at scale, consider purpose-built systems: NetSuite, SAP, Microsoft Dynamics 365, Cin7, Zoho Inventory. Shopify manages e-commerce inventory natively; integrate via API. Demand forecasting / ML reorder predictions: Snowflake ML, BigQuery ML, Databricks, or Lokad. Barcode/RFID for warehouse ops: Square, Zebra, SOTI. Shipping integration: EasyPost, Shippo, ShipStation. Supplier EDI: SPS Commerce, TrueCommerce. Periodic cycle counts reconcile cached on_hand against physical reality — never trust the database alone. Multi-currency requires per-supplier currency fields and FX feeds (OpenExchangeRates, Fixer.io). For perishables / batch tracking, add a lots collection with expiration dates. FIFO/LIFO/FEFO picking strategies depend on product class — encode as warehouse settings.

Question 46

How would you handle data encryption and security for sensitive information in MongoDB?

Situation: an app stores PII (names, emails, SSNs, payment data) and faces GDPR / HIPAA / PCI-DSS / SOC 2 obligations. Encryption at rest, encryption in transit, field-level encryption for sensitive data, audit trails on access, key rotation.

Approach: defense in depth: TLS for transport, encrypted storage volumes, Queryable Encryption (7.0+) for application-level field encryption (still queryable!), strict RBAC, audit logging, and an external KMS for keys.

// Queryable Encryption setup — client-side encryption with server-side queries (7.0+)
const clientEncryption = new ClientEncryption(client, {
  keyVaultNamespace: "encryption.__keyVault",
  kmsProviders: { aws: { accessKeyId: "...", secretAccessKey: "..." } }
});

// Define an encrypted schema — ssn equality, dob range queries supported
const encryptedFields = {
  fields: [
    { keyId: dataKeyId1, path: "ssn",
      bsonType: "string",
      queries: [{ queryType: "equality" }] },
    { keyId: dataKeyId2, path: "dob",
      bsonType: "date",
      queries: [{ queryType: "range", min: ISODate("1900-01-01"), max: ISODate("2100-01-01") }] },
    { keyId: dataKeyId3, path: "salary_cents",
      bsonType: "long" }   // not queryable; only retrievable
  ]
};

// Create the collection with encryption metadata
await db.createCollection("patients", { encryptedFields });

// Insert — values are encrypted client-side automatically
await db.collection("patients").insertOne({
  external_id: "P-001",       // unencrypted, queryable normally
  name:        "Alice Smith",
  ssn:         "123-45-6789", // encrypted, equality-queryable
  dob:         ISODate("1990-05-12"),  // encrypted, range-queryable
  salary_cents: 75000_00      // encrypted, retrievable only
});

// Queries against encrypted fields work like normal
db.patients.find({ ssn: "123-45-6789" });           // index-backed equality
db.patients.find({ dob: { $gte: ISODate("1980-01-01"), $lt: ISODate("2000-01-01") } });

// Database user separation — RBAC
db.createUser({
  user: "app_service",
  pwd:  generatedPassword,
  roles: [
    { role: "readWrite", db: "app" }   // no access to admin/local/config
  ]
});
db.createUser({
  user: "analytics_readonly",
  pwd:  generatedPassword,
  roles: [{ role: "read", db: "app" }]   // only read for BI/reporting
});

// TLS (transport encryption) — required in production
// connection string: mongodb+srv://...?tls=true&tlsCAFile=ca.pem

// Audit logging (Enterprise / Atlas)
//   --setParameter "auditAuthorizationSuccess=true"
//   --auditDestination=file --auditFormat=BSON --auditPath=/var/log/mongo-audit.bson

// Application-side audit log
async function logAccess(userId, action, resource, result) {
  await db.collection("audit_log").insertOne({
    user_id: userId, action, resource, result,
    ip: requestIp, user_agent: requestUa, ts: new Date()
  });
}

// Key rotation pattern (CSFLE / Queryable Encryption supports it)
await clientEncryption.rewrapManyDataKey(
  { keyAltNames: "patient_ssn_key" },
  { provider: "aws", masterKey: { region: "us-east-1", key: newKmsKeyArn } }
);

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Queryable EncryptionEquality + range queries on encrypted fieldsPlaintext — non-compliant; CSFLE (legacy) — equality only
External KMSKeys never on DB host; rotation managedLocal key file — single point of total compromise
Per-service DB usersCompromise of one service doesn’t expose othersOne superuser — total exposure on credential leak
Audit log (DB + app)Compliance evidence; forensics on breachNo audit — non-compliant; can’t prove access patterns
TLS in transitNetwork sniffing yields nothingPlain — unacceptable in any production context

Production polish: use MongoDB Atlas — encryption at rest is automatic, TLS is enforced, KMS integration with AWS KMS, Azure Key Vault, GCP Cloud KMS, or HashiCorp Vault is one click. Compliance frameworks need automated evidence: Drata, Vanta, Secureframe, Sprinto handle SOC 2/HIPAA/ISO/GDPR continuously. Tokenization of PCI data (don’t store raw cards): Stripe, VGS, Basis Theory, Skyflow, CipherStash. PII discovery / DLP: BigID, Privacera, Microsoft Purview. Secret management: HashiCorp Vault, AWS Secrets Manager, Doppler, Infisical. SIEM aggregation: pipe audit logs to Splunk, Datadog, Sumo Logic. Penetration testing annually; Cobalt, Bugcrowd, HackerOne for crowdsourced pentests. Application-side: never log PII (filter via Pino redact, winston formatters); rotate any leaked secrets immediately via runbook automation.

Question 47

Describe how you would implement a recommendation engine that suggests similar products to users based on their browsing history.

Situation: an e-commerce site needs “customers who viewed this also viewed” and “recommended for you”. Real-time enough that browsing history influences the next page; precise enough to drive clicks.

Approach: compute recommendations offline (collaborative filtering) or via vector embeddings (content-based). Store the precomputed top-N for each product / user. Serve from MongoDB at request time — sub-10ms lookup, never on-the-fly model inference for the hot path.

// Browsing history (time-series; powers cohort & individualized recs)
db.createCollection("page_views", {
  timeseries: { timeField: "ts", metaField: "user_id", granularity: "minutes" },
  expireAfterSeconds: 90 * 24 * 3600
});
db.page_views.insertOne({
  ts: new Date(),
  user_id: ObjectId(),
  product_id: ObjectId(),
  session_id: "abc123",
  device: "mobile"
});

// Pattern A — co-view recommendations (item-to-item, collaborative)
// Compute nightly: for each product, top 10 products viewed in same session
db.page_views.aggregate([
  // 1. Pair products viewed in same session (this is simplified)
  { $sort: { session_id: 1, ts: 1 } },
  { $group: { _id: "$session_id", products: { $push: "$product_id" } } },
  { $unwind: "$products" },
  { $unwind: "$products" },     // every pair (lhs, rhs)
  // ... (real impl uses self-join via $lookup or precomputed pairs)

  // 2. Count co-occurrence
  { $group: {
      _id: { product_a: "$products_a", product_b: "$products_b" },
      coview_count: { $sum: 1 }
  }},
  { $match: { coview_count: { $gte: 5 } } },

  // 3. Pick top 10 co-views per product
  { $sort:  { "_id.product_a": 1, coview_count: -1 } },
  { $group: {
      _id: "$_id.product_a",
      top: { $push: { product_id: "$_id.product_b", score: "$coview_count" } }
  }},
  { $project: { top: { $slice: ["$top", 10] } } },

  // 4. Materialize to a fast-read collection
  { $merge: {
      into: "product_recs",
      on:   "_id",
      whenMatched: "replace",
      whenNotMatched: "insert"
  }}
]);

db.product_recs.createIndex({ _id: 1 });

// Serve at request time — one lookup
db.product_recs.findOne({ _id: productId }, { projection: { top: 1 } });
// Then $lookup the top product details (or denormalize them at compute time)

// Pattern B — vector embeddings (Atlas Vector Search) — modern, content-based
// Store an embedding per product (from CLIP / OpenAI / Cohere / Voyage)
db.products.insertOne({
  _id:        ObjectId(),
  name:       "Premium Widget",
  description: "...",
  embedding:  [0.12, -0.45, 0.78, /* 1536 dims for OpenAI ada-002 */],
  category:   "widgets",
  price_cents: 4900
});

// Atlas Vector Search index
db.runCommand({
  createSearchIndexes: "products",
  indexes: [{
    name: "vector_index",
    type: "vectorSearch",
    definition: {
      fields: [{
        type: "vector", path: "embedding",
        numDimensions: 1536, similarity: "cosine"
      }, {
        type: "filter", path: "category"
      }]
    }
  }]
});

// Find similar products via vector search
db.products.aggregate([
  { $vectorSearch: {
      index:         "vector_index",
      path:          "embedding",
      queryVector:   currentProductEmbedding,
      numCandidates: 200,
      limit:         10,
      filter:        { category: "widgets" }   // narrow by metadata
  }},
  { $project: { _id: 1, name: 1, score: { $meta: "vectorSearchScore" } } }
]);

// Personalized recs — combine user’s recent embeddings (avg of viewed)
async function recommendForUser(userId) {
  const recent = await db.page_views.find({ user_id: userId })
    .sort({ ts: -1 }).limit(20).toArray();
  const products = await db.products.find(
    { _id: { $in: recent.map(v => v.product_id) } }
  ).toArray();
  const userVec = avgVectors(products.map(p => p.embedding));

  return db.products.aggregate([
    { $vectorSearch: {
        index: "vector_index", path: "embedding",
        queryVector: userVec, numCandidates: 500, limit: 20
    }}
  ]);
}

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Precompute & cache top-NSub-10ms serving; no hot-path inferenceOn-the-fly compute — CPU cost; high latency
Co-view + vector hybridCold-start handled by content; warm by collaborativeEither alone — misses cold-start or popular-item drift
Atlas Vector SearchOne platform; same auth, same clusterExternal vector DB — another component to operate
Filter inside $vectorSearchPre-filter by category cuts candidate setFilter after — vector search returns wrong category items
Time-series for viewsCompressed; auto TTL purge after 90dRegular collection — bigger, slower analytics

Production polish: generate embeddings via OpenAI text-embedding-3-large, Cohere, Voyage AI, Anthropic embeddings, or open-source via Hugging Face (BGE, E5, GTE families). Pure vector dbs as alternatives: Pinecone, Weaviate, Qdrant, Milvus, Chroma — but Atlas Vector Search keeps everything in one place. ML models for collaborative filtering: Apache Spark ALS, Snowflake ML, Databricks, Vertex AI, Amazon Personalize. Real-time signals (this session’s clicks): combine via Redis short-term memory + Mongo precomputed long-term. Diversity / serendipity matters — otherwise recs collapse to top-sellers; mix in random, exploration items per A/B testing framework like Statsig, LaunchDarkly, Eppo, GrowthBook. Track every recommendation impression and click for offline evaluation; eval metrics: CTR, CVR, NDCG. Compliance: respect opt-out preferences (GDPR Article 22 right to no automated decisions); avoid recommending in sensitive categories (medical, legal) without disclaimers.

Question 48

You need to design a schema for a job portal with employers, job listings, and applicants. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a job portal connects employers, job listings, and applicants. Employers post jobs; candidates apply; recruiters search/filter applicants. Search is the dominant read pattern.

Approach: four collections. Jobs reference employer with cached metadata. Applications join candidates and jobs as a separate collection. Users carry searchable profile data. Atlas Search indexes power keyword + filter + autocomplete.

// Employers
db.employers.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  name: "Acme Corp",
  industry: "software",
  size: "201-500",
  website: "https://acme.com",
  logo_url: "https://cdn.app/logos/acme.png",
  description: "...",
  verified: true,
  status: "active",
  created_at: new Date()
});
db.employers.createIndex({ industry: 1, size: 1 });

// Users (both candidates and recruiters; type field distinguishes)
db.users.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  email: "alice@example.com",
  type:  "candidate",                   // candidate | recruiter
  profile: {
    name:     "Alice Doe",
    headline: "Senior Backend Engineer",
    location: { city: "Berlin", country: "DE" },
    bio:      "10 years building distributed systems...",
    skills:   ["mongodb", "kubernetes", "python", "kafka"],
    experience: [
      { company: "Prev Co", title: "SWE", from: "2018-01", to: "2022-12" }
    ],
    salary_expectation_cents: 110000_00,
    open_to_work: true
  },
  resume: { url: "s3://...", filename: "alice-resume.pdf" },
  created_at: new Date()
});
db.users.createIndex({ email: 1 }, { unique: true });
db.users.createIndex({ type: 1, "profile.open_to_work": 1, "profile.location.city": 1 });

// Jobs — snapshot employer for fast list rendering
db.jobs.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  employer_id: ObjectId(),
  employer_snapshot: { name: "Acme Corp", logo_url: "..." },
  title: "Senior Backend Engineer",
  description: "...",
  requirements: ["5+ years", "MongoDB", "Kubernetes"],
  skills:       ["mongodb", "kubernetes", "python"],
  location: { city: "Berlin", country: "DE", remote: "hybrid" },
  salary: { min_cents: 90000_00, max_cents: 130000_00, currency: "EUR" },
  employment_type: "full_time",
  status: "open",                       // draft | open | filled | expired
  applications_count: 0,
  posted_at: new Date(),
  expires_at: new Date(Date.now() + 30*24*3600*1000)
});
db.jobs.createIndex({ status: 1, posted_at: -1 });
db.jobs.createIndex({ "location.city": 1, status: 1 });
db.jobs.createIndex({ employer_id: 1, status: 1 });
db.jobs.createIndex({ skills: 1, status: 1 });    // multikey

// Atlas Search index for keyword search across multiple fields
db.runCommand({
  createSearchIndexes: "jobs",
  indexes: [{
    name: "jobs_search",
    definition: {
      mappings: {
        dynamic: false,
        fields: {
          title:        { type: "string", analyzer: "lucene.english" },
          description:  { type: "string", analyzer: "lucene.english" },
          skills:       { type: "string" },
          "location.city": { type: "stringFacet" },
          status:       { type: "stringFacet" }
        }
      }
    }
  }]
});

// Applications (candidate ↔ job many-to-many)
db.applications.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  job_id:        ObjectId(),
  candidate_id:  ObjectId(),
  job_snapshot:       { title: "Senior Backend Engineer", employer_name: "Acme Corp" },
  candidate_snapshot: { name: "Alice Doe", email: "alice@example.com" },
  cover_letter: "...",
  resume_url: "s3://...",
  stage: "screening",        // applied | screening | interview | offer | hired | rejected
  status: "active",
  applied_at: new Date(),
  notes: []
});
db.applications.createIndex({ candidate_id: 1, applied_at: -1 });
db.applications.createIndex({ job_id: 1, stage: 1 });
db.applications.createIndex({ job_id: 1, candidate_id: 1 }, { unique: true });   // no double-apply

// Apply — idempotent + counter
async function apply(jobId, candidateId, coverLetter) {
  const session = client.startSession();
  await session.withTransaction(async () => {
    const job = await db.collection("jobs").findOne({ _id: jobId, status: "open" }, { session });
    if (!job) throw new Error("Job not open");

    await db.collection("applications").insertOne({
      _id: new ObjectId(), job_id: jobId, candidate_id: candidateId,
      stage: "applied", status: "active", applied_at: new Date(),
      cover_letter: coverLetter
    }, { session });
    await db.collection("jobs").updateOne(
      { _id: jobId },
      { $inc: { applications_count: 1 } },
      { session }
    );
  });
}

// Search jobs (Atlas Search)
db.jobs.aggregate([
  { $search: {
      index: "jobs_search",
      compound: {
        must:   [{ text: { query: "backend engineer", path: ["title", "description", "skills"] } }],
        filter: [
          { equals: { path: "status",        value: "open" } },
          { equals: { path: "location.city", value: "Berlin" } }
        ]
      }
  }},
  { $limit: 50 }
]);

// Recruiter view: applicants for a job
db.applications.find({ job_id: jobId, stage: { $in: ["applied", "screening"] } })
  .sort({ applied_at: -1 });

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Applications as separate collectionMany-to-many; unbounded growth tolerableEmbed in job/user — arrays explode
Snapshot job + candidate on applyList view doesn’t need joins; survives renames$lookup — slower; fragile if profile deleted
Atlas Search for keyword + facetBM25 ranking, fuzzy, autocompleteRegex on Mongo — slow, no relevance scoring
Unique (job_id, candidate_id)DB enforces no double-applyApp-only check — race conditions
Counter on jobs.applications_countIndexed sort by popularitycountDocuments on every render — expensive

Production polish: integrate ATS systems: Greenhouse, Lever, Ashby, Workable, Recruitee, SmartRecruiters. Resume parsing via Affinda, Sovren, RChilli, or LLM-based: Anthropic Claude/OpenAI structured extraction. Background checks: Checkr, Sterling. Video interview platforms: HireVue, Spark Hire, Willo. Calendar / scheduling: Cal.com, Calendly, integrate via Google Calendar API / Microsoft Graph. Vector search for candidate-job matching using profile embeddings — Atlas Vector Search over skills/experience. Email outreach via Postmark, Resend, SendGrid. Compliance: EEOC reporting, GDPR right-to-be-forgotten implies hard-delete or pseudonymize on candidate request; data retention policies (typical 1-2 year hold post-process). For diversity hiring, blind review hides candidate names/photos until later stages. Verification badges (LinkedIn-style) via Stripe Identity, Persona.

Question 49

How would you handle schema changes and data migrations in MongoDB without downtime?

Situation: a production app needs schema changes — rename fields, restructure embedded docs, change types — without taking the service offline. Customer-facing, no maintenance window allowed.

Approach: backwards-compatible, multi-stage rollout. App writes both shapes; reads handle both shapes; backfill in batches; eventually drop old shape. Each stage is independently deployable and reversible.

// Initial state: documents have first_name + last_name
// Goal: single name field

// === Stage 1: Code that READS both shapes ===
function getName(user) {
  return user.name ?? `${user.first_name} ${user.last_name}`.trim();
}
// Deploy. App tolerant of either shape. No DB writes yet.

// === Stage 2: Code that WRITES both shapes ===
async function updateUser(id, changes) {
  if (changes.name) {
    const [first, ...rest] = changes.name.split(" ");
    changes.first_name = first;
    changes.last_name  = rest.join(" ");
  }
  await db.users.updateOne({ _id: id }, { $set: changes });
}
// Deploy. New writes maintain both fields. Reads can hit either.

// === Stage 3: Backfill old documents ===
//   Run in chunks to avoid replication lag spikes
async function backfillNames() {
  const cursor = db.users.find(
    { name: { $exists: false }, first_name: { $exists: true } },
    { batchSize: 500 }
  );
  let count = 0;
  for await (const doc of cursor) {
    await db.users.updateOne(
      { _id: doc._id },
      [
        { $set: {
            name: { $concat: [{ $ifNull: ["$first_name", ""] }, " ", { $ifNull: ["$last_name", ""] }] },
            schema_version: 2
        }}
      ]
    );
    if (++count % 500 === 0) await sleep(50);   // pace replication
  }
  return count;
}

// === Stage 4: Drop reads of old shape ===
function getName(user) { return user.name; }
// Deploy. App now requires the new field on every doc.

// === Stage 5: Drop writes of old shape ===
async function updateUser(id, changes) {
  await db.users.updateOne({ _id: id }, { $set: changes });
}
// Deploy. No more dual-writes.

// === Stage 6: Cleanup ===
db.users.updateMany(
  { schema_version: 2 },
  { $unset: { first_name: "", last_name: "" } }
);
db.users.dropIndex("first_name_1");
db.users.dropIndex("last_name_1");

// === Pattern: feature flag for safe rollback ===
async function getName(user) {
  if (await flags.isEnabled("use_new_name_field")) {
    return user.name ?? `${user.first_name} ${user.last_name}`;
  }
  return `${user.first_name} ${user.last_name}`;
}

// === Online index builds (4.4+) ===
//  Hybrid build — non-blocking
db.users.createIndex({ name: 1 });   // builds online by default; reads/writes continue

// Watch progress
db.adminCommand({ currentOp: 1, "command.createIndexes": "users" });

// === Schema validation rolled out gradually ===
//  Use validationLevel:"moderate" — validates on inserts and on updates that touch validated fields
//  Use validationAction:"warn" first — logs without rejecting
db.runCommand({
  collMod: "users",
  validator: {
    $jsonSchema: {
      bsonType: "object",
      required: ["email", "name"],
      properties: {
        email: { bsonType: "string" },
        name:  { bsonType: "string", minLength: 1 }
      }
    }
  },
  validationLevel:  "moderate",
  validationAction: "warn"        // change to "error" only after backfill
});

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Multi-stage rolloutEach step reversible; no atomic cutoverBig-bang — if it fails halfway, you’re stuck
Schema_version fieldIdentifies migrated docs; idempotent re-runsNo version — can’t resume after a crash
Validate WARN before ERRORCatches misses without breaking writesERROR immediately — production rejection cascade
Online index builds4.4+ doesn’t lock the collectionForeground build — brief but real lock
Pace via batch+sleepReplication lag stays boundedupdateMany({}) — primary saturated, secondaries fall minutes behind

Production polish: always test the full migration on a restored production snapshot first — staging never has the same edge cases as prod. Use feature flags (LaunchDarkly, Statsig, GrowthBook, Unleash) for instant rollback. blue-green deploys minimize risk: route a small % of traffic to the new code first. Keep a kill switch (a config doc the migration polls every loop) so on-call can stop without redeploying. Monitor: replication lag, oplog window, primary CPU, write latency. Alert when any cross threshold; halt the migration. Log progress to a stats doc ({ phase, total, processed, errors }) for ops visibility. Atlas Migration Service, mongosync, Cluster-to-Cluster Sync for cluster-level moves. Debezium + Kafka for streaming-style migration to a new collection while keeping old in service. Schema validation should arrive AFTER backfill, not before.

Question 50

Explain how you would use MongoDB Atlas to manage and monitor a large-scale MongoDB deployment.

Situation: a production MongoDB deployment grows beyond what hand-managed self-hosted ops can handle — replication, backups, monitoring, security, compliance, capacity planning all demand attention. The team needs a managed service.

Approach: use MongoDB Atlas for the full lifecycle — provisioning, scaling, backups, monitoring, security, search, and analytics. Atlas removes 80% of the ops burden while keeping the same MongoDB API.

// Atlas concepts — managed via UI, Terraform, or Atlas API
//   Project       — logical container (think: environment)
//   Cluster       — replica set or sharded cluster
//   Database User — auth principal
//   Network access— IP allowlist or VPC peering / PrivateLink

// Terraform example for a production cluster
/*
resource "mongodbatlas_cluster" "prod" {
  project_id   = mongodbatlas_project.app.id
  name         = "app-prod"
  cluster_type = "REPLICASET"
  provider_name = "AWS"
  provider_instance_size_name = "M40"
  provider_region_name = "US_EAST_1"
  mongo_db_major_version = "7.0"
  backup_enabled = true
  cloud_backup   = true
  auto_scaling_disk_gb_enabled = true
  auto_scaling_compute_enabled = true
  replication_specs {
    num_shards = 1
    regions_config {
      region_name     = "US_EAST_1"
      electable_nodes = 3
      priority        = 7
      read_only_nodes = 0
    }
  }
}
*/

// Connection — SRV records auto-discover topology
const uri = "mongodb+srv://user:pass@app-prod.abc12.mongodb.net/app?retryWrites=true&w=majority";

// Built-in features:
//   * Continuous Cloud Backup with point-in-time restore (PITR)
//   * Online schema/index changes
//   * Atlas Search (Lucene)
//   * Atlas Vector Search
//   * Atlas Data Federation (query S3, BigQuery, etc. via MongoDB API)
//   * Atlas Online Archive (auto-tier old data to cold storage)
//   * Atlas Stream Processing (Kafka-style streams)
//   * Atlas Triggers (managed Lambda-style functions)
//   * Performance Advisor (auto-index recommendations)
//   * Query Insights (slow queries, profiling)
//   * Charts (built-in visualization)
//   * Data API (HTTP-based access without driver)

// Monitoring API — expose metrics to your stack
// /api/atlas/v2/groups/{groupId}/processes/{host}/measurements
//   metrics: CPU, memory, disk IOPS, ops/sec, replication lag, cache miss rate

// Alerts API — integrate with PagerDuty/Slack/email/Datadog
// metric thresholds + custom logic + escalation policies

// Backup — PITR
// /api/atlas/v2/groups/{groupId}/backup/snapshots
//   restore to any point within retention window
//   restore to a NEW cluster for testing without touching prod

// Atlas Search index — defined declaratively
{
  "name": "products_search",
  "definition": {
    "mappings": {
      "fields": {
        "name":     { "type": "string", "analyzer": "lucene.standard" },
        "category": { "type": "stringFacet" },
        "price":    { "type": "number" }
      }
    }
  }
}

// Atlas Online Archive — auto-archive cold data to cheap storage
{
  "criteria": {
    "type": "DATE",
    "dateField": "created_at",
    "expireAfterDays": 365
  },
  "collName": "events",
  "dbName":   "app"
}
// Old docs queryable via federation; storage cost ~5% of hot tier

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Atlas (managed)Zero ops; instant scaling; built-in securitySelf-host — full control but heavy ops burden
Cloud Backup with PITRRestore to any second in retention windowmongodump — full snapshots only; manual ops
Auto-scale disk + computeSurvives sudden traffic spikesStatic sizing — outage during unexpected load
VPC peering / PrivateLinkTraffic never traverses public internetPublic + IP allowlist — works but exposes attack surface
Atlas Search co-locatedOne platform; same auth + clusterExternal Elasticsearch — another component to manage

Production polish: manage Atlas as code via Terraform (mongodbatlas_* resources) or Pulumi — clusters, users, network rules, search indexes, all version-controlled. Integrate alerts with PagerDuty, Opsgenie, Slack, Microsoft Teams, Datadog, New Relic. Identity: federate database access via OIDC with Auth0/Okta/WorkOS/Azure AD — no shared passwords. Compliance: Atlas is SOC 2, HIPAA (BAA), PCI-DSS Level 1, ISO 27001, FedRAMP; Drata/Vanta/Secureframe consume Atlas evidence automatically. Cost optimization: use Online Archive for cold data; auto-pause dev clusters; right-size via Performance Advisor recommendations. Atlas Application Services (formerly Realm) provide GraphQL/REST endpoints, sync to mobile, and serverless functions — useful for rapid prototypes. Atlas Stream Processing handles Kafka-style ingestion natively. For multi-region failover, Atlas Global Clusters enable zone-aware sharding with GDPR-compliant data residency. Self-hosted equivalent for full control: MongoDB Ops Manager + Kubernetes Operator.

Question 51

You are asked to design a database for a travel booking platform with flights, hotels, and car rentals. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a travel booking platform aggregates flights, hotels, and car rentals. Users search availability with date ranges, locations, and filters; bookings are atomic across multiple line items; inventory updates arrive from external suppliers (GDS, hotel APIs, rental aggregators) in near real time.

Approach: separate domain collections (flights, hotels, car_rentals) keep schema clarity and indexing patterns clean; bookings snapshot all line items at purchase time so historical records survive supplier price/availability changes. Inventory caches sit in MongoDB but the source of truth is the upstream supplier API; cache TTL keeps drift bounded.

// flights
db.flights.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  flight_no: "BA178",
  carrier: "British Airways",
  origin: { code: "JFK", city: "New York" },
  destination: { code: "LHR", city: "London" },
  departure: ISODate("2026-06-01T18:30:00Z"),
  arrival:   ISODate("2026-06-02T07:30:00Z"),
  cabins: [
    { class: "economy",  fare_cents: 65000, seats_available: 23 },
    { class: "business", fare_cents: 320000, seats_available: 4 }
  ],
  cached_until: ISODate("2026-04-29T16:00:00Z")
})

// hotels — rooms embedded with rate plans
db.hotels.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  name: "Grand Plaza",
  location: { type: "Point", coordinates: [-0.127, 51.507] },
  star_rating: 4,
  rooms: [
    { type: "deluxe_king", rate_cents: 28900, available_dates: [/* ... */] }
  ],
  cached_until: ISODate("...")
})

// bookings — snapshot everything
db.bookings.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  pnr: "ABC123",
  status: "confirmed",
  items: [
    { type: "flight", flight_no: "BA178", origin: "JFK", destination: "LHR",
      departure: ISODate("..."), passenger: "Alice Smith", seat: "14A",
      fare_cents: 65000 },
    { type: "hotel", hotel_name: "Grand Plaza", check_in: ISODate("..."),
      check_out: ISODate("..."), nights: 3, rate_cents: 28900 },
    { type: "car",   vendor: "Hertz", pickup: "LHR", days: 3, rate_cents: 4500 }
  ],
  totals: { subtotal_cents: 161200, tax_cents: 12880, total_cents: 174080 },
  payment: { provider: "stripe", intent_id: "pi_..." },
  created_at: new Date()
})

// Indexes
db.flights.createIndex({ "origin.code": 1, "destination.code": 1, departure: 1 })
db.hotels.createIndex({ location: "2dsphere" })
db.hotels.createIndex({ "rooms.available_dates": 1 })
db.bookings.createIndex({ user_id: 1, created_at: -1 })
db.bookings.createIndex({ pnr: 1 }, { unique: true })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Separate collections per productDistinct query patterns, indexes, schemasSingle products — sparse indexes, polymorphic mess
Snapshot all line items in bookingSurvives supplier price/availability changesReferences — broken when flight updates
Cached inventory + TTLFast search; drift boundedLive supplier calls per search — rate limits, latency
2dsphere on hotel location“Hotels near LHR” in millisecondsFilter by city string — misses nearby cities
Compound flight index (origin + destination + date)Most common search pattern is index-coveredSingle-field — intersect at query time, slow

Production polish: integrate Amadeus/Sabre/Travelport GDS for live flight inventory and Booking.com/Expedia EAN/Hotelbeds for hotel inventory; payments through Stripe/Adyen; idempotency keys on booking POST defeat double-clicks and retries; Saga pattern for multi-supplier bookings — if hotel confirms but flight fails, compensating refund triggers automatically via Temporal/Inngest; fraud detection via Sift/Stripe Radar; pricing experiments via Statsig/LaunchDarkly; transactional confirmations via Postmark/Resend/SendGrid; SMS via Twilio; cancellation/refund flows hit the supplier API and update the booking status — never delete the original; analytics export to BigQuery/Snowflake via Airbyte; cache hot search routes (NYC → LON next 30 days) in Redis with 60s TTL.

Question 52

How would you handle data deduplication to remove duplicate records from a MongoDB collection?

Situation: a MongoDB collection contains duplicate records — same logical entity ingested multiple times due to retries, bad ETL, or relaxed uniqueness constraints in legacy systems. The duplicates must be detected, merged, and prevented going forward.

Approach: three phases. Detect with an aggregation that groups by candidate keys; merge with a deterministic strategy (latest wins, oldest wins, field-by-field) inside a transaction or idempotent script; prevent with a unique index on the natural key. For fuzzy duplicates (typo variations), use Atlas Search or external tools.

// 1. DETECT — group by candidate key, find groups with count > 1
db.users.aggregate([
  { $group: {
      _id: { $toLower: "$email" },
      ids: { $push: "$_id" },
      count: { $sum: 1 },
      latest: { $max: "$updated_at" }
  }},
  { $match: { count: { $gt: 1 } } },
  { $merge: "users_duplicates" }   // materialize for review
])

// 2. MERGE — for each group, keep the latest, remove others
db.users_duplicates.find().forEach(group => {
  const winner = db.users.find({ _id: { $in: group.ids } })
                          .sort({ updated_at: -1 }).limit(1).next()
  const losers = group.ids.filter(id => !id.equals(winner._id))

  // Reassign foreign keys before deleting
  db.orders.updateMany({ user_id: { $in: losers } }, { $set: { user_id: winner._id } })

  // Optionally union arrays from losers into winner
  const merged = mergeFields(winner, losers.map(id => db.users.findOne({ _id: id })))
  db.users.updateOne({ _id: winner._id }, { $set: merged })

  db.users.deleteMany({ _id: { $in: losers } })
})

// 3. PREVENT — unique index on natural key
db.users.createIndex({ email: 1 }, { unique: true, collation: { locale: "en", strength: 2 } })

// Handle write conflicts on inserts — idempotent upsert
db.users.updateOne(
  { email: "alice@example.com" },
  { $setOnInsert: { created_at: new Date() }, $set: { updated_at: new Date() } },
  { upsert: true }
)

// Fuzzy duplicate detection (Levenshtein, soundex) via Atlas Search
db.users.aggregate([
  { $search: {
      index: "users_search",
      moreLikeThis: { like: [{ name: "Alice Smith", email: "alice@example.com" }] }
  }}
])

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Aggregation-based detectionMaterializes candidate groups for human reviewLive dedup — slow, locks during merge
Latest-wins by updated_atDeterministic, reproducible, debuggableRandom pick — unpredictable, breaks idempotency
Reassign FKs before deleteAvoids orphan recordsCascade delete — data loss
Unique index with collationCase/accent-insensitive uniquenessPlain unique — Alice@ and alice@ both inserted
Idempotent upsert patternRetries don’t create duplicatesPlain insert — double-spend on network retry

Production polish: for fuzzy duplicates (Alice Smith vs. Alyce Smith), use MongoDB Atlas Search with the moreLikeThis operator or Atlas Vector Search on name embeddings; identity resolution at scale — tools like Senzing, Zingg, or AWS Entity Resolution; for customer data unification use Segment Personas/RudderStack/Hightouch; preventive guard rails: idempotency keys on every external integration (Stripe, webhooks); schema validation with $jsonSchema requiring email/external_id; data quality monitoring via Soda/Great Expectations/Monte Carlo with daily duplicate count metrics; change streams trigger an immediate dedup check when a write hits a known dupe pattern; for compliance (GDPR “right to be forgotten”), the dedup process becomes part of the data subject deletion workflow; audit log every merge with the original IDs, timestamps, and operator for traceability.

Question 53

Describe how you would implement a schema for a loyalty program with customers, points, and rewards.

Situation: a loyalty program tracks customer points earned through purchases, points redeemed for rewards, tiers (bronze/silver/gold), and partner offers. Reads — current point balance, history — are frequent; writes happen on every transaction; reconciliation must be auditable for finance.

Approach: three collections. customers holds profile and a denormalized cached points_balance for O(1) reads; point_events is an append-only ledger of every earn/redeem; rewards defines what can be redeemed. Balance is the source of truth, but reconciled against the ledger nightly.

// customers — cached balance + tier
db.customers.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  email: "alice@example.com",
  member_since: new Date(),
  tier: "silver",                    // bronze | silver | gold | platinum
  tier_valid_until: ISODate("2026-12-31"),
  points_balance: 4_280,             // cached for O(1) reads
  lifetime_points: 12_450,
  preferences: { offers: true }
})

// point_events — append-only ledger
db.point_events.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  customer_id: ObjectId("..."),
  type: "earn",                      // earn | redeem | expire | adjust
  points: 250,                        // signed: +earn, -redeem
  reason: "purchase",
  reference: { type: "order", id: ObjectId("...") },
  expires_at: ISODate("2027-04-29"), // points expire after 1 year
  ts: new Date()
})

// rewards catalog
db.rewards.insertOne({
  _id: "reward_freeshipping",
  name: "Free Shipping",
  cost_points: 500,
  active: true,
  inventory: 10_000
})

// Atomic earn (with cache update + ledger insert)
const session = client.startSession()
await session.withTransaction(async () => {
  await db.point_events.insertOne({
    customer_id, type: "earn", points: 250, reason: "purchase",
    reference: { type: "order", id: orderId },
    expires_at: addYears(new Date(), 1), ts: new Date()
  }, { session })
  await db.customers.updateOne(
    { _id: customer_id },
    { $inc: { points_balance: 250, lifetime_points: 250 } },
    { session }
  )
})

// Redeem — refuse if insufficient
await db.customers.updateOne(
  { _id: customer_id, points_balance: { $gte: 500 } },
  { $inc: { points_balance: -500 } }
)
// Conditional update returns matchedCount=0 if balance < 500

// Indexes
db.point_events.createIndex({ customer_id: 1, ts: -1 })
db.point_events.createIndex({ expires_at: 1, type: 1 })   // for expiry job
db.customers.createIndex({ email: 1 }, { unique: true })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Append-only ledgerAudit trail; finance can reconcileMutate balance only — no audit, can’t debug
Cached points_balanceO(1) reads on customer pageLive $sum — expensive on long histories
Conditional decrementPrevents negative balance race conditionsRead-then-write — check-time-of-use bug
Transaction for earnCache and ledger always in syncTwo writes — partial failures cause drift
Per-event expiryFIFO expiration; simple to computeBucket expiry — complex partial expiries

Production polish: nightly reconciliation job recomputes points_balance = sum(point_events) and alerts on any drift — integrate with Datadog/PagerDuty; tier evaluation runs nightly against the rolling 12-month lifetime points; expiration job at month-end emits expire events with reference to the original earn event; partner offers (earn 5x at restaurants) tagged on the event for analytics; fraud detection via velocity checks (1000 points earned in 5 minutes flags the account); customer service portal uses type: "adjust" events with reason codes for goodwill credits; marketing automation triggers (you’re 200 points from gold!) via Customer.io/Braze/Iterable; export ledger to Snowflake/BigQuery via Airbyte for finance reporting; blockchain-style verifiability (chain hashes on events) is overkill for most loyalty programs — a daily SHA-256 of the day’s ledger appended to a tamper-evident log via AWS QLDB or Datomic is enough.

Question 54

You need to design a database for a sports league with teams, players, and matches. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a sports league with teams, players, matches, schedules, and stats. Players move between teams across seasons; match results update standings in near real time; statistics need historical accuracy (a player’s 2024 stats reflect their 2024 team, not their current team).

Approach: four collections. teams holds canonical team data; players holds player identity; player_seasons records per-season team affiliations and stats (so historical accuracy survives transfers); matches records games with snapshotted lineups. Standings are computed via aggregation.

// teams — canonical
db.teams.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  name: "Manchester United",
  short: "MUN",
  league: "premier_league",
  founded: 1878,
  stadium: "Old Trafford"
})

// players — identity, not affiliation
db.players.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  full_name: "Marcus Lee",
  born: ISODate("2000-03-12"),
  nationality: "GB",
  position: "forward"
})

// player_seasons — per-season team and stats
db.player_seasons.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  player_id: ObjectId("..."),
  team_id:   ObjectId("..."),
  season:    "2025-26",
  jersey_no: 9,
  stats: {
    appearances: 0,
    goals: 0,
    assists: 0,
    yellow_cards: 0,
    minutes_played: 0
  }
})

// matches — with snapshotted lineups
db.matches.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  league: "premier_league",
  season: "2025-26",
  matchday: 12,
  kickoff: ISODate("2026-04-29T15:00:00Z"),
  home: {
    team_id: ObjectId("..."), team_name: "Manchester United",
    score: 2,
    lineup: [
      { player_id: ObjectId("..."), name: "Marcus Lee", position: "ST", jersey: 9 }
    ]
  },
  away: {
    team_id: ObjectId("..."), team_name: "Liverpool",
    score: 1,
    lineup: [/* ... */]
  },
  events: [
    { minute: 23, type: "goal", team: "home", player_id: ObjectId("...") },
    { minute: 67, type: "yellow_card", team: "away", player_id: ObjectId("...") }
  ],
  status: "completed"     // scheduled | live | completed | postponed
})

// Standings — aggregated on demand
db.matches.aggregate([
  { $match: { season: "2025-26", league: "premier_league", status: "completed" } },
  { $facet: {
      home: [
        { $project: {
            team_id: "$home.team_id", points: {
              $cond: [{ $gt: ["$home.score", "$away.score"] }, 3,
                { $cond: [{ $eq: ["$home.score", "$away.score"] }, 1, 0] }]
            },
            gf: "$home.score", ga: "$away.score"
        }}
      ],
      away: [
        { $project: {
            team_id: "$away.team_id", points: {
              $cond: [{ $gt: ["$away.score", "$home.score"] }, 3,
                { $cond: [{ $eq: ["$home.score", "$away.score"] }, 1, 0] }]
            },
            gf: "$away.score", ga: "$home.score"
        }}
      ]
  }},
  { $project: { all: { $concatArrays: ["$home", "$away"] } } },
  { $unwind: "$all" },
  { $group: {
      _id: "$all.team_id",
      points: { $sum: "$all.points" },
      gf: { $sum: "$all.gf" }, ga: { $sum: "$all.ga" }
  }},
  { $set: { gd: { $subtract: ["$gf", "$ga"] } } },
  { $sort: { points: -1, gd: -1, gf: -1 } },
  { $merge: "standings_2025_26" }
])

// Indexes
db.player_seasons.createIndex({ player_id: 1, season: 1 }, { unique: true })
db.matches.createIndex({ season: 1, matchday: 1 })
db.matches.createIndex({ "home.team_id": 1, kickoff: -1 })
db.matches.createIndex({ "away.team_id": 1, kickoff: -1 })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Per-season player recordHistorical stats unchanged when player transfersSingle player doc with current team — rewrites history
Snapshot lineup in matchPlayer numbers, names, positions accurate foreverLive join — breaks when player leaves
Materialized standingsStandings page loads in O(team count)Live aggregate per request — 380 matches scanned per page load
Embed events in matchOne read renders the match pageSeparate match_events — multiple round trips
Status field driving UILive/completed/postponed — clear state machineComputed from kickoff — ambiguous (delays, abandons)

Production polish: ingest live match data from Opta/StatsPerform/Sportradar; live updates via change streams piped to WebSockets (Pusher/Ably/PartyKit); fantasy points and projections rebuilt nightly to BigQuery/Snowflake; player headshots and team logos via Cloudinary; video highlights via Mux/Cloudflare Stream; predictions and odds from third-party APIs (The Odds API); fan engagement push notifications via OneSignal/Firebase Cloud Messaging; Atlas Search powers player and team typeahead; caching: hot match pages and standings in Redis with TTL invalidation triggered by match-update change streams; betting integrations require strict separation and KYC via Persona/Onfido; export to warehouse via Airbyte/Fivetran for analytics dashboards in Metabase/Hex/Atlas Charts.

Question 55

How would you handle real-time notifications and alerts in a MongoDB application?

Situation: the application sends real-time notifications and alerts — new message alerts, mentions, payment confirmations, system warnings. Notifications go to web (in-app + browser push), iOS, Android, email, and SMS. Users can mute, snooze, and configure preferences per category.

Approach: separate generation from delivery. A notifications collection stores the canonical record (delivered to the in-app inbox). A separate worker reads new notifications via change streams and dispatches to channels (push, email, SMS) via specialized providers, respecting per-user preferences and quiet hours.

// notifications — canonical record
db.notifications.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  category: "mention",            // mention | dm | system | billing | marketing
  title: "Alice mentioned you",
  body: "Alice mentioned you in #general",
  link: "/channels/general?msg=...",
  data: { actor_id: ObjectId("..."), channel_id: ObjectId("...") },
  channels: ["inapp", "push", "email"],   // requested fan-out
  status: "queued",                        // queued | delivered | seen | dismissed
  created_at: new Date(),
  delivered_at: null,
  seen_at: null
})

// user_notification_preferences
db.user_notification_preferences.insertOne({
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  preferences: {
    mention:   { inapp: true,  push: true,  email: false, sms: false },
    dm:        { inapp: true,  push: true,  email: false, sms: true  },
    billing:   { inapp: true,  push: true,  email: true,  sms: false },
    marketing: { inapp: true,  push: false, email: true,  sms: false }
  },
  quiet_hours: { start: "22:00", end: "07:00", tz: "America/New_York" },
  push_tokens: [
    { device: "ios",     token: "...", added_at: new Date() },
    { device: "android", token: "...", added_at: new Date() }
  ]
})

// Worker watches new notifications and dispatches
const stream = db.notifications.watch([
  { $match: { operationType: "insert" } }
], { fullDocument: "default" })

for await (const change of stream) {
  const notif = change.fullDocument
  const prefs = await db.user_notification_preferences.findOne({ user_id: notif.user_id })
  if (inQuietHours(prefs.quiet_hours)) {
    await db.notifications.updateOne({ _id: notif._id }, { $set: { snoozed_until: nextWindow(prefs) } })
    continue
  }
  for (const channel of notif.channels) {
    if (!prefs.preferences[notif.category]?.[channel]) continue
    await dispatch(channel, notif, prefs)
  }
}

// Mark as seen (user opens inbox)
db.notifications.updateMany(
  { user_id, seen_at: null },
  { $set: { seen_at: new Date(), status: "seen" } }
)

// Indexes
db.notifications.createIndex({ user_id: 1, created_at: -1 })
db.notifications.createIndex({ user_id: 1, seen_at: 1 })   // unread badge
db.user_notification_preferences.createIndex({ user_id: 1 }, { unique: true })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
