Production polish: integrate Petfinder API/Adopt-a-Pet API for cross-listing on the largest networks; photos via Cloudinary/imgix/Cloudflare Images with auto background removal (Remove.bg) for cleaner thumbnails; matching recommendations via Atlas Vector Search on pet trait embeddings vs adopter preference embeddings; application workflow orchestrated via Temporal/Inngest/Trigger.dev with steps (review → reference check → home visit → approval); messaging between adopters and shelters via Stream Chat/SendBird with moderation; donations to shelters via Stripe/Donorbox/GiveButter with optional recurring; email notifications via Postmark/Resend/SendGrid at status changes; status sync: change streams update search indexes (Atlas Search/Algolia/Meilisearch) and remove adopted pets from listings within seconds; fraud detection on applications (puppy mills, resellers) via Sift/Castle; follow-up surveys 30/60/90 days post-adoption via scheduled jobs.

Question 85

How would you implement a feature that tracks and analyzes transaction histories in a financial application using MongoDB?

Situation: a financial application tracks transaction histories — payments, transfers, refunds, fees — with strict requirements: every transaction is auditable, immutable once posted, queryable by user/account/date range, and balanceable. Volume can reach billions of transactions over years. Reports must reconstruct any account’s state at any point in time.

Approach: append-only ledger pattern. A transactions collection records every event with full context; cached account_balances hold current state for O(1) reads. The ledger is the source of truth; balances are projections. Use Decimal128 for money. Partition by date for query performance and archival.

// transactions — append-only ledger
db.transactions.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  type: "transfer",   // payment | transfer | refund | fee | adjustment
  status: "posted",   // pending | posted | reversed
  account_id: ObjectId("..."),       // affected account
  counterparty_account_id: ObjectId("..."),
  amount: Decimal128("100.00"),       // signed: positive = credit, negative = debit
  currency: "USD",
  balance_after: Decimal128("1234.56"),  // running balance snapshot at post time
  reference: "txn_2026_04_29_abc123",     // idempotency key
  description: "Transfer to Bob",
  metadata: { external_ref: "ach_xyz", fee_id: ObjectId("...") },
  posted_at: new Date(),
  created_at: new Date(),
  reversed_by: null    // pointer to reversal txn if any
})

// account_balances — cached projection, eventually consistent
db.account_balances.updateOne(
  { _id: accountId },
  {
    $inc: { balance: Decimal128("100.00") },
    $set: { last_txn_id: txnId, updated_at: new Date() }
  }
)

// Atomic transfer (debit + credit + balance updates)
const session = client.startSession()
await session.withTransaction(async () => {
  // 1. Debit source (with sufficiency check)
  const src = await db.account_balances.findOneAndUpdate(
    { _id: fromId, balance: { $gte: Decimal128("100.00") } },
    { $inc: { balance: Decimal128("-100.00") } },
    { session, returnDocument: "after" }
  )
  if (!src) throw new Error("insufficient_funds")

  // 2. Credit destination
  const dst = await db.account_balances.findOneAndUpdate(
    { _id: toId },
    { $inc: { balance: Decimal128("100.00") } },
    { session, returnDocument: "after" }
  )

  // 3. Write both legs of the ledger
  await db.transactions.insertMany([
    { type: "transfer", account_id: fromId, amount: Decimal128("-100.00"),
      balance_after: src.balance, reference: ref + ":debit", posted_at: new Date(), session },
    { type: "transfer", account_id: toId, amount: Decimal128("100.00"),
      balance_after: dst.balance, reference: ref + ":credit", posted_at: new Date(), session }
  ], { session })
})

// Reconstruct balance at point in time
db.transactions.aggregate([
  { $match: { account_id, posted_at: { $lte: pointInTime } } },
  { $group: { _id: null, balance: { $sum: "$amount" } } }
])

// Indexes
db.transactions.createIndex({ account_id: 1, posted_at: -1 })
db.transactions.createIndex({ reference: 1 }, { unique: true })   // idempotency
db.transactions.createIndex({ counterparty_account_id: 1, posted_at: -1 })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Append-only ledgerAudit, reversibility, point-in-time reconstructionMutable rows — lose history, useless in court
Cached balance + ledger source-of-truthO(1) reads + auditable writesSum on read — O(history), too slow at scale
Decimal128 for moneyNo floating-point driftFloat — pennies disappear
Idempotency key unique indexSafe retries, no duplicate transfersNone — network retries cause double-charges
Two ledger entries per transferDouble-entry accounting; balances always sum to zeroSingle row — can’t reconcile

Production polish: for serious financial systems, use a purpose-built ledger database — TigerBeetle (open source, designed for double-entry at extreme throughput), AWS QLDB (cryptographically verifiable), or Modern Treasury/Increase/Unit as a service; for retail-scale projects, MongoDB works well; regulatory compliance: PCI-DSS for card storage (use Stripe/Adyen/Spreedly tokens, never raw PANs), SOX for audit retention (7+ years), AML/KYC via Persona/Onfido/Sumsub/Alloy; archival: transactions >1 year old to Atlas Online Archive (queryable, ~$0.01/GB) or S3 Glacier with manifest in MongoDB; reconciliation jobs nightly compare ledger sum to cached balance; reports via aggregation pipelines exported to BigQuery/Snowflake; currency conversion handled by Wise/Currencylayer at the boundary, not in the ledger; fraud detection in real time via Sift/Stripe Radar; reversals recorded as new transactions with reverses pointer, never deletes.

Question 86

Explain how you would design a schema for a hobby community site with users, hobbies, and events.

Situation: a community site for hobbyists (knitting, hiking, board games) where users join hobby groups, attend events, and discuss in forums. Each user follows multiple hobbies; events have RSVPs and capacity limits; groups are local-first (geo-based discovery) with online-only fallback; activity feeds show events and posts from followed hobbies.

Approach: four collections — users (with embedded hobby preferences), groups (one per hobby+location), events (one per scheduled meetup), and rsvps (separate so capacity caps and waitlists work cleanly). Use 2dsphere for “groups near me”; conditional inserts for atomic capacity-bounded RSVPs.

// users
db.users.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  username: "alice",
  display_name: "Alice",
  bio: "Hiker and knitter",
  hobby_ids: ["hobby_hiking", "hobby_knitting"],
  home_location: { type: "Point", coordinates: [-73.99, 40.71] },
  groups_joined: [ObjectId("..."), ObjectId("...")],
  created_at: new Date()
})

// groups — one per hobby + region
db.groups.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  hobby_id: "hobby_hiking",
  name: "NYC Hikers",
  slug: "nyc-hikers",
  description: "Day hikes in and around NYC",
  location: {
    type: "Point",
    coordinates: [-73.99, 40.71],
    city: "NYC"
  },
  online_only: false,
  member_count: 1247,
  organizer_ids: [ObjectId("...")],
  created_at: new Date()
})

// events
db.events.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  group_id: ObjectId("..."),
  group_name: "NYC Hikers",   // denorm
  hobby_id: "hobby_hiking",
  title: "Bear Mountain Day Hike",
  description: "...",
  cover_image: "https://...",
  starts_at: ISODate("2026-05-10T13:00:00Z"),
  ends_at:   ISODate("2026-05-10T19:00:00Z"),
  location: {
    type: "Point",
    coordinates: [-74.01, 41.32],
    name: "Bear Mountain State Park"
  },
  capacity: 30,
  rsvp_count: 0,
  waitlist_count: 0,
  status: "scheduled",   // scheduled | cancelled | completed
  organizer_id: ObjectId("..."),
  created_at: new Date()
})

// rsvps — separate to enforce capacity atomically
db.rsvps.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  event_id: ObjectId("..."),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  status: "going",   // going | waitlist | declined | cancelled
  position: 1,        // for waitlist ordering
  rsvp_at: new Date()
})

// Atomic RSVP with capacity check
async function rsvp(eventId, userId) {
  const result = await db.events.findOneAndUpdate(
    { _id: eventId, status: "scheduled", rsvp_count: { $lt: ["$capacity", "$rsvp_count"] === false ? 1e9 : 0 } },
    {} // dummy, we’ll use a proper $expr filter below
  )
  // Better: use $expr-based filter
  const e = await db.events.findOneAndUpdate(
    {
      _id: eventId,
      status: "scheduled",
      $expr: { $lt: ["$rsvp_count", "$capacity"] }
    },
    { $inc: { rsvp_count: 1 } },
    { returnDocument: "after" }
  )
  if (e) {
    await db.rsvps.insertOne({ event_id: eventId, user_id: userId, status: "going", rsvp_at: new Date() })
    return { status: "going" }
  }
  // Full — add to waitlist
  await db.events.updateOne({ _id: eventId }, { $inc: { waitlist_count: 1 } })
  await db.rsvps.insertOne({ event_id: eventId, user_id: userId, status: "waitlist", rsvp_at: new Date() })
  return { status: "waitlist" }
}

// Indexes
db.groups.createIndex({ location: "2dsphere" })
db.groups.createIndex({ hobby_id: 1, member_count: -1 })
db.events.createIndex({ location: "2dsphere" })
db.events.createIndex({ hobby_id: 1, starts_at: 1, status: 1 })
db.rsvps.createIndex({ event_id: 1, user_id: 1 }, { unique: true })
db.rsvps.createIndex({ user_id: 1, rsvp_at: -1 })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Separate rsvps collectionPer-user uniqueness, capacity enforcement, waitlistsEmbed RSVPs in event — doc bloat at high attendance
$expr capacity checkAtomic check-and-increment in one operationRead-then-write — race past capacity
Denormalized countersO(1) reads of attendanceLive $count on rsvps — expensive on hot events
2dsphere geo for groups + events“Near me” discoveryCity strings — rigid, misses suburban areas
Hobby IDs referenceHobby taxonomy editable in one placeEmbed hobby strings — rename = mass update

Production polish: for turnkey community platforms, Meetup API, Discord, Slack, Circle.so, Discourse deliver months of features for free; discussion forums via embedded Discourse or Discord webhooks; chat via Stream Chat/SendBird/TalkJS; email digests via Customer.io/Iterable/Klaviyo with weekly “upcoming events near you”; calendar invites via iCal/Google Calendar API; notifications mobile + web via OneSignal/Knock/Courier; moderation via Perspective API/Hive/OpenAI Moderation API on posts; maps via Mapbox/Google Maps Platform; recommendations via Atlas Vector Search on hobby/interest embeddings; events ingest from external sources (Meetup, Eventbrite, Facebook Events) via webhooks; SEO: per-group/event pages with structured data (Schema.org Event); monetization: paid premium memberships via Stripe Billing; SMS reminders for RSVPs via Twilio.

Question 87

You are asked to design a database for a meal planning app with users, recipes, and meal plans. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a meal planning app where users browse recipes, drag them into weekly meal plans, generate shopping lists, and track nutrition. Recipes have ingredients with quantities, allergens, and tags; meal plans are per-user weekly grids; shopping lists aggregate ingredients across multiple recipes with smart deduplication.

Approach: three collections — recipes, meal_plans, and shopping_lists. Recipes are canonical, indexed by tags, allergens, and dietary fit. Meal plans embed recipe references per slot (no live join). Shopping lists are materialized from a meal plan via aggregation, with on-the-fly ingredient consolidation.

// recipes
db.recipes.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  author_id: ObjectId("..."),
  title: "Chicken Tikka Masala",
  slug: "chicken-tikka-masala",
  description: "Classic Indian curry...",
  cuisine: "indian",
  meal_types: ["dinner"],
  tags: ["spicy", "comfort", "high-protein"],
  diet_tags: ["gluten-free"],
  allergens: ["dairy"],
  servings: 4,
  prep_minutes: 20,
  cook_minutes: 40,
  difficulty: "medium",
  ingredients: [
    { name: "chicken thighs", quantity: 700, unit: "g", category: "protein" },
    { name: "yogurt", quantity: 200, unit: "g", category: "dairy" },
    { name: "tomato puree", quantity: 400, unit: "ml", category: "pantry" },
    { name: "garam masala", quantity: 2, unit: "tsp", category: "spice" }
  ],
  steps: [
    { step: 1, text: "Marinate chicken in yogurt and spices for 30 min..." },
    { step: 2, text: "Sear chicken pieces..." }
  ],
  nutrition: {
    calories: 480, protein_g: 38, carbs_g: 18, fat_g: 28, fiber_g: 3
  },
  image_url: "https://cdn.../tikka.jpg",
  created_at: new Date()
})

// meal_plans
db.meal_plans.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  week_start: ISODate("2026-04-27"),
  servings_default: 2,
  slots: [
    {
      day: 1, meal: "dinner",
      recipe_id: ObjectId("..."),
      recipe_title: "Chicken Tikka Masala",   // snapshot
      servings: 2,
      notes: "Use leftover for tomorrow lunch"
    }
  ],
  generated_shopping_list_id: ObjectId("..."),
  created_at: new Date()
})

// shopping_lists — materialized from a meal plan
db.shopping_lists.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  meal_plan_id: ObjectId("..."),
  items: [
    { name: "chicken thighs", quantity: 700, unit: "g", category: "protein", purchased: false, recipes: ["Chicken Tikka Masala"] },
    { name: "yogurt", quantity: 200, unit: "g", category: "dairy", purchased: false, recipes: ["Chicken Tikka Masala"] }
  ],
  generated_at: new Date()
})

// Build shopping list from a meal plan
async function buildShoppingList(planId) {
  const plan = await db.meal_plans.findOne({ _id: planId })
  const recipes = await db.recipes.find(
    { _id: { $in: plan.slots.map(s => s.recipe_id) } }
  ).toArray()
  const recipeMap = Object.fromEntries(recipes.map(r => [r._id.toString(), r]))

  // Consolidate ingredients across all slots
  const acc = new Map()
  for (const slot of plan.slots) {
    const recipe = recipeMap[slot.recipe_id.toString()]
    const scale = slot.servings / recipe.servings
    for (const ing of recipe.ingredients) {
      const key = `${ing.name}::${ing.unit}`
      const existing = acc.get(key) || { ...ing, quantity: 0, recipes: [] }
      existing.quantity += ing.quantity * scale
      existing.recipes.push(recipe.title)
      acc.set(key, existing)
    }
  }
  return Array.from(acc.values())
}

// Indexes
db.recipes.createIndex({ tags: 1 })
db.recipes.createIndex({ diet_tags: 1, allergens: 1, meal_types: 1 })
db.recipes.createIndex({ slug: 1 }, { unique: true })
db.meal_plans.createIndex({ user_id: 1, week_start: -1 })
db.shopping_lists.createIndex({ user_id: 1, generated_at: -1 })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Embed ingredients in recipeOne read; matches edit patternSeparate collection — joins, no reason to split
Reference recipes from meal plansRecipe edits propagate to future plansEmbed full recipe — mass updates
Materialize shopping listEditable, persisted, marks “purchased”Compute every render — loses checkmarks
App-side ingredient consolidationSmart unit conversion, recipe attributionAggregation pipeline — harder to express unit rules
Snapshot recipe title in plan slotPlan still readable if recipe deleted$lookup on render — latency, broken on delete

Production polish: nutrition data from Edamam/Spoonacular/Nutritionix/USDA FoodData Central APIs — cached locally; recipe import from URLs via schema.org/Recipe structured data parsing or OpenAI/Anthropic with structured output; image generation for missing photos via DALL·E/Imagen/Stable Diffusion; recommendations via Atlas Vector Search on recipe embeddings + dietary preferences; shopping list export to Instacart Affiliate API/Amazon Fresh/Walmart Open API/AnyList; barcode scanning at home (mark “already have”) via Open Food Facts; meal kit integration with HelloFresh/Blue Apron partnerships; family sharing via shared meal plan documents (Q37 collaboration patterns); seasonal/regional adjustment swaps unavailable produce via Atlas Vector Search; caloric/macro targets from user profile drive plan suggestions; fitness app sync via Apple HealthKit/Google Fit; recipe versioning tracks user customizations; full-text search via Atlas Search with synonyms (e.g., “cilantro”/“coriander”).

Question 88

How would you handle data migration and synchronization between MongoDB and a data warehouse?

Situation: data lives in MongoDB (transactional, high-write) but the analytics team needs a data warehouse (BigQuery, Snowflake, Databricks) for SQL reports, BI dashboards, and ML training. Live aggregation against MongoDB is too slow and risks impacting production. The pipeline must keep the warehouse fresh (minutes-to-hours lag), handle schema drift, and survive failures.

Approach: two-track pipeline. Change Data Capture (CDC) streams MongoDB writes via change streams → Debezium MongoDB connector → Kafka → warehouse loaders for near-real-time tables. Batch ETL periodically (hourly/daily) does full-collection snapshots for backfill, schema fix-ups, and dimensional rebuilds. Tools like Airbyte/Fivetran/Estuary/Stitch handle both modes.

// 1. Change Streams — CDC source
const stream = db.collection("orders").watch([], {
  fullDocument: "updateLookup",
  fullDocumentBeforeChange: "whenAvailable",
  resumeAfter: lastResumeToken
})

for await (const change of stream) {
  await kafkaProducer.send({
    topic: "mongo.orders.cdc",
    messages: [{
      key: change.documentKey._id.toString(),
      value: JSON.stringify({
        op: change.operationType,         // insert | update | delete | replace
        ts: change.clusterTime,
        ns: change.ns,
        before: change.fullDocumentBeforeChange,
        after:  change.fullDocument,
        change_id: change._id              // resume token
      })
    }]
  })
  await persistResumeToken(change._id)
}

// 2. Warehouse loader (BigQuery example)
//    Append-only landing table; incremental MERGE into dim/fact tables
//    BigQuery SQL:
//    MERGE warehouse.dim_orders T
//    USING staging.orders_cdc S
//    ON T.order_id = S.after.order_id
//    WHEN MATCHED AND S.op = "delete" THEN DELETE
//    WHEN MATCHED THEN UPDATE SET ...
//    WHEN NOT MATCHED THEN INSERT (...)

// 3. Batch ETL (Airbyte/Fivetran connector config)
//    Source: MongoDB (full + incremental sync via _id watermark)
//    Destination: BigQuery / Snowflake / Databricks
//    Sync schedule: hourly
//    Schema inference: enabled, with manual overrides

// 4. Schema versioning — embedded in CDC message
{
  "schema_version": 2,
  "after": { "order_id": "...", "total_cents": 4999, ... }
}

// 5. Reconciliation — periodic count check
//    MongoDB:   db.orders.countDocuments({})
//    Warehouse: SELECT COUNT(*) FROM warehouse.dim_orders
//    Alert if drift > 0.1%

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
CDC + batch hybridNear-real-time + reliable backfillBatch only — hours of lag, fragile
Debezium/Kafka middle layerBuffers consumer outages, replays history, scales fanoutDirect change streams → warehouse — tightly coupled, no replay
Schema versioning in messagesConsumers handle drift gracefullyImplicit schema — breaks on field renames
MERGE-based loaders (idempotent)Safe re-delivery; consumers can replayAppend-only — data drift, requires dedupe job
Reconciliation jobsDetect missed events, broken pipelinesTrust without verify — silent corruption

Production polish: for most teams, managed ETL via Airbyte Cloud/Fivetran/Estuary Flow/Stitch/Hevo beats hand-rolled — they handle schema drift, retries, dead-letter queues, monitoring, and 100+ destination integrations; warehouse choice: BigQuery (separation of storage/compute, serverless), Snowflake (multi-cloud, time travel), Databricks (ML workloads, Unity Catalog), ClickHouse/Tinybird/SingleStore (real-time OLAP); transformations in the warehouse via dbt (source of truth for metrics) or SQLMesh; reverse ETL (warehouse → ops tools) via Hightouch/Census/Polytomic; data quality via Great Expectations/Soda/Monte Carlo; data catalog via Atlan/Collibra/DataHub; BI via Looker/Tableau/Mode/Metabase/Hex/Preset; cost monitoring via Vantage/SELECT.dev; PII handling: tokenize via Skyflow/Basis Theory/VGS/CipherStash before warehouse load; sync from warehouse to operational systems (lookups in apps) via reverse ETL or feature stores like Tecton/Feast.

Question 89

Describe how you would implement a schema for an event ticketing platform with events, venues, and tickets.

Situation: an event ticketing platform sells tickets to concerts, sports, and conferences. Each event has a venue with seats; ticket purchases are concurrent (high-demand “drops”); seats can’t be sold twice; refunds and resales must be tracked; tickets are scanned at entry and must be verifiable. Bot abuse and scalpers are persistent threats.

Approach: four collections — events, venues, tickets, and orders. Tickets exist as pre-generated rows (one per seat or one per ticket type with capacity); purchases atomically claim them via conditional updates. Tickets carry signed QR codes that scanners verify offline; payment flows through Stripe with idempotency keys.

// venues
db.venues.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  name: "Madison Square Garden",
  capacity: 20000,
  layout: { sections: [
    { name: "Floor", row_count: 30, seats_per_row: 50 },
    { name: "200 Level", row_count: 20, seats_per_row: 80 }
  ]}
})

// events
db.events.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  venue_id: ObjectId("..."),
  venue_name: "Madison Square Garden",   // denorm
  title: "Artist Tour 2026",
  starts_at: ISODate("2026-08-15T20:00:00Z"),
  on_sale_at: ISODate("2026-05-01T16:00:00Z"),
  status: "scheduled",
  ticket_types_summary: [
    { name: "VIP",     price_cents: 50000, total: 100,  remaining: 100 },
    { name: "Floor",   price_cents: 25000, total: 1500, remaining: 1500 },
    { name: "200",     price_cents: 8000,  total: 5000, remaining: 5000 }
  ]
})

// tickets — one per seat (general admission uses ticket_type capacity instead)
db.tickets.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  event_id: ObjectId("..."),
  ticket_type: "Floor",
  seat: { section: "Floor", row: 5, seat: 12 },
  price_cents: 25000,
  status: "available",   // available | held | sold | refunded | cancelled
  hold_expires_at: null,
  order_id: null,
  qr_code: null,         // populated on sale
  scanned_at: null
})

// orders
db.orders.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  event_id: ObjectId("..."),
  ticket_ids: [ObjectId("..."), ObjectId("...")],
  total_cents: 50000,
  payment: {
    stripe_intent_id: "pi_...",
    status: "succeeded",
    idempotency_key: "ord_2026_05_01_abc123"
  },
  created_at: new Date()
})

// Atomic seat hold (10-minute reservation while user pays)
const ticket = await db.tickets.findOneAndUpdate(
  {
    event_id: eventId,
    "seat.section": "Floor", "seat.row": 5, "seat.seat": 12,
    status: "available"
  },
  {
    $set: { status: "held", hold_expires_at: new Date(Date.now() + 600_000), held_by: userId }
  },
  { returnDocument: "after" }
)

// On payment success, finalize the sale
await db.tickets.updateOne(
  { _id: ticket._id, status: "held", held_by: userId },
  { $set: { status: "sold", order_id: orderId, qr_code: signQr(ticket._id), hold_expires_at: null } }
)

// Cleanup expired holds (cron job)
db.tickets.updateMany(
  { status: "held", hold_expires_at: { $lt: new Date() } },
  { $set: { status: "available", hold_expires_at: null, held_by: null } }
)

// Indexes
db.tickets.createIndex({ event_id: 1, status: 1 })
db.tickets.createIndex({ event_id: 1, "seat.section": 1, "seat.row": 1, "seat.seat": 1 }, { unique: true })
db.tickets.createIndex({ status: 1, hold_expires_at: 1 })
db.orders.createIndex({ "payment.idempotency_key": 1 }, { unique: true })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Pre-generated ticket docsAtomic claim via single update; no raceCounter-based — dual-claim races
Hold → sold state machineReserves seat while user pays; cleanup on timeoutSell-on-add — abandoned carts hold seats forever
Signed QR codesOffline scanner verification; tamper-proofDB lookup at gate — outage = no entry
Idempotency keys on ordersStripe retries don’t double-chargeNone — refund nightmares
Unique seat compound indexSchema-enforced no-duplicate-seatApp-only — missed bugs sneak in

Production polish: for massive on-sale events (Taylor Swift, World Cup) MongoDB alone won’t cut it — front with Redis for atomic counter-based holds, queue requests via Cloudflare Waiting Room/Queue-it, then commit settled holds to MongoDB; bot defense via Cloudflare Turnstile/hCaptcha/Arkose Labs/Datadome; payments via Stripe/Adyen with 3DS2 for chargeback protection; QR codes signed with JWT or HMAC, scanned via scandit/ZXing; resale market via separate listing collection with platform fees through Stripe Connect; fraud detection via Sift/Riskified; customer support tickets via Zendesk/Intercom/Front; refunds handled by Stripe with audit trail; email/SMS confirmations via Postmark/Resend/Twilio; secondary market compliance varies by jurisdiction (StubHub clones); for full-service alternatives: Eventbrite, Ticketmaster Reseller API, Universe, Ticket Tailor.

Question 90

You need to design a database for a vehicle rental service with cars, customers, and rentals. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a vehicle rental service (cars, vans, scooters) where customers book vehicles for date ranges, pick up at locations, and return. Concurrent customers can’t book the same vehicle for overlapping windows; pricing varies by date and demand; insurance, deposits, and damages are tracked; fleet utilization and maintenance schedules need visibility.

Approach: three collections — vehicles, customers, and rentals. Vehicles hold static metadata; rentals are date-range bookings with overlap checks. Use the same overlap-prevention pattern as Q81 (appointment scheduling) but with date ranges. Pickup/return locations support geo queries. Pricing is computed at booking time from a rate card and stored as snapshot.

// vehicles
db.vehicles.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  vin: "1HGCM82633A004352",
  category: "midsize_sedan",
  make: "Toyota", model: "Camry", year: 2024,
  features: ["automatic", "ac", "bluetooth"],
  current_location: {
    type: "Point",
    coordinates: [-73.99, 40.71],
    branch_id: ObjectId("...")
  },
  daily_rate_cents: 6500,
  status: "available",   // available | rented | maintenance | retired
  odometer_km: 12450,
  last_maintenance_at: ISODate("2026-03-15"),
  next_maintenance_due_km: 15000,
  insurance_policy: "POL-12345"
})

// customers
db.customers.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  full_name: "Alice Smith",
  driver_license: { number: encrypt("D12345678"), state: "NY", expires: ISODate("2030-01-15") },
  age: 32,
  payment_method_id: "pm_stripe_xxx",
  rental_count: 3,
  flagged: false
})

// rentals
db.rentals.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  vehicle_id: ObjectId("..."),
  customer_id: ObjectId("..."),
  pickup: {
    branch_id: ObjectId("..."),
    location: { type: "Point", coordinates: [-73.99, 40.71] },
    scheduled_at: ISODate("2026-05-10T10:00:00Z"),
    actual_at: null
  },
  return: {
    branch_id: ObjectId("..."),
    scheduled_at: ISODate("2026-05-15T10:00:00Z"),
    actual_at: null
  },
  daily_rate_cents: 6500,        // snapshot
  estimated_total_cents: 32500,
  deposit_cents: 50000,
  insurance_addon_cents: 1500,
  status: "confirmed",   // confirmed | active | completed | cancelled | overdue
  damages: [],
  payment: { stripe_intent_id: "pi_...", status: "captured" },
  created_at: new Date()
})

// Booking with overlap check (similar to Q81)
async function bookVehicle(vehicleId, customerId, pickupAt, returnAt) {
  // Check no overlapping confirmed/active rental exists
  const overlap = await db.rentals.findOne({
    vehicle_id: vehicleId,
    status: { $in: ["confirmed", "active"] },
    "pickup.scheduled_at": { $lt: returnAt },
    "return.scheduled_at": { $gt: pickupAt }
  })
  if (overlap) throw new Error("vehicle_unavailable")

  return await db.rentals.insertOne({
    vehicle_id: vehicleId, customer_id: customerId,
    pickup: { scheduled_at: pickupAt },
    return: { scheduled_at: returnAt },
    daily_rate_cents: vehicle.daily_rate_cents,
    status: "confirmed",
    created_at: new Date()
  })
}

// Indexes
db.vehicles.createIndex({ "current_location": "2dsphere" })
db.vehicles.createIndex({ category: 1, status: 1 })
db.rentals.createIndex({ vehicle_id: 1, "pickup.scheduled_at": 1, "return.scheduled_at": 1 })
db.rentals.createIndex({ customer_id: 1, "pickup.scheduled_at": -1 })
db.rentals.createIndex({ status: 1, "return.scheduled_at": 1 })   // overdue scan

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Date-range overlap queryStandard interval-overlap test, indexableSlot-based bookings — rigid, wastes capacity
Snapshot rate at bookingCustomer paid agreed price even if rate changesLive rate — surprise charges
Vehicle status field + rental stateTwo views of the same fact; ops dashboardCompute status — expensive on large fleets
Pickup/return geoBranch network optimizationCity strings — rigid
Encrypted driver licensePII protection (CSFLE/Queryable Encryption)Plaintext — PCI/GDPR risk

Production polish: integrate Stripe for deposits and final charges (manual capture for the deposit, capture-and-refund-on-return for damages); fraud screening via Sift/Stripe Radar/Persona + driver-license OCR/verification; telematics integration (mileage, location, harsh braking) via Geotab/Samsara/Stem Mobility/Smartcar API — for connected vehicles, sync via change streams to the rental record; dynamic pricing via demand surfaces in BigQuery/Snowflake; maintenance scheduling as separate state machine, blocks the vehicle from being booked when due; damage reporting with photos to S3/Cloudinary + AI-assisted assessment via Tractable/Hyperscience; peer-to-peer rentals (Turo-style): Stripe Connect, host KYC via Persona/Onfido, insurance via Buckle/Slice; airport pickups integrate with FlightAware/FlightStats for arrival tracking; route optimization for fleet rebalancing via Mapbox Optimization API/Google Maps Platform; customer support via Zendesk/Intercom; EV-specific: charge level, charging-station integration via OCPP/EV Connect/ChargePoint API.

Question 91

How would you implement a feature that tracks and manages inventory levels across multiple warehouses in MongoDB?

Situation: an inventory system tracks stock across many warehouses (regional fulfillment centers, retail stores, third-party logistics partners). Each SKU has a different on-hand quantity per warehouse; orders are routed to the nearest stocked warehouse; transfers move inventory between locations; counts must remain accurate under concurrent reservations, sales, and receipts.

Approach: a per-product-per-warehouse stock document is the unit of inventory. products hold catalog metadata; warehouses hold location and capabilities; inventory holds the (product, warehouse) crossing with on-hand, reserved, and incoming counts. Atomic conditional updates on the inventory doc handle reservations and prevent oversells. Transfers use a transaction across the source and destination inventory docs.

// products — catalog only
db.products.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  sku: "WIDGET-001",
  name: "Blue Widget",
  unit_cost_cents: 1200,
  active: true
})

// warehouses
db.warehouses.insertOne({
  _id: "wh_nyc_01",
  name: "NYC Fulfillment",
  type: "fulfillment",     // fulfillment | retail | 3pl | dropship
  location: { type: "Point", coordinates: [-74.006, 40.712] },
  active: true
})

// inventory — one doc per (product, warehouse)
db.inventory.insertOne({
  _id: { product_id: ObjectId("..."), warehouse_id: "wh_nyc_01" },
  on_hand:    142,        // physically present, sellable
  reserved:   8,          // allocated to unfilled orders
  incoming:   50,         // PO in transit
  reorder_at: 25,         // threshold
  updated_at: new Date()
})

// Atomic reservation — the heart of inventory correctness
const result = await db.inventory.updateOne(
  {
    _id: { product_id: pid, warehouse_id: whId },
    on_hand: { $gte: qty }     // guard
  },
  {
    $inc: { on_hand: -qty, reserved: qty },
    $currentDate: { updated_at: true }
  }
)
if (result.modifiedCount === 0) throw new Error("Insufficient stock")

// Order routing: pick nearest warehouse with stock
const wh = await db.inventory.aggregate([
  { $match: { "_id.product_id": pid, on_hand: { $gte: qty } } },
  { $lookup: { from: "warehouses", localField: "_id.warehouse_id", foreignField: "_id", as: "wh" } },
  { $unwind: "$wh" },
  { $geoNear: { /* compute distance from customer */ } },
  { $sort: { distance: 1 } },
  { $limit: 1 }
]).next()

// Transfer between warehouses (atomic across two docs)
const session = client.startSession()
await session.withTransaction(async () => {
  await db.inventory.updateOne(
    { _id: { product_id: pid, warehouse_id: srcWh }, on_hand: { $gte: qty } },
    { $inc: { on_hand: -qty } }, { session }
  )
  await db.inventory.updateOne(
    { _id: { product_id: pid, warehouse_id: dstWh } },
    { $inc: { incoming: qty } }, { session }
  )
  await db.transfers.insertOne({
    product_id: pid, src: srcWh, dst: dstWh, qty,
    status: "in_transit", created_at: new Date()
  }, { session })
})

// Indexes
db.inventory.createIndex({ "_id.product_id": 1, on_hand: -1 })
db.inventory.createIndex({ "_id.warehouse_id": 1, on_hand: 1, reorder_at: 1 })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Per (product, warehouse) docOne-doc atomic updates, no contention across warehousesEmbed warehouses array in product — hot doc, contention
Compound _idNatural unique key; idempotent upserts on receiptSurrogate _id + unique compound index — works but extra index
Conditional $gte guardRace-free: oversells impossibleRead-then-write — classic race condition
Reserve / on_hand splitMid-flight orders held without committingSingle available count — loses intent on cancellation
Transactions for transfersBoth legs commit or neither — no phantom inventoryTwo updates — netting errors over time

Production polish: integrate with WMS (warehouse management) systems via APIs — ShipBob, ShipStation, Shippo, Flexport, EasyPost for label generation and tracking; cycle counts reconcile physical vs DB counts — record variance per cycle in an audit collection; safety stock and reorder logic via BullMQ/Inngest daily jobs that file POs when on_hand + incoming < reorder_at; change streams on inventory pipe low-stock alerts to Slack/email; reverse logistics (returns) flow back into a separate “returns processing” bucket before re-shelving; multi-channel sales (Shopify, Amazon, retail POS) sync via Trayio/Stedi EDI; analytics on stockouts, turn rate, dead stock via warehouse export to BigQuery/Snowflake; for real-time multi-region consistency, MongoDB global clusters with zone sharding by warehouse region; 3PL integrations (Amazon FBA, ShipBob) call back via webhooks — idempotent upserts on inventory using PO number.

Question 92

Explain how you would design a schema for a photo-sharing app with users, photos, and comments.

Situation: a photo-sharing app stores user uploads, comments, likes, and a discovery feed. Photos may be public or private; users follow each other; the home feed shows recent photos from followed users. EXIF data must be stripped for privacy; thumbnails generated on the fly; abusive content moderated.

Approach: images live in object storage (S3/R2/Cloudinary) with metadata in MongoDB. Three core collections: users, photos, comments. Likes are tracked as a denormalized counter on photos plus a separate likes collection for “has user X liked photo Y” lookups. The follow graph in follows drives feeds via fan-out-on-read for users with normal follower counts and fan-out-on-write for celebrities (already covered for the social platform Q14, applied here).

// users
db.users.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  username: "alice",
  display_name: "Alice",
  bio: "photographer",
  avatar_url: "https://cdn.../alice.jpg",
  followers_count: 0,    // denorm
  following_count: 0,
  posts_count: 0,
  created_at: new Date()
})

// photos — metadata only, image in S3
db.photos.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  owner_id:    ObjectId("..."),
  caption:     "Sunset at the bridge",
  s3_key:      "photos/u123/01HRG.../full.webp",
  variants:    {                                    // pre-generated sizes
    thumb_160:  "photos/u123/01HRG.../t160.webp",
    feed_640:   "photos/u123/01HRG.../f640.webp",
    full_1920:  "photos/u123/01HRG.../full.webp"
  },
  width: 4032, height: 3024,
  exif_stripped: true,
  visibility: "public",        // public | followers | private
  tags: ["sunset", "bridge", "nyc"],
  location: { type: "Point", coordinates: [-73.96, 40.78] },   // optional
  likes_count: 0,
  comments_count: 0,
  moderation: { status: "approved", scores: { nsfw: 0.01, violence: 0.00 } },
  created_at: new Date()
})

// comments — separate collection
db.comments.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  photo_id: ObjectId("..."),
  user_id:  ObjectId("..."),
  text: "Beautiful!",
  created_at: new Date()
})

// likes — one doc per (user, photo)
db.likes.insertOne({
  _id: { user_id: uid, photo_id: pid },
  created_at: new Date()
})
// Plus increment denorm counter atomically
db.photos.updateOne({ _id: pid }, { $inc: { likes_count: 1 } })

// Indexes
db.photos.createIndex({ owner_id: 1, created_at: -1 })
db.photos.createIndex({ visibility: 1, created_at: -1 })
db.photos.createIndex({ tags: 1, created_at: -1 })
db.photos.createIndex({ location: "2dsphere" })
db.comments.createIndex({ photo_id: 1, created_at: -1 })

// User feed — fan-out-on-read for non-celebs
const followingIds = await db.follows.find({ follower_id: meId }).toArray()
const feed = await db.photos.find({
  owner_id: { $in: followingIds.map(f => f.followed_id) },
  visibility: { $in: ["public", "followers"] }
}).sort({ created_at: -1 }).limit(20).toArray()

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
S3 + variants + CDNCheap storage, fast global deliveryBSON blobs — bloats working set
EXIF stripping mandatoryRemoves GPS leaks, camera serial numbersKeep EXIF — privacy disaster
Pre-generate variantsO(1) feed renders, no on-the-fly resize costLive resize — CPU per request, latency
Likes in separate doc + counterPer-user uniqueness + fast countsEmbed user IDs array in photo — 16MB cap, contention
Comments separate, not embeddedUnbounded growth; pagination neededEmbed — doc grows, hot writes contend

Production polish: use Cloudflare Images/Cloudinary/imgix/Imgproxy for image processing — auto-format (AVIF/WebP), responsive sizes, blurhash placeholders; EXIF strip via sharp or ImageMagick on upload; NSFW/violence detection via Hive/AWS Rekognition/Google Cloud Vision/Amazon Comprehend — flagged content goes to a moderation queue before publish; perceptual hashing (pHash) detects duplicates and known illegal content (PhotoDNA-style); face blurring for privacy-sensitive contexts via face-api.js; direct upload via presigned URLs from browser; resumable uploads via tus.io/uppy; discovery feed mixes following + recommended via Atlas Vector Search on caption + visual embeddings; trending tags computed hourly from a time-series collection; copyright protection via watermarking and DMCA workflows; analytics (impressions, engagement) export to Mixpanel/Amplitude/PostHog; user reporting with workflows in Linear/Jira; rate-limit upload abuse via Upstash Ratelimit.

Question 93

You are asked to design a database for an online learning platform with courses, quizzes, and certificates. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: an online learning platform offers courses with quizzes and issues certificates upon completion. Students enroll, take quizzes, complete modules, and earn verifiable certificates that employers can validate. The schema must track progress per student per course, store quiz attempts (multiple allowed), and produce tamper-resistant certificates.

Approach: five collections. users, courses with embedded modules and lessons, enrollments (one per student per course) carrying progress, quiz_attempts (one per attempt; many per student per quiz), and certificates (immutable, signed). Certificate IDs are public; verification via a public endpoint that recomputes the signature.

// courses — embed modules and lessons; quizzes referenced separately
db.courses.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  slug: "intro-to-mongodb",
  title: "Intro to MongoDB",
  modules: [
    {
      module_id: "m1",
      title: "Getting Started",
      lessons: [
        { lesson_id: "l1", title: "Installation", video_key: "vid/abc.m3u8", duration_min: 6 },
        { lesson_id: "l2", title: "First query",  video_key: "vid/def.m3u8", duration_min: 8 }
      ],
      quiz_id: ObjectId("...")
    }
  ],
  pass_threshold: 0.7,
  certificate_template: "default_v3",
  active: true,
  created_at: new Date()
})

// quizzes — separate (large question banks, randomization)
db.quizzes.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  course_id: ObjectId("..."),
  module_id: "m1",
  questions: [
    {
      qid: "q1",
      stem: "What does MongoDB store?",
      options: ["BSON documents", "rows", "files"],
      correct: 0,
      points: 1
    }
  ],
  randomize: true,
  time_limit_minutes: 30
})

// enrollments — one doc per (student, course) with progress
db.enrollments.insertOne({
  _id: { student_id: ObjectId("..."), course_id: ObjectId("...") },
  status: "active",     // active | completed | dropped
  progress: {
    completed_lessons: ["m1.l1"],
    current: { module_id: "m1", lesson_id: "l2" },
    percent: 25
  },
  enrolled_at: new Date(),
  completed_at: null
})

// quiz_attempts — one per attempt, append-only
db.quiz_attempts.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  student_id: ObjectId("..."),
  quiz_id: ObjectId("..."),
  answers: [{ qid: "q1", selected: 0 }],
  score: 0.85,
  passed: true,
  started_at: ISODate("..."),
  submitted_at: ISODate("...")
})

// certificates — immutable, signed
db.certificates.insertOne({
  _id: "cert_01HRG...",                 // human-shareable
  student_id: ObjectId("..."),
  course_id: ObjectId("..."),
  course_title: "Intro to MongoDB",     // snapshotted
  student_name:  "Alice Smith",          // snapshotted
  issued_at: new Date(),
  signature: "ed25519:abc123...",        // signed by private key
  verification_url: "https://learn.example.com/verify/cert_01HRG..."
})

// Indexes
db.enrollments.createIndex({ "_id.student_id": 1, status: 1 })
db.quiz_attempts.createIndex({ student_id: 1, quiz_id: 1, submitted_at: -1 })
db.certificates.createIndex({ student_id: 1, issued_at: -1 })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Embed modules and lessons in courseOne read renders the whole course; bounded countSeparate collection — multiple reads per page
Quizzes separate (large question banks)Question randomization; faculty edits decoupledEmbed — doc bloats, edit contention
Compound _id on enrollmentNatural unique constraint; cheap upserts on enrollRandom _id + unique index — works, extra index
Append-only quiz attemptsAudit trail, retake history, analyticsOverwrite latest attempt — loses history
Cryptographically signed certificateVerifiable without trusting a database lookupDB-only check — can’t prove offline

Production polish: for video delivery use Mux/Cloudflare Stream/Bitmovin with adaptive bitrate; DRM via Widevine/FairPlay/PlayReady for paid content; certificate verification via Accredible, Sertifier, or Credly — or DIY with Ed25519 signatures and public verification endpoint; plagiarism on essay assessments via Turnitin; SCORM/xAPI/LTI standards for enterprise LMS interoperability; quiz proctoring via Honorlock/ProctorU/Respondus; course recommendations via Atlas Vector Search on course descriptions + completion patterns; discussion forums via Stream Chat/Discourse; calendaring for cohort-based courses via Calendly/Cal.com; payments and subscriptions via Stripe Billing/Chargebee; analytics on completion rates, drop-off points via Mixpanel/Amplitude/PostHog; accessibility: closed captions via Rev/AssemblyAI/Deepgram; certificate sharing with LinkedIn Add to Profile deep links; multi-language via i18next/next-intl + Lokalise/Crowdin.

Question 94

How would you handle data backup and recovery to ensure minimal data loss in MongoDB?

Situation: the production database holds millions of customer records, financial transactions, and audit logs. A backup strategy must protect against accidental deletes, ransomware, regional outages, schema-corrupting bugs, and operator mistakes — with bounded RPO (recovery point objective) and RTO (recovery time objective). Compliance may mandate retention for years.

Approach: three layers. Continuous backups with point-in-time restore (oplog tailed continuously) for low RPO. Periodic snapshots (daily, weekly, monthly) for medium-term retention. Cold archives (Glacier/Archive Storage) for long-term compliance. Combine with cross-region replication for AZ/region failure protection. Test restores regularly — an untested backup is a hope, not a strategy.

// MongoDB Atlas: built-in
//   - Continuous Cloud Backup (oplog continuously archived to S3)
//   - Snapshot Schedule (every 6h / daily / weekly / monthly)
//   - Point-in-time restore to any second within retention window
//   - Cross-region restore + automatic encryption with KMS

// Self-hosted: mongodump + oplog tail
// Daily logical backup
mongodump --uri="$URI" --out=/backup/$(date +%Y%m%d) --gzip --oplog

// Continuous oplog tailing (resumable)
mongodump --uri="$URI" --db=local --collection=oplog.rs \
          --query='{ "ts": { "$gt": { "$timestamp": ... } } }'

// Filesystem snapshot (faster, atomic with --fsync & lock)
db.adminCommand({ fsync: 1, lock: true })
//   take EBS / LVM snapshot
db.adminCommand({ fsyncUnlock: 1 })

// Application-level “soft delete” for accidental drops
db.collection.updateMany(
  { _id: { $in: [...] } },
  { $set: { deleted: true, deleted_at: new Date(), deleted_by: userId } }
)
// TTL index reaps after 30 days
db.collection.createIndex({ deleted_at: 1 }, { expireAfterSeconds: 30 * 86400 })

// Restore a single collection from snapshot
mongorestore --uri="$URI" --nsInclude="myapp.users" /backup/20260429/

// Verify backup integrity
mongorestore --uri="$VERIFY_URI" --dryRun /backup/20260429/

// PITR to a specific timestamp (Atlas)
// Atlas UI: Restore -> Point in Time -> pick exact second within retention

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Continuous + snapshot + archiveEach layer covers different failure modesSingle layer — gaps in coverage
PITR for low RPORecover from accidental writes within secondsDaily snapshot only — up to 24h data loss
Cross-region copiesSurvives regional disastersSame-region only — AZ outage = data loss
Soft delete + TTL30-day undo for accidental application deletesRestore-from-backup — slow, disruptive
Quarterly restore drillsVerifies the backups actually workUntested backups — discover failures during outages

Production polish: use MongoDB Atlas Backup for managed backups — PITR up to 7 days, snapshot retention up to 75 years, restore to any cluster, automatic encryption, no operator burden; for self-hosted, Percona Backup for MongoDB (PBM) is the gold standard — consistent backups across sharded clusters, oplog continuous backup, point-in-time restore; 3-2-1 rule: 3 copies, 2 different media, 1 off-site — e.g., production cluster, daily snapshots in S3, weekly snapshots in S3 Glacier in another region; encryption: backups encrypted at rest with customer-managed keys (CMK) via AWS KMS/GCP KMS/HashiCorp Vault — a leaked S3 bucket without keys is useless; access control: backup buckets in a separate AWS account with read-only access from production, write-only from backup runner — ransomware can’t delete backups; S3 Object Lock / versioning for immutable backups (compliance, anti-ransomware); backup monitoring: alert if no successful backup in 25 hours, alert on RPO drift; quarterly disaster recovery drills — pick a snapshot, restore to a fresh cluster, run smoke tests, time-track RTO; document runbooks in Notion/Confluence; for compliance (SOX, HIPAA, GDPR) match retention to legal requirements; logical backups for migrations use mongodump + mongorestore with --archive for streaming.

Question 95

Describe how you would implement a schema for a financial analytics platform with users, portfolios, and transactions.

Situation: a financial analytics platform tracks user portfolios, transactions, and computed analytics (returns, allocations, risk metrics). Users link brokerage accounts, transactions stream in continuously, and the platform must compute time-weighted returns, asset allocation, and tax lots accurately. Money math must never lose precision.

Approach: four collections. users with linked accounts; accounts per-brokerage account with positions; transactions as an append-only ledger of every buy, sell, dividend, deposit, withdrawal; portfolios as denormalized rollups updated nightly. All money in Decimal128; all dates UTC; all rates from authoritative sources cached daily.

// users
db.users.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  email: "alice@example.com",
  base_currency: "USD",
  created_at: new Date()
})

// accounts — per linked brokerage
db.accounts.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  provider: "plaid",
  provider_account_id: "acct_xyz",
  institution: "Vanguard",
  account_type: "brokerage",   // brokerage | ira | 401k | crypto
  positions: [
    { symbol: "VTI", qty: NumberDecimal("142.5832"), avg_cost: NumberDecimal("234.12") }
  ],
  cash_balance: NumberDecimal("1834.42"),
  last_synced_at: new Date()
})

// transactions — append-only ledger
db.transactions.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  account_id: ObjectId("..."),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  date: ISODate("2026-04-29"),
  type: "buy",                       // buy | sell | dividend | deposit | withdrawal | fee
  symbol: "VTI",
  qty: NumberDecimal("10.0000"),
  price: NumberDecimal("265.42"),
  amount: NumberDecimal("-2654.20"), // signed
  fees: NumberDecimal("0.00"),
  fx_rate: NumberDecimal("1.0000"),
  source: "plaid",                   // plaid | manual | csv
  imported_at: new Date()
})

// portfolios — nightly rollup
db.portfolios.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  as_of: ISODate("2026-04-29"),
  total_value:    NumberDecimal("142583.42"),
  cash:           NumberDecimal("3242.18"),
  invested:       NumberDecimal("139341.24"),
  ytd_return:     NumberDecimal("0.0842"),
  twr_1y:         NumberDecimal("0.1342"),
  allocation: {
    equity:      NumberDecimal("0.78"),
    bond:        NumberDecimal("0.18"),
    cash:        NumberDecimal("0.04")
  },
  computed_at: new Date()
})

// Time-weighted return (Modified Dietz approximation, daily)
// Sub-period returns chained — correct under cash flows
const subPeriods = await db.transactions.aggregate([
  { $match: { user_id: uid, date: { $gte: from, $lte: to }, type: { $in: ["deposit", "withdrawal"] } } },
  { $sort: { date: 1 } },
  { $group: { _id: "$date", flow: { $sum: "$amount" } } }
]).toArray()

// Indexes
db.transactions.createIndex({ user_id: 1, date: -1 })
db.transactions.createIndex({ account_id: 1, date: -1 })
db.transactions.createIndex({ user_id: 1, symbol: 1, date: -1 })
db.portfolios.createIndex({ user_id: 1, as_of: -1 })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Decimal128 throughoutExact decimal math; no float drift on centsDouble — classic floating-point bugs in money
Append-only transactionsAuditable, replayable; reconciliation trivialMutable — corrections lose history
Nightly portfolio rollupDashboard reads in O(1); compute heavy work offlineLive aggregation — slow, inconsistent under load
Cached positions on accountReal-time-ish balance display without scanning ledgerRecompute every read — expensive
FX rates per transactionAccurate multi-currency; reproducible historical reportsLive rate — non-reproducible analytics

Production polish: integrate brokerages via Plaid Investments, SnapTrade, Yodlee, MX, or direct OAuth (Coinbase, Robinhood APIs); market data from Polygon.io, IEX Cloud, Alpha Vantage, Finnhub, Tiingo; FX rates from Open Exchange Rates/currencylayer; tax lots tracked per buy with FIFO/LIFO/specific-ID accounting — produce 8949/1099-B forms via TaxBit/CoinTracker/Cointracking; risk analytics (Sharpe, beta, VaR) computed in a Python service using numpy/pandas/QuantLib; charting via TradingView Lightweight Charts/Recharts/Victory; real-time prices via WebSocket from market data providers, broadcast via Pusher/Ably/Pubnub; compliance: SEC, FINRA reporting, KYC via Persona/Onfido/Sumsub; encryption: PII via Skyflow/Basis Theory/VGS; for backtesting, ETL to BigQuery/Snowflake/ClickHouse; warehouse-grade analytics use dbt + Cube.js; fraud detection via Sift/Stripe Radar; monitoring: alert on stale syncs, large unexpected balance changes.

Question 96

You need to design a database for a home automation system with devices, users, and actions. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a home automation system manages devices (lights, locks, thermostats, sensors, cameras), users (homeowners and family members), and actions (rules, scenes, schedules). Devices report state continuously over MQTT; users trigger actions from apps; rules fire automatically on conditions (motion + after sunset = turn on porch light).

Approach: four core collections. users per home owner; homes as the multi-device unit; devices with current state and metadata; actions (rules and triggered events). Telemetry (sensor readings, on/off events) goes to a separate time-series collection. State changes flow through change streams to drive automations.

// homes
db.homes.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  owner_id: ObjectId("..."),
  members: [
    { user_id: ObjectId("..."), role: "admin",  added_at: new Date() },
    { user_id: ObjectId("..."), role: "member", added_at: new Date() }
  ],
  name: "Main House",
  timezone: "America/New_York",
  location: { type: "Point", coordinates: [-73.96, 40.78] },
  created_at: new Date()
})

// devices — current state + metadata
db.devices.insertOne({
  _id: "dev_thermo_01",
  home_id: ObjectId("..."),
  vendor: "ecobee",
  model: "SmartThermostat",
  type: "thermostat",
  room: "Living Room",
  state: {
    current_temp_f: 72.4,
    target_temp_f:  70.0,
    mode: "heat",
    online: true
  },
  capabilities: ["temperature", "humidity", "schedule"],
  firmware_version: "4.7.1",
  last_seen: new Date(),
  paired_at: ISODate("2024-12-01")
})

// telemetry — time-series, very high volume
db.createCollection("telemetry", {
  timeseries: { timeField: "ts", metaField: "meta", granularity: "seconds" },
  expireAfterSeconds: 90 * 86400    // 90-day retention
})
db.telemetry.insertOne({
  ts: new Date(),
  meta: { device_id: "dev_thermo_01", home_id: ObjectId("..."), type: "temperature" },
  value: 72.4,
  unit: "F"
})

// actions — rule definitions
db.actions.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  home_id: ObjectId("..."),
  name: "Porch light at sunset",
  enabled: true,
  trigger: { type: "schedule", expr: "sunset" },
  conditions: [{ field: "weather.is_dark", op: "eq", value: true }],
  effects: [{ device_id: "dev_porch_light", set: { on: true, brightness: 80 } }],
  created_at: new Date()
})

// action runs — one per execution, auditable
db.action_runs.insertOne({
  action_id: ObjectId("..."),
  home_id: ObjectId("..."),
  triggered_at: new Date(),
  trigger_source: "sensor:motion",
  effects_applied: [{ device_id: "dev_porch_light", outcome: "ok" }],
  duration_ms: 142
})

// Atomic state update from MQTT message
db.devices.updateOne(
  { _id: "dev_thermo_01" },
  { $set: { "state.current_temp_f": 72.5, last_seen: new Date() } }
)

// Indexes
db.devices.createIndex({ home_id: 1, type: 1 })
db.devices.createIndex({ home_id: 1, room: 1 })
db.actions.createIndex({ home_id: 1, enabled: 1 })
db.action_runs.createIndex({ home_id: 1, triggered_at: -1 })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Time-series for telemetry10x compression, optimized appendRegular collection — storage explodes
Current state on device docFast app reads of “is the light on?”Compute from telemetry — slow
TTL on telemetryBounded storage; long-term in warehouseForever — expensive, pointless
Action runs as separate audit collectionWhy-did-this-happen forensicsNo log — mysterious automation behavior
Home-scoped indexesMulti-tenant isolation; one user’s data never scanned for anotherGlobal indexes — security and performance issues

Production polish: the MQTT broker (EMQX, Mosquitto, HiveMQ, AWS IoT Core) sits between devices and the app — persistence to MongoDB happens via a bridge service that batches writes; device shadow pattern (AWS IoT) handles intermittent connectivity — desired vs reported state; integrations with Matter/Thread (Apple HomeKit, Google Home, Amazon Alexa, Samsung SmartThings); cloud-to-cloud APIs: Ecobee, Nest, Ring, Philips Hue, August, Tuya, Z-Wave/Zigbee via Home Assistant; edge-side processing (Home Assistant, openHAB) for local-first automation when internet is down; video storage via Cloudflare Stream/Mux/Frigate; privacy: cameras process motion detection on-device with Frigate + Coral TPU — only events reach the cloud; geofencing via mobile SDKs (Radar, Gimbal) for arrive/leave triggers; natural language automation via OpenAI — “turn off all lights when I say good night”; energy management integration with utility APIs; scheduled rules and complex orchestration via Inngest/Temporal/Trigger.dev; monitoring via Datadog; alert on offline devices, stuck automations.

Question 97

How would you implement a feature that tracks and manages service requests and work orders in MongoDB?

Situation: a service-management platform tracks customer-submitted tickets, internal work orders, technician assignments, parts used, and SLAs. Tickets flow through a workflow (open → assigned → in_progress → awaiting_parts → resolved → closed). SLA breaches must trigger escalations; technicians need mobile access; managers need dashboards.

Approach: four collections. tickets for customer-facing requests; work_orders for technician-facing dispatch (one ticket may spawn multiple WOs); technicians with skills and schedules; parts with inventory references. Status changes are tracked in an embedded history array on the ticket; SLA timers are computed from the ticket and a holiday calendar.

// tickets — customer-facing
db.tickets.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  number: "T-2026-04231",     // human-readable
  org_id:      ObjectId("..."),
  customer_id: ObjectId("..."),
  title: "AC unit not cooling",
  description: "Started yesterday afternoon...",
  category: "hvac.repair",
  priority: "high",                 // low | normal | high | critical
  status:   "in_progress",          // open | assigned | in_progress | awaiting_parts | resolved | closed
  sla: {
    response_due: ISODate("2026-04-29T18:00:00Z"),
    resolve_due:  ISODate("2026-04-30T17:00:00Z"),
    response_at:  ISODate("2026-04-29T15:42:00Z"),
    resolve_at:   null
  },
  assigned_to:   ObjectId("..."),    // tech user
  history: [
    { ts: ISODate("..."), by: ObjectId("..."), from: "open",       to: "assigned" },
    { ts: ISODate("..."), by: ObjectId("..."), from: "assigned",   to: "in_progress" }
  ],
  attachments: [{ name: "photo.jpg", s3_key: "tickets/.../photo.jpg" }],
  created_at: new Date()
})

// work_orders — technician dispatch
db.work_orders.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  ticket_id: ObjectId("..."),
  technician_id: ObjectId("..."),
  scheduled_at: ISODate("2026-04-30T09:00:00Z"),
  duration_minutes: 90,
  location: { type: "Point", coordinates: [-73.96, 40.78] },
  parts_planned: [{ part_id: "PRT-001", qty: 1 }],
  parts_used:    [],
  status: "scheduled",     // scheduled | en_route | on_site | completed
  signature_s3_key: null,
  notes: "",
  completed_at: null
})

// technicians
db.technicians.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  name: "Bob Smith",
  skills: ["hvac", "electrical"],
  service_area: { type: "Polygon", coordinates: [[[-74,40], [-73,40], [-73,41], [-74,41], [-74,40]]] },
  van_inventory: [{ part_id: "PRT-001", qty: 5 }],
  active: true
})

// SLA breach detection (cron)
db.tickets.find({
  status: { $nin: ["resolved", "closed"] },
  "sla.resolve_due": { $lt: new Date() }
})

// Atomic status transition with history
db.tickets.updateOne(
  { _id: ticketId, status: "in_progress" },     // optimistic
  {
    $set:  { status: "resolved", "sla.resolve_at": new Date() },
    $push: { history: { ts: new Date(), by: userId, from: "in_progress", to: "resolved" } }
  }
)

// Indexes
db.tickets.createIndex({ org_id: 1, status: 1, priority: 1, created_at: -1 })
db.tickets.createIndex({ assigned_to: 1, status: 1 })
db.tickets.createIndex({ "sla.resolve_due": 1, status: 1 })  // SLA breach scanner
db.work_orders.createIndex({ technician_id: 1, scheduled_at: 1 })
db.work_orders.createIndex({ location: "2dsphere" })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Ticket vs work order splitCustomer view (one ticket) vs ops view (multi-visit)One collection — awkward dual perspective
Embedded history arrayOne read shows full lifecycle; bounded lengthSeparate collection — extra $lookup
Optimistic status transitionsPrevents stale concurrent updatesLast-write-wins — lost transitions
SLA fields denormalizedIndex-backed breach scanCompute on query — full scan
2dsphere on work ordersRoute optimization, nearest-tech dispatchLinear scan — slow at scale

Production polish: for field service management consider ServiceNow, Jira Service Management, Salesforce Field Service, FieldEdge, ServiceTitan, or HouseCallPro — massive specialized industry; for in-house, build on the above schema; route optimization for daily tech dispatching via Mapbox Optimization API, Google Maps Distance Matrix, OptimoRoute, Routific; technician mobile app needs offline-first — WatermelonDB/RxDB/SQLite with sync to MongoDB via background sync; customer notifications (en route, arriving in 15min) via Twilio/MessageBird; signature capture on completion stored in S3; parts inventory integrates with the warehouse system via change streams; payments via Stripe Terminal (in-person card readers); customer portal for status tracking; SLA escalations via BullMQ/Inngest/Temporal repeating jobs that page on-call via PagerDuty/Opsgenie; analytics: first-call resolution rate, mean time to repair, parts utilization via warehouse export; customer satisfaction survey via Delighted/Qualtrics; chat support via Intercom/Zendesk/Front; for predictive maintenance integrate IoT telemetry with Atlas Vector Search on failure patterns.

Question 98

Explain how you would design a schema for a health and wellness app with users, activities, and progress tracking.

Situation: a health and wellness app tracks user activity (steps, workouts, meals, sleep, mood), goals (weight, fitness, mindfulness), and progress over time. Users sync from wearables (Apple Watch, Fitbit, Garmin); the app shows trends, streaks, and personalized recommendations. Health data is sensitive — HIPAA awareness needed for medical-adjacent features.

Approach: four core collections. users with profile, preferences, and consent flags; activities as a time-series collection of events from any source; goals with current progress; summaries as daily/weekly rollups for fast dashboard reads. Wearable data lands via OAuth-authenticated webhook integrations; the schema is source-agnostic so any provider plugs in.

// users — profile + preferences (PHI-light)
db.users.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  email: "alice@example.com",
  display_name: "Alice",
  dob:        ISODate("1990-04-12"),       // for age-based zones
  sex_at_birth: "female",                  // for calorie/HR formulas
  height_cm:  168,
  weight_kg:  64.5,
  units: "metric",
  timezone: "America/New_York",
  consent: {
    health_data: true,
    research_share: false,
    marketing: true,
    consented_at: new Date()
  },
  created_at: new Date()
})

// activities — time-series, source-agnostic
db.createCollection("activities", {
  timeseries: { timeField: "ts", metaField: "meta", granularity: "seconds" }
})
db.activities.insertMany([
  { ts: new Date(), meta: { user_id, type: "steps",   source: "apple_health" }, value: 8421 },
  { ts: new Date(), meta: { user_id, type: "hr_avg",  source: "fitbit" },       value: 72 },
  { ts: new Date(), meta: { user_id, type: "sleep",   source: "oura" },         duration_min: 423, stages: { rem: 95, deep: 78 } },
  { ts: new Date(), meta: { user_id, type: "workout", source: "manual" },       category: "running", duration_min: 32, calories: 312 },
  { ts: new Date(), meta: { user_id, type: "meal",    source: "manual" },       calories: 412, protein_g: 32, carbs_g: 28, fat_g: 18 },
  { ts: new Date(), meta: { user_id, type: "mood",    source: "manual" },       score: 7, note: "energetic" }
])

// goals
db.goals.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  user_id: ObjectId("..."),
  type: "weight",                         // weight | steps_daily | sleep | calories | custom
  target: NumberDecimal("60.0"),
  unit: "kg",
  start_value: NumberDecimal("64.5"),
  start_date: ISODate("2026-01-01"),
  target_date: ISODate("2026-06-30"),
  status: "active",                       // active | achieved | abandoned
  created_at: new Date()
})

// summaries — daily rollup
db.summaries.insertOne({
  _id: { user_id: ObjectId("..."), date: ISODate("2026-04-29") },
  steps: 8421,
  hr_avg: 72,
  sleep_min: 423,
  workouts: 1,
  workout_minutes: 32,
  calories_in: 1842,
  calories_out: 2042,
  net_calories: -200,
  mood_avg: 7,
  computed_at: new Date()
})

// Daily rollup pipeline (runs nightly per user)
db.activities.aggregate([
  { $match: { "meta.user_id": uid, ts: { $gte: dayStart, $lt: dayEnd } } },
  { $group: { _id: "$meta.type", total: { $sum: "$value" }, count: { $sum: 1 } } },
  { $merge: { into: "summaries", on: "_id", whenMatched: "merge", whenNotMatched: "insert" } }
])

// Indexes
db.goals.createIndex({ user_id: 1, status: 1 })
db.summaries.createIndex({ "_id.user_id": 1, "_id.date": -1 })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Time-series for activities10x compression; optimized for high-volume health dataRegular collection — storage explodes
Source-agnostic activity schemaPlug-and-play wearables; no per-vendor schemasPer-vendor collections — combinatorial explosion
Daily summaries materializedFast dashboard reads, streak detectionLive aggregation — slow on long histories
Consent flags first-classGDPR/HIPAA compliance; audit trailImplicit consent — legal exposure
Decimal128 for weight/caloriesExact display; no float driftFloat — weird-looking 0.00001 errors

Production polish: integrate wearables via Apple HealthKit, Google Fit, Fitbit Web API, Garmin Connect, Strava, Oura, WHOOP, Withings, Polar — or use unified providers like Terra, Vital, Fitbit OAuth, Spike API, Rook for one integration to rule them all; nutrition databases via Edamam, Nutritionix, USDA FoodData Central; barcode scanning for meal logging via Open Food Facts; guided workouts via partnered video providers (Mux/Vimeo); meditations via partnerships or generated audio with ElevenLabs; recommendations: workout suggestions, meal ideas via Atlas Vector Search on user history; streaks and gamification via badges, leaderboards (with consent); HIPAA boundary: if the app touches medical data (Rx, conditions), enable BAA via Atlas, encrypt PHI with Queryable Encryption; data export in standard formats (FHIR, GPX, TCX); privacy: granular consent, ability to delete all data per GDPR; research data sharing only with explicit opt-in, de-identified via Tonic.ai/Gretel; analytics (engagement, retention) export to Mixpanel/Amplitude/PostHog — never include health values; safety: detect concerning patterns (sudden weight loss, low HR variability) and surface gentle nudges to consult a doctor; chat support via Intercom with mental-health resource integration.

Question 99

You are asked to design a database for a smart city platform with sensors, data streams, and analytics. How would you structure the collections?

Situation: a smart-city platform ingests data from thousands of sensors (traffic counters, air quality monitors, parking occupancy, noise meters, water flow) deployed across a city. Streams arrive at high rates (millions of readings per hour); analytics dashboards show real-time and historical views; alerts trigger on threshold breaches; researchers run ad-hoc queries on years of history.

Approach: three core collections. sensors describes each deployed device with location, type, calibration. A time-series readings collection holds every measurement with TTL for hot data and Online Archive tiering older data to cheap storage. alerts is rule-driven and triggered via change streams. Aggregation rollups (per-minute, per-hour, per-day) populate aggregates for fast dashboards.

// sensors — metadata, slow-changing
db.sensors.insertOne({
  _id: "sens_traffic_001",
  type: "traffic_counter",     // traffic_counter | air_quality | parking | noise | water_flow
  location: {
    type: "Point",
    coordinates: [-73.985, 40.749]
  },
  district: "midtown",
  installed_at: ISODate("2024-06-15"),
  vendor: "FlowMetrics",
  model: "FM-3000",
  calibration: { last_at: ISODate("2025-12-01"), offset: 0.0, scale: 1.0 },
  status: "online"             // online | offline | maintenance | retired
})

// readings — high-volume time-series
db.createCollection("readings", {
  timeseries: { timeField: "ts", metaField: "meta", granularity: "seconds" },
  expireAfterSeconds: 30 * 86400      // hot retention 30 days
})
db.readings.insertMany([
  { ts: new Date(), meta: { sensor_id: "sens_traffic_001", type: "traffic_counter", district: "midtown" }, value: 142, unit: "veh/min" },
  { ts: new Date(), meta: { sensor_id: "sens_air_042",     type: "air_quality",     district: "midtown" }, pm25: 12.4, pm10: 28.1, no2: 18.2 }
])

// aggregates — minute / hour / day rollups via $merge
db.readings.aggregate([
  { $match: { ts: { $gte: oneMinuteAgo } } },
  { $group: {
      _id: {
        sensor_id: "$meta.sensor_id",
        minute:    { $dateTrunc: { date: "$ts", unit: "minute" } }
      },
      avg_value:  { $avg: "$value" },
      max_value:  { $max: "$value" },
      sample_count: { $sum: 1 }
  }},
  { $merge: { into: "aggregates_minute", on: "_id", whenMatched: "replace", whenNotMatched: "insert" } }
])

// alerts — rule-driven
db.alert_rules.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  sensor_type: "air_quality",
  field: "pm25",
  op: "gt",
  threshold: 35,
  duration_min: 15,
  notify: ["dept-health@city.gov"],
  active: true
})

db.alerts.insertOne({
  rule_id: ObjectId("..."),
  sensor_id: "sens_air_042",
  triggered_at: new Date(),
  value: 47.3,
  acknowledged: false
})

// 2dsphere for “sensors near a location” queries
db.sensors.createIndex({ location: "2dsphere" })

// Geo + threshold dashboard query
db.readings.aggregate([
  { $match: { ts: { $gte: oneHourAgo }, "meta.type": "air_quality" } },
  { $group: { _id: "$meta.sensor_id", avg_pm25: { $avg: "$pm25" } } },
  { $match: { avg_pm25: { $gt: 35 } } },
  { $lookup: { from: "sensors", localField: "_id", foreignField: "_id", as: "s" } },
  { $unwind: "$s" }
])

// Indexes
db.readings.createIndex({ "meta.sensor_id": 1, ts: -1 })   // collection-level index on TS
db.aggregates_minute.createIndex({ "_id.sensor_id": 1, "_id.minute": -1 })
db.alerts.createIndex({ acknowledged: 1, triggered_at: -1 })

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
Time-series collection10x compression, optimized append at city scaleRegular collection — cost prohibitive
TTL + Online ArchiveHot 30 days in cluster, cold years in S3One-tier — expensive or short retention
Materialized rollups via $mergeDashboards in milliseconds, not minutesLive aggregation — impossible at this volume
Sensors metadata separateSlow-changing; $lookup when neededEmbed metadata per reading — massive duplication
Alert rules in DBOperators tweak without deploysHard-coded — deploy for every threshold

Production polish: at city scale, the ingestion path matters more than the database — sensors push to MQTT brokers (EMQX/HiveMQ/AWS IoT Core) that batch into Kafka; consumers write to MongoDB time-series + downstream warehouse; specialized OLAP for ad-hoc analytical queries: ClickHouse, Apache Druid, InfluxDB, TimescaleDB, QuestDB — MongoDB time-series is great for moderate scale; for billions of points/day consider hybrid; streaming analytics via Apache Flink/Materialize/Apache Pinot for windowed aggregations and complex event processing; geospatial visualization via Mapbox GL JS/deck.gl/Kepler.gl with H3 hex binning; open data publishing via CKAN/Socrata — cities legally open historical data; data quality monitoring via Great Expectations/Soda/Monte Carlo — broken sensors send broken data; edge processing on the sensor (anomaly detection, downsampling) reduces upstream load; ML: traffic prediction (Prophet/NeuralProphet), anomaly detection (Anomalo/Datadog Watchdog); citizen-facing dashboards via Grafana/Apache Superset/Metabase; compliance: city data may be FOIA-able — build retention and access policies accordingly; privacy: aggregate before publish (no per-vehicle tracks).

Question 100

How would you handle real-time data ingestion and processing in MongoDB for a high-frequency trading application?

Situation: a high-frequency trading (HFT) platform ingests market data ticks at extreme rates (hundreds of thousands per second per symbol), runs algorithmic strategies with bounded latency (microseconds to milliseconds), places orders, and reconciles positions. Storage requirements span hot in-memory data, recent durable history, and archival tick data for backtesting.

Approach: MongoDB is not the hot path in HFT. The order-management critical loop runs in C++/Rust on co-located servers using shared memory and kernel-bypass networking (DPDK, Solarflare). MongoDB’s role is persistence and analytics: store strategy parameters, position states, post-trade transaction logs, and historical tick data for backtesting and TCA (transaction cost analysis). The hot ingestion happens in Kafka/Aeron/Chronicle Queue; persistence to MongoDB happens asynchronously.

// strategies — parameters and lifecycle
db.strategies.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  name: "vwap_reversion_v3",
  symbols: ["AAPL", "MSFT", "GOOG"],
  params: {
    lookback_seconds: 300,
    z_threshold: 2.5,
    max_position: NumberDecimal("100000"),
    risk_limit: NumberDecimal("50000")
  },
  enabled: true,
  pnl_today: NumberDecimal("12482.42"),
  updated_at: new Date()
})

// positions — current state, single doc per (strategy, symbol)
db.positions.insertOne({
  _id: { strategy_id: ObjectId("..."), symbol: "AAPL" },
  qty: NumberDecimal("250"),
  avg_price: NumberDecimal("182.42"),
  realized_pnl: NumberDecimal("1284.32"),
  unrealized_pnl: NumberDecimal("234.18"),
  updated_at: new Date()
})

// orders — append-only audit
db.orders.insertOne({
  _id: ObjectId(),
  strategy_id: ObjectId("..."),
  symbol: "AAPL",
  side: "buy",
  qty: NumberDecimal("100"),
  type: "limit",
  limit_price: NumberDecimal("182.40"),
  venue: "NASDAQ",
  client_order_id: "co_01HRG...",
  exchange_order_id: "exch_xyz",
  status: "filled",          // pending | sent | acked | partial | filled | canceled | rejected
  fill_qty: NumberDecimal("100"),
  fill_price: NumberDecimal("182.41"),
  sent_at:   ISODate("2026-04-29T13:30:42.193456Z"),
  acked_at:  ISODate("2026-04-29T13:30:42.193512Z"),
  filled_at: ISODate("2026-04-29T13:30:42.194021Z"),
  latency_us: 565
})

// market data ticks — time-series, very high write rate
db.createCollection("ticks", {
  timeseries: { timeField: "ts", metaField: "meta", granularity: "seconds" },
  expireAfterSeconds: 7 * 86400        // 7-day hot; archive elsewhere
})
db.ticks.insertOne({
  ts: new Date(),
  meta: { symbol: "AAPL", venue: "NASDAQ", type: "trade" },
  price: NumberDecimal("182.42"),
  qty: 100,
  cond: "@"     // tape conditions
})

// Indexes
db.orders.createIndex({ strategy_id: 1, sent_at: -1 })
db.orders.createIndex({ symbol: 1, sent_at: -1 })
db.orders.createIndex({ client_order_id: 1 }, { unique: true })

// Periodic position reconciliation (every minute, post-trade)
db.orders.aggregate([
  { $match: { status: "filled", filled_at: { $gte: oneMinuteAgo } } },
  { $group: {
      _id: { strategy_id: "$strategy_id", symbol: "$symbol" },
      delta_qty: { $sum: { $multiply: ["$fill_qty", { $cond: [{ $eq: ["$side", "buy"] }, 1, -1] }] } }
  }},
  // ... pipe to position update
])

Trade-offs:

DecisionWhyAlternative
MongoDB off the hot pathHFT needs μs; MongoDB is msTry to use Mongo on the hot path — misses every fill window
Time-series for ticks10x compression, append-optimized for backtesting dataRegular collection — storage explodes
Append-only ordersRegulatory audit; reconstruction; SEC/FINRA recordsMutable orders — can’t prove what happened
Decimal128 for price/qtyExact; no float-rounding affecting fillsFloat — off-by-cent errors that compound
Position state separate from ledgerFast reads of current exposureRecompute from orders — slow under load

Production polish: the real critical path uses kernel-bypass networking (DPDK, Solarflare/Onload, Mellanox VMA), FPGA-accelerated parsers (Algo-Logic, Enyx), shared-memory IPC (Aeron, Chronicle Queue, nanomsg), and lock-free data structures — written in C++ or Rust; co-location at exchanges (NY4, LD4, TY3) is non-negotiable for serious HFT; FIX protocol via QuickFIX or vendor APIs (ITCH, OUCH); MongoDB’s role is persistence and analytics — tick storage for backtesting, post-trade transaction cost analysis, strategy parameter tuning, regulatory reporting; archival tick data in Parquet on S3 with DuckDB/Polars/ClickHouse/kdb+; backtesting via QuantConnect/Zipline/Backtrader/vectorbt; real-time risk: pre-trade risk checks (max position, max loss, fat-finger limits) implemented in the order gateway in microseconds, not in the database; compliance: SEC Rule 17a-3/17a-4 retention (6 years), CAT (Consolidated Audit Trail) reporting, MAR/MAD market abuse surveillance; monitoring: per-strategy P&L every second, latency histograms, queue depths via Datadog/Grafana; kill switches in hardware and software for emergency stops; for retail-grade “real-time” trading apps (Robinhood-style, ms latency tolerable), MongoDB can sit in the path with care — cache positions in Redis, persist async; integrate execution via Alpaca/Tradier/Interactive Brokers/Lime Brokerage.
